Chapter 1
1 Introduction
In recent years, societies and individuals alike have been forced to navigate an increasingly complex web of external shocks. These disruptions—ranging from regional conflicts and the lingering effects of the global pandemic to rapid technological change—have been compounded by the intensifying consequences of climate change. These developments are reshaping not only how communities adapt to risk, but also how financial decision-makers grapple with a long-standing tension: how to create value in the traditional financial sense—returns, shareholder wealth, measurable performance—while also incorporating values such as climate responsibility, social equity, and long-term sustainability. That tension, once peripheral, now lies at the center of global financial debates.
This evolving dynamic is perhaps best illustrated by the actions of BlackRock, the world’s largest asset manager. As a bellwether for the financial industry, BlackRock’s strategies and public statements often signal broader trends and influence regulatory responses. The firm’s evolution over the past decade mirrors broader shifts in sustainable finance—its rise, its politicization, and its current recalibration.
In 2018, CEO Larry Fink’s annual letter to corporate leaders marked a watershed moment, urging companies to articulate a “sense of purpose” and demonstrate their contributions to society as a prerequisite for long-term profitability. This argument signaled a departure from shareholder primacy and encouraged companies to consider the broader societal implications of their operations.
By 2020, BlackRock moved from rhetoric to strategy, incorporating ESG (Environmental, Social, and Governance) factors across all active portfolios, distancing itself from thermal coal, and positioning climate risk explicitly as investment risk. The surge in ESG-labelled funds and the firm’s increased support for climate- and social-related shareholder proposals cemented its role as a global leader in sustainable investing.
The landscape shifted between 2022 and 2023. BlackRock’s ESG funds experienced significant outflows, driven by underperformance and a surge of anti-ESG sentiment. In response, Larry Fink abandon the term “ESG,” emphasizing instead BlackRock’s fiduciary duty to clients and its role in providing comprehensive risk information, including climate-related risks. The firm maintained its stance that climate risk remains investment risk but sought to balance its approach by continuing investments in fossil fuel companies while encouraging energy transition strategies.  This nuanced position reflects the complexities of navigating stakeholder expectations, regulatory pressures, and market realities.
The evolution of BlackRock’s messaging continued in subsequent years. In the 2024 Chairman’s Letter, Fink replaced “ESG” with terms such as stakeholder capitalism and energy pragmatism, highlighting the need to balance the transition to renewable energy with concerns about energy security.  
By 2025, references to sustainability and net zero had all but disappeared from official communications, with the focus shifting toward democratizing access to private markets and infrastructure investments.
However, these rhetorical changes were not matched by withdrawals from climate-aligned investments. A closer examination of BlackRock’s recent acquisitions—such as Global Infrastructure Partners (GIP), which prioritizes decarbonization, and Preqin, a provider of private market data —suggests that the firm remains committed to climate resilience and transition investing, albeit through alternative channels. BlackRock remains focused on climate change and transition finance, even if it has distanced itself from the politicized vocabulary surrounding ESG.
This divergence between public messaging and capital allocation raises an essential question that extends far beyond one firm: Should financial institutions prioritize value creation for clients, or do they bear a responsibility to champion broader societal values? And if climate risk is indeed investment risk, is the separation between profit and purpose even meaningful? As climate tipping points draw closer and sustainability debates intensify, the answers to these questions will shape the trajectory of climate finance and the future of sustainable investing.
In this book, we seek to trace the development of sustainable finance, examining its impact on corporate governance, investment management, and the emergence of new trends such as artificial intelligence. Chapter 1 examines the intellectual foundations of sustainable finance and its evolving role at the intersection of markets, institutions, and environmental constraints. By understanding how these concepts have evolved—and why they have become so contested—we can better assess how sustainable finance might guide societies toward a more resilient and equitable future.
1.1 What is sustainable finance?
The European Commission (EC) defines sustainable finance as follows:
‘’Sustainable finance refers to the process of taking environmental, social and governance (ESG) considerations into account when making investment decisions in the financial sector, leading to more long-term investments in sustainable economic activities and projects’’.
Building on the EC’s definition of sustainable finance, it introduces the core ESG dimensions. Environmental considerations encompass mitigation and adaptation to climate change, biodiversity preservation, pollution prevention, resource efficiency, and circularity. Social factors span human and animal rights, equity and inclusion, labor relations, and consumer protection. Governance addresses how public and private organizations are structured and run—board composition and oversight, incentive design and executive pay, internal controls, and stakeholder engagement.


Table 1.1: Taxonomy of individual ESG factors

The Concept surrounding ESG often overlaps with adjacent terms—climate finance, green finance, social finance, impact finance, and sustainable finance—yet these labels are not fully interchangeable. Clarifying scope matters for policy and strategies design. As the ICMA (2020) framework illustrates, sustainable finance is the broadest umbrella, integrating ESG investing (also referred to as responsible investing), green finance, social finance, and climate finance. Climate finance is understood as a subset of green finance focused specifically on mitigation and adaptation of climate change; green finance covers the full spectrum of environmental objectives, from emissions reduction to pollution control and biodiversity conservation. Impact finance cuts across green and social finance where capital is deployed with intentionality to deliver demonstrable outcomes alongside financial returns. 
Additional terms refine the picture. Carbon finance concentrates on pricing and transacting emissions reductions and removals—through carbon markets, credits, and internal carbon pricing—linking project-level abatement to portfolio-level decarbonization. As highlighted by IFC (2022), blue finance, an emerging branch of climate finance, targets the ocean economy: water security, sustainable fisheries, marine conversation and more. Many countries rely on blue economy to buffer mounting climate risks—intensifying storms, sea-level rise, acidification—and to support livelihoods. Keeping marine ecosystems healthy is not just good for the environment, but it is directly linked to alleviating poverty and strengthening climate resilience. 
Figure 1.1: Interaction between key high-level definitions

Source: ICMA.





Table 1.2: Comparison for key definitions

Source
Definition
Carbon finance
World Bank Group
Carbon finance (CF) is a generic term used for the revenue streams that can be generated by low-carbon projects and activities from sale of their global greenhouse gas (GHG) emission reductions by sources, or GHG emission removals by sinks, or from trading in carbon credits.
Blue finance
IFC
Blue finance offers tremendous opportunities to help safeguard our access to clean water, protect underwater environments, and invest in a sustainable water economy.
Climate finance
United Nations
Climate finance refers to local, national or transnational financing—drawn from public, private and alternative sources of financing—that seeks to support mitigation and adaptation actions that will address climate change.
Green finance
World Economic Forum
Green finance is any structured financial activity – a product or service – that’s been created to ensure a better environmental outcome. It includes an array of loans, debt mechanisms and investments that are used to encourage the development of green projects or minimize the impact on the climate of more regular projects.
Social finance
ICMA
Social Finance is financing that supports actions mitigating or addressing a specific social issue and/or seeking to achieve positive social outcomes especially but not exclusively for a target population(s).
Impact finance
European Investment Bank
Impact finance is all about taking on risk to catalyze financial flows and to tackle social and environmental challenges such as poverty, social exclusion and climate change.
Responsible or ESG Investing
Principles for Responsible Investment
Responsible Investing, also known as ESG Investing, refers to strategies and practices that incorporate material environmental, social and governance (ESG) factors in investment decisions and active ownership with a view to minimize risks and maximize returns. It can be pursued by all investors as part of their fiduciary duty on the basis that ESG factors are considered as having a material impact on returns.
Sustainable finance
European Commission (EC)
Sustainable finance refers to the process of taking environmental, social and governance (ESG) considerations into account when making investment decisions in the financial sector, leading to more long-term investments in sustainable economic activities and projects.
Financing gaps and priorities
A central motivation for the emergence of sustainable finance is the persistent and widening gap between global development ambitions and the financial resources available to achieve them. Nowhere is this gap more evident than in the context of the United Nations Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs), which set out a comprehensive vision for social, economic, and environmental progress by 2030. Despite broad international support, the world faces a significant shortfall in the capital required to realize these goals.
Recent United Nations estimates underscore the magnitude of this challenge: by 2030, the global SDG financing gap could reach USD 6.4 trillion. This shortfall is particularly acute in developing and emerging economies, where fiscal constraints and limited access to private capital hinder progress on critical development priorities. The gap is not merely a matter of insufficient funds; it also reflects deeper structural issues within the global financial system, including risk aversion among private investors and inefficiencies in channeling capital to high-impact sectors. 
Figure 1.2: Projected SDGs financing gap 

Source: OECD, https://www.oecd.org/en/publications/global-outlook-on-financing-for-sustainable-development-2025_753d5368-en.html.
A closer examination of the SDG financing gap reveals that climate-related investments account for more than half of the total shortfall. The urgency of the climate crisis has made the need for capital in areas such as clean energy, climate adaptation, and resilience especially pressing. For example, the United Nations Environment Programme estimates that an additional USD 250 billion per year will be needed by 2030 to strengthen climate adaptation and resilience, with major cost drivers including flood protection, infrastructure upgrades, and coastal defenses—particularly in vulnerable regions like East Asia, the Pacific, and Latin America.
Figure 1.3: Projected Climate financing needs 

Source: OECD, https://www.oecd.org/en/publications/global-outlook-on-financing-for-sustainable-development-2025_753d5368-en.html.
The financing challenge has been further exacerbated by a series of external shocks in recent years. The COVID-19 pandemic, for example, not only strained public health systems but also diverted fiscal resources away from long-term sustainability initiatives. Simultaneous crises in food, fuel, and finance have disproportionately affected developing economies and least developed countries (LDCs), further narrowing the fiscal space available for green and social investments. Rising inflation and tighter global financial conditions have pushed up borrowing costs, making it even more difficult for vulnerable countries to access the capital needed for sustainable development. These overlapping crises expose the fragility of the current financial architecture and highlight the limitations of relying solely on market mechanisms to drive progress toward the SDGs.
Given these realities, aligning global finance with the SDGs has become an urgent imperative. The path forward requires more than incremental adjustments; it calls for a fundamental rethinking of how capital is mobilized and allocated. A hybrid financing framework—one that strategically blends public and private capital, domestic and international resources, and concessional and commercial finance—will be essential. Moreover, financial instruments and mechanisms must be tailored to the unique institutional contexts and development stages of individual countries. Without such targeted and coordinated efforts, the credibility of the global commitment to sustainable development and climate resilience is at risk.
How, then, can the world bridge this daunting financing gap? What innovative approaches might unlock new sources of capital and ensure that resources are directed where they are needed most? These questions are central to the ongoing debate about the future of sustainable finance and the prospects for achieving the SDGs by 2030.
The global evolution of sustainable finance
Discussions about sustainable finance are inseparable from the framework of Environmental, Social, and Governance (ESG) principles. ESG has shaped the way financial systems align with sustainability goals, influencing instruments such as green bonds, climate finance, and impact investing. However, in recent years, ESG has become a subject of debate—celebrated by some as a foundation for responsible capitalism yet criticized by others as superficial or politicized. To understand these debates, it is helpful to review the evolution of sustainable finance and the reasons behind its growing controversy.
Sustainable finance evolution 
The roots of sustainable finance can be traced back to the 1972 United Nations Conference on the Human Environment in Stockholm, which first recognized the environment as a global policy issue. This led to the creation of the United Nations Environment Programme (UNEP), a key institution for environmental governance. The 1992 Earth Summit in Rio de Janeiro expanded this vision, emphasizing the interconnectedness of social, economic, and environmental systems. Around this time, the UNEP Finance Initiative (UNEP FI) was launched, marking the beginning of efforts to integrate sustainability into the financial sector.
The late 1990s and early 2000s saw further progress. The 1997 Kyoto Protocol formalized international commitments to reduce greenhouse gas emissions, embedding environmental concerns within the ESG agenda. Meanwhile, corporate scandals such as Enron’s collapse highlighted the importance of governance and transparency. In 2004, the United Nations explicitly called for the integration of ESG factors into investment decisions, leading to the launch of the Principles for Responsible Investment (PRI) in 2006. This period marked the transition from ethical investing to a more systematic approach to ESG integration.
Major environmental disasters, such as the 2010 BP Deepwater Horizon oil spill, reinforced the need for measurable standards and accountability. The creation of the Sustainability Accounting Standards Board (SASB) in 2011 helped translate ESG issues into financial terms that investors could use. By 2015, ESG had become mainstream, with the adoption of the Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs), the Paris Agreement, and the establishment of the Task Force on Climate-related Financial Disclosures (TCFD). 
In the 2020s, ESG considerations became central to financial regulation and corporate strategy across major economies. Disclosure frameworks such as GRI, TCFD, CSRD, and ISSB began shaping policy, and companies increasingly addressed issues beyond the environment, including labor standards and diversity. ESG matured into a multidimensional approach to value creation. These developments reflected a shift from shareholder primacy to stakeholder responsibility, embedding sustainability into the core of business legitimacy.
Recent backlash and controversy
Despite its rapid adoption, ESG has faced growing criticism and skepticism. Since 2023, the field has experienced policy reversals, capital outflows, and increased scrutiny. In the United States, ESG has become a politically charged topic, with some critics arguing that it prioritizes social agendas over financial returns and distorts market mechanisms. In Europe, the energy crisis following the Ukraine conflict reignited debates about the exclusion of traditional energy from ESG portfolios, with fiscal pressures leading to a more pragmatic approach to climate policy.
These tensions have had tangible effects on capital flows. For example, global sustainable funds saw significant net outflows in the third quarter of 2025, with notable redemptions from UK-based BlackRock funds. Even after accounting for these large movements, sustainable investments overall experienced a reversal from previous inflows. This suggests that skepticism about ESG is spreading beyond the United States to other regions.
Figure 1.4: Quarterly Global Sustainable Fund Flows (USD Billion)
Source: Morningstar Direct, https://www.morningstar.com/business/insights/research/global-esg-flows.
At the same time, some companies, such as Accenture, Google, and Meta, have scaled back or redefined their sustainability commitments. While others have reaffirmed their goals under new labels such as “responsible business” or “sustainable growth.”  The language may change, but the underlying debate about the balance between purpose and profit persists.
Why is ESG controversial?
The controversy surrounding ESG stems from several factors. First, the reason, perhaps, lies not in ideology but in finance itself. Early outperformance was often driven by favorable market trends rather than inherent advantages. By 2022, however, the surge in fossil fuel prices after the Ukraine conflict benefited sectors typically excluded from ESG portfolios, while technology stocks—often overweight in ESG funds—underperformed.
Policy debates have also played a role. In the US, some lawmakers argue that excluding fossil fuels threatens energy security and jobs, while in Europe, there is pushback against regulations seen as too restrictive. ESG-focused financial institutions and climate advocates have recently faced prominent antitrust allegations. Critics claim that these groups have coordinated efforts—such as shareholder activism and selective capital allocation—to pressure companies into reducing activities that generate high emissions. Some argue that such collective actions may limit competition or unfairly influence business decisions.
Additionally, ESG has sometimes become more of a marketing tool or compliance exercise than a genuine driver of long-term value. The complexity and cost of meeting diverse stakeholder expectations have led to overpromised targets and, in some cases, accusations of “greenwashing”.
Despite these challenges, it would be premature to declare the failure of sustainable finance. Impact investing in areas such as renewable energy, sustainable agriculture, and healthcare continues to grow. The current backlash may signal a period of adjustment, where the focus shifts from superficial compliance to more meaningful, long-term strategies for resilience and innovation.
The history of sustainable finance: timeline
The evolution of sustainable finance reflects a journey from early environmental awareness to a complex, multidimensional approach to value creation. While ESG principles have become central to global finance, recent backlash highlights unresolved tensions between financial returns, regulatory expectations, and broader societal goals. Maintaining an objective perspective is essential as the field continues to adapt and redefine its purpose.


Figure 1.5: The timeline of sustainable finance

What is next? 
In today’s climate change and shifting policies, organizations and governments face a crucial decision: should they scale back ESG initiatives or reaffirm their commitment to sustainability? This dilemma reflects a broader tension between immediate economic pressures and the long-term risks that sustainability frameworks are designed to address.
Climate change: a systemic risk
Despite ongoing debates about ESG, the reality is that climate risk is intensifying. For many, climate change may still seem distant or abstract, especially for those who have not directly experienced severe floods, heatwaves, or droughts. Historically, climate was viewed as a slow-moving backdrop to human activity, but the industrial era changed this dynamic. Human-driven emissions from fossil fuels have accelerated global warming at an unprecedented rate.
As global temperatures rise, the risk of crossing critical climate thresholds increases.   For example, tropical coral reefs have already surpassed their thermal tolerance, leading to widespread bleaching and ecosystem loss. This is not just an environmental issue; coral reefs support fisheries, coastal protection, and livelihoods for millions. The collapse of one system can trigger cascading effects in others, such as ice sheet destabilization, amazon rainforest dieback, and weaker Atlantic Meridional Overturning Circulation (AMOC). Once these limits are breached, rapid structural transformations within the Earth system become more probable, with consequences that are difficult to predict and, in many cases, impossible to reverse. These interconnected risks highlight that climate change is not a localized problem—it is a global, systemic challenge that affects economies and societies everywhere. 


Figure 1.6: Parts of the Earth system identified as featuring tipping points

Source: Global Tipping Points Report 2025, https://global-tipping-points.org/.
The challenges of sustainable finance extend beyond climate. Social issues—including labor rights, gender equality, education, healthcare, and ethical supply chains—remain unresolved, emphasizing that investment in people is central to sustainable progress. The global financing gap for sustainability remains significant, especially in developing countries. Bridging this gap requires effective integration of public and private capital, as well as financial instruments that balance short-term costs with long-term benefits.
Trends in sustainable finance
While investor enthusiasm has fluctuated due to policy uncertainty and uneven returns, sustainable investing continues to grow. As of September 2025, global sustainable fund assets reached approximately USD 3.7 trillion, with notable growth in Canada, Australia, New Zealand, and parts of Asia. Sustainable investments play a key role in mitigating risk and generating long-term value, especially in sectors like renewable energy, healthcare, and education. 


Figure 1.7: Quarterly Global Sustainable Fund Assets (USD Billion)

Source: Morningstar Direct, https://www.morningstar.com/business/insights/research/global-esg-flows.
For corporate leaders, the current environment calls for recalibration rather than retreat. Viewing ESG as merely a branding exercise or compliance burden misses its true purpose: to enhance resilience, innovation, and trust. Companies that align sustainability goals with measurable outcomes—such as energy efficiency or supply chain transparency—are already seeing improved performance and investor confidence. Regulators are also strengthening standards for sustainability reporting, pushing for greater transparency and consistency in financial markets.
The rise of artificial intelligence is further shaping the field. AI tools improve data quality, reduce reporting costs, and help link ESG practices to financial outcomes. They also enable real-time monitoring of ESG risks and support regulators in detecting greenwashing. However, the adoption of AI brings new challenges, such as concerns about job displacement, security, and governance. Ensuring that technology serves broader sustainable development goals will be essential.
Taken together, these risks and trends reaffirm the importance of sustainable finance. It is not just about balancing financial returns with ethical values; it is a necessary mechanism for closing the global financing gap and directing resources toward a low-carbon, equitable economy. As risks evolve, refining sustainable finance practices and understanding their impacts will be crucial for building resilience and supporting long-term development.
The ESG ecosystem
To understand how ESG principles are applied in practice, it is important to look at the main sectors and organizations involved in the ESG landscape. 
Global foundations and scientific leadership 
The United Nations has played a central role in shaping the scientific and policy foundations of environmental and climate action. Since its establishment in 1972, the United Nations Environment Programme (UNEP) has brought together governments, civil society, and the private sector to address major environmental challenges. In 1988, UNEP and the World Meteorological Organization created the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC), which provides regular scientific assessments on climate change. The IPCC’s reports are widely recognized as the most comprehensive sources of climate science and have directly influenced international policy, including the annual Conferences of the Parties (COPs) under the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (UNFCCC). These meetings, such as COP30 in Brazil, are where governments review progress toward global climate goals set in the Paris Agreement.
Industry initiatives and investor collaboration
Beyond global policy, industry-led initiatives have become important drivers of ESG practice. The Principles for Responsible Investment (PRI), launched in 2006, encourages investors to integrate ESG factors into their decisions. With over 5,000 signatories representing $139.6 trillion in assets under management, PRI demonstrates the scale of investor commitment. Other collaborative efforts include Climate Action 100+, which brings together more than 600 investors to engage with climate actions, and the Net Zero Asset Owner Alliance (NZAOA), whose members set clear targets for reducing portfolio emissions. These alliances show how financial institutions are working together to address climate risks and promote sustainability.
Additional initiatives address the broader pillars of ESG, such as the Asia Investor Group on Climate Change (AIGCC), Platform Living Wage Financials (PLWF), and the International Corporate Governance Network (ICGN). These groups focus on issues ranging from climate action to fair wages and corporate governance.
ESG data providers and ratings
Reliable data is essential for evaluating sustainability strategies. The ESG ratings industry is led by firms such as ISS, MSCI, LSEG, S&P Global, Sustainalytics, and Moody’s. These organizations assess companies on ESG criteria, helping investors make informed decisions. New entrants (e.g., Covalence, OWL ESG, and Truvalue Labs) are using advanced technologies like artificial intelligence, big data, and natural language processing to improve analysis. Despite concerns about the consistency and transparency of ESG scores, ESG ratings have helped raise standards and improve transparency across industries.
Investors, asset managers, and financial products
Investors are at the heart of the ESG ecosystem, using sustainability information to guide investment and risk management. Asset owners include pension funds, sovereign wealth funds, insurance companies, endowments, family offices, and retail investors. Asset managers oversee funds for these investors, including mutual funds, hedge funds, private equity, and infrastructure funds. On the financing side, banks and issuers structure ESG products such as green bonds and assess ESG risks when issuing debt.
As ESG has become more prominent, concerns about greenwashing and inconsistent practices have grown. Regulators and supervisors now play a key role in shaping the ESG landscape. International bodies like the Basel Committee on Banking Supervision (BCBS), the International Association of Insurance Supervisors (IAIS), the International Organization of Securities Commissions (IOSCO), and the Financial Stability Board (FSB) have recognized sustainable risk alongside traditional financial risks. Governments are also active: the European Union leads with comprehensive ESG regulations, the UK has integrated ESG into corporate governance, and exchanges like the Hong Kong Stock Exchange require ESG reporting for listed companies.

Reporting standards and disclosure
Transparency is supported by global reporting frameworks. The International Sustainability Standards Board (ISSB) has introduced standards for sustainability and climate-related disclosures (IFRS S1 and IFRS S2), which are being adopted worldwide. These frameworks help distinguish between general sustainability reporting and climate-specific reporting, providing clarity for both issuers and investors.

Purpose and structure of this book
This chapter offers a global overview of sustainable finance, introducing its core concepts, historical development, key players, and regulatory frameworks. We also address the limitations and controversies that have emerged, especially in real-world applications.
The following chapters build on this foundation:
Chapter 2 examines the effects and drivers of corporate sustainable governance, exploring how companies implement and benefit from sustainability practices.
Chapter 3 focuses on sustainable investing strategies and their impact on financial performance.
Chapter 4 discusses current climate challenges and their broader implications for financial systems.
Chapter 5 investigates the growing influence of artificial intelligence, considering both its advantages for sustainable strategies and the potential risks it poses to sustainability goals.
Chapter 6 concludes with a summary of key themes, highlighting ongoing challenges and emerging opportunities in the field.
Throughout the book, we present recent research and practical cases to connect theory with real-world practice. Our aim is to help students and readers understand both the historical context and current trends in sustainable finance, encouraging them to develop their own perspectives on future directions.
We recommend that readers familiarize themselves with the overall structure and basic theories of sustainable finance and actively engage with case studies and discussion questions. As this is a rapidly evolving field, maintaining a dynamic and practical outlook is essential. Exploring recent high-quality research can help identify gaps and opportunities for further study.
In a world facing increasing uncertainty, climate risks, and technological change, reaffirming sustainable finance is both timely and meaningful. We hope this book provides valuable insights and prompts reflection on how sustainable finance relates to your academic interests or professional goals. Our purpose is to guide you in understanding, questioning, and applying sustainable finance in practice. 

Chapter 2
Corporate Finance and Sustainability 
Measure ESG
Generally speaking, there are four main components in sustainability (ESG) ecosystem. As we can see in Figure 2.1, Companies are positioned at the upstream end of the ESG data flow, serving as the primary source of most sustainability information that investors focus on—such as carbon emissions, employee diversity, and anti-bribery measures. They release this information either voluntarily or to meet mandatory regulatory demands in their regions, following established sustainability standards or frameworks, thus producing ESG raw data. The raw data is collected by data aggregators like Bloomberg and LSEG, which calculate commonly used ESG metrics (e.g., carbon emission intensity or green revenue percentage), and display them consistently on their platforms. Meanwhile, ESG rating agencies like MSCI and Sustainalytics also leverage this raw data alongside additional sources (e.g., news releases and expert interviews). Using their own rating methodologies, these agencies conduct thorough, systematic assessments of companies’ sustainability performance across sectors, providing standardized ratings to enable cross-company comparisons. The end users of the ESG data flow are banks or investors, who integrate these ESG metrics or ratings into their investment or risk management processes. For example, they might adjust loan interest rates based on a company’s ESG rating or use the carbon intensity of invested companies to shape engagement strategies or divestment decisions. 
According to the above illustration, it is clear that calculating sustainability metrics from ESG raw data and directly referencing ESG ratings represent unstructured and structured approaches, respectively, to evaluating a company’s ESG performance. The first part of this section will closely examine the leading sustainability disclosure standards or frameworks in use today, offering a clear view of how companies report their ESG raw data and highlighting the types of sustainability information that regulators, investors, and other stakeholders prioritize. The second part will focus on introducing key ESG rating agencies and explaining the core methodologies analysts employ to rate companies. 
Figure 2.1: McKinsey-ESG disclosure

Source: McKinsey
Sustainability disclosure standards and frameworks
Sustainability standards or frameworks guide companies in disclosing ESG information to investors and a broader range of stakeholders, enabling businesses to communicate their impact. The sustainability reports generated through these standards or frameworks serve as a vital resource for stakeholders seeking to understand a company’s ESG and climate policies. Over the past two decades, numerous organizations have established various types of sustainability disclosure standards or frameworks to regulate corporate ESG reporting. Each differs in its scope and depth of sustainability topics, materiality assessment requirements, and intended audience. The table below identifies widely used international sustainability standards or frameworks and provides a comparative analysis to clearly illustrate their similarities and differences for readers.

Before delving into the upcoming content, it’s worth distinguishing two key sets of concepts from the table above: 1. financial materiality versus impact materiality; 2. framework versus standard.
Financial materiality vs impact materiality
Financial Materiality refers to sustainability issues that significantly affect a company’s financial condition and operational performance. These issues—such as climate risks, social reputation, or executive bribery—can influence financial outcomes like cash flows, profitability, or asset values. For example, a company might face higher costs due to environmental regulations or supply chain disruptions from climate events. The focus here is on how these factors impact the company’s fundamentals and long-term value, providing critical information for investors to make informed decisions.
Impact Materiality, in contrast, focuses on the effects of a company’s activities on the economy, environment, and society. This includes aspects like greenhouse gas emissions, water usage, waste management, labor rights, or community impacts. For instance, a business might report on its efforts to reduce its carbon footprint or improve working conditions in its supply chain. The emphasis is on the company’s responsibility to external stakeholders and its contribution to sustainable development, highlighting its broader societal and environmental footprint.
Conducting a materiality assessment to identify material topics and subsequently reporting on a company’s performance, governance, strategy, and targets related to these topics forms the core of nearly all sustainability reporting. In practice, every type of standards mentions the interplay between a company and its external environment to some extent, though each has a distinct focus. Standards or frameworks emphasizing financial materiality—such as IIRC, SASB, ISSB, CDSB, TCFD, and TNFD—primarily target investors as their audience. Those focusing on impact materiality, like GRI, cater to a broader group of stakeholders, including regulators and communities. Meanwhile, ESRS and CDP balance both perspectives, prioritizing double materiality.
Framework vs standard
ESG frameworks provide the overarching structure and principles for sustainability reporting, guiding the “what” and “why”, for example, TNFD recommends that companies report biodiversity-related risks and opportunities across four dimensions: governance, strategy, risk management, and metrics and targets. While ESG standards provide detailed specifications and requirements, outlining the “how” and “to what extent”, for instance, GRI Standard 304-2 mandates that companies disclose the species they impact and the duration of those impacts, while SASB’s Metals & Mining industry standard requires companies to report the percentage of probable reserves located in sites with endangered species habitats.
ESG frameworks are typically adopted voluntarily by companies and serve as the foundation or inspiration for developing specific standards. For example, the TCFD framework has notably shaped the structure of the ISSB’s IFRS S2. In contrast, ESG standards can become mandatory when incorporated into regulatory regimes, such as the Corporate Sustainability Reporting Directive (CSRD), which requires companies to disclose sustainability performance in accordance with the ESRS.
As the field of sustainability disclosure evolves, a broad range of stakeholders, including investors, increasingly demand a consistent and integrated global sustainability disclosure system. This will allow companies to reduce compliance burdens and generate comparable—even verifiable—sustainability reports across industries, which will enhance the quality of ESG raw data, making it more actionable for investors. As illustrated in the table above, various standards and frameworks are not in isolated competition. In fact, since the TCFD’s release in 2017, these disclosure systems have begun to reference, align with, and even merge with one another: In 2018, CDP redesigned its climate change questionnaire to align with the TCFD recommendations. In April of the same year, CDSB officially announced on its website that its reporting framework matched TCFD structure. In 2021, SASB and IIRC officially announced their merger to form the Value Reporting Foundation, and in 2022, they merged with CDSB into the IFRS Foundation. In 2023, TCFD announced its dissolution and transferred all its functions to ISSB. The IFRS S1 and S2 sustainability and climate disclosure standards released by ISSB in the same year fully adopted the 4 disclosure pillars and 11 recommended disclosures of TCFD and provided detailed guidance. Moreover, TNFD and the EU’s ESRS disclosure standard E1—Climate Change, released in 2023, have also adopted or integrated the TCFD framework.
Figure 2.2: TCFD Major Milestones 2016–2023

Source: TCFD, https://www.fsb.org/2023/10/2023-tcfd-status-report-task-force-on-climate-related-financial-disclosures/.
Figure 2.3: TCFD Major Milestones 2016–2023 (Continued)

Source: TCFD, https://www.fsb.org/2023/10/2023-tcfd-status-report-task-force-on-climate-related-financial-disclosures/.
From this, we can clearly understand that the current international sustainability disclosure system comprises the following: three widely used standards—GRI, ESRS, and ISSB; one environmental information disclosure platform—CDP; one biodiversity framework with growing influence—TNFD; and one internationally recognized carbon emissions accounting standard—the GHG Protocol. It’s also worth noting that these existing standards and frameworks are actively collaborating to improve interoperability, aiming to reduce the disclosure burden on companies and enhance comparability: 
In April 2024, ISSB will commence projects to research disclosure about risks and opportunities associated with biodiversity, ecosystems and ecosystem services, which will build from pre-existing initiatives like SASB guidance, CDSB guidance and relevant aspects of the work of TNFD.
In May 2024, the ISSB and GRI announced enhanced cooperation to achieve full interoperability, facilitating seamless sustainability reporting. An initial outcome of the collaboration will involve a methodology pilot building on the recently published GRI 101 Biodiversity Standard and the ISSB’s upcoming project on Biodiversity, Ecosystems and Ecosystem Services.
In May 2024, ISSB and ESRS jointly published guidance material on the alignment between the ISSB Standards and ESRS, which provides interoperability guidance for climate-related sustainability disclosures under ESRS and ISSB Standards.
In June 2024, GHG Protocol and IFRS foundation signed a memorandum to make sure ongoing compatibility between GHG protocol and ISSB standards.
In June 2024, CDP introduced a new questionnaire aligned with the ISSB’s IFRS S2, which has now become the foundational benchmark for CDP’s climate disclosures.
In March 2025, CDP and EFRAG published correspondence mapping between CDP question bank and European Sustainability Reporting Standard E1. According to this, ESRS E1 reporters will be better equipped to complete CDP disclosures, while CDP disclosing companies will be well prepared for ESRS E1 reporting requirements.  
Adoption of standards and frameworks
Through the analysis above, we have gained a thorough understanding of the mainstream standards and frameworks in the sustainability disclosure landscape. Next, it’s essential to explore their adoption rates, as ESG raw data can only be generated when companies—particularly publicly listed ones—implement these disclosure standards to produce sustainability reports. With over 58,000 listed companies worldwide, tracking their adoption in detail is challenging. However, the Sustainable Stock Exchange Initiative offers an alternative perspective by examining how 74 stock exchanges reference various standards or frameworks in their ESG guidance. Although many of their guidance are not mandatory, the mention of ESG standards in these contents carries significant “impact materiality." As shown in the figure below, among the 74 stock exchanges included in the analysis, 96% referenced the GRI standard in their ESG and sustainability reporting guidance documents, making it the most widely used sustainability disclosure standard. Since the SSE (Sustainable Stock Exchanges Initiative) did not include the ISSB standards released in 2023 in its statistics, and given that SASB, IIRC, TCFD, and CDSB have all been absorbed by the IFRS Foundation, we use SASB—the most frequently cited standard—as a proxy for ISSB standard mentions, which stands at 81%.
Figure 2.4: ESG disclosure guideline

Source: SSE, http://sseinitiative.org/esg-guidance-database.
Let’s examine the references to standards by Asian exchanges. According to SSE data, with the exception of the Indonesia Stock Exchange, which does not mention any sustainability standards, all Asian stock exchanges have referenced at least one standard in their ESG guidance documents. Furthermore, aside from Southeast Asia’s Vietnam, South Asia’s Bangladesh, and the Middle East’s Jordan, all other Asian stock exchanges have cited standards and frameworks now under the ISSB umbrella.

Asia’s sustainability disclosure landscape
In March 2025, PwC released The Sustainability Reporting Adoption Tracker, which monitors local regulatory or legal sustainability reporting requirements across 83 countries and regions spanning all continents via a questionnaire survey. This includes the ISSB standards, European Sustainability Reporting Standards, the SEC’s climate disclosure rules, and other region-specific obligations. For each jurisdiction, the Tracker summarizes their sustainability disclosure policy profile with high granularity, based on the following six questions: 
Which regulatory or legal sustainability reporting requirements is the territory adopting or has adopted? 
Is your territory going to mandate that entities report in accordance with the ISSB requirements?
When will the regulatory or legal sustainability reporting requirements be adopted?
Scope of companies included in the regulatory or legal sustainability reporting (e.g. assets/revenues greater than X)
Do the regulatory or legal sustainability reporting requirements apply to entities that are headquartered or listed in other territories?
What decisions have been made regarding who can provide required assurance opinion (i.e. financial statement auditor, other statutory auditor, and/or independent assurance service provider)?
The six questions mentioned above primarily target jurisdictions outside the EU, while the questionnaire for EU member states consists of four questions, structured similarly to the six questions but more focusing on ESRS, so we won't elaborate them in our main text.
It’s particularly noteworthy that this Adoption Tracker includes the implementation status of sustainability disclosure in 23 Asian countries/regions. I’ve condensed the questionnaire content into the table below. We can observe that in East Asia, China, Hong Kong SAR, Japan, South Korea, and Taiwan (China) have all either implemented or plan to implement mandatory sustainability disclosure requirements for local listed companies, with standards issued by local regulators fully aligned with the ISSB standards. A similar trend is evident in Southeast Asia, where Malaysia, Singapore, and Thailand will adopt ISSB standards and phase in mandatory disclosure over time. Although Indonesia and the Philippines currently base their mandatory disclosure rules on GRI and local SEC guidelines respectively, Indonesia’s government released PSPK1&2 based on IFRS S1&S2 in December 2024, and the Philippines announced a roadmap for adopting IFRS S1&2 around the same time. In South Asia, India has implemented mandatory disclosure through the Business Responsibility and Sustainability Reporting (BRSR) standard, which draws on GRI and SASB, with no plans to adopt ISSB standards. We can also note that nearly all jurisdictions prioritize large-cap companies for mandatory disclosure, employing a phased approach to expand the scope. 
Turning to West Asia, our earlier analysis of SSE data (see the table above) confirmed that Bahrain, Jordan, Kuwait, Oman, Qatar, Saudi Arabia, the UAE, and Turkey have all issued sustainability disclosure guidelines, with at least a reference to GRI standards, encouraging companies to report ESG information based on existing standards. However, PwC’s findings indicate that only Bahrain, Jordan, Oman, the UAE, and Turkey have plans or requirements for mandatory disclosure. The first three prioritize GRI for disclosure, while the UAE’s mandatory requirements stem primarily from the Law on the Reduction of Climate Change Effects, set to take effect in May 2025. Only Turkey has fully adopted IFRS S1&S2. Meanwhile, Iraq, Israel, Lebanon, and Palestine have no plans for sustainability disclosure.

We’ve included the total stock market capitalization data for each Asian country or region in the final column of the table. As we can see, jurisdictions that have adopted or plan to adopt ISSB standards had a combined stock market capitalization of approximately $18,385 billion USD as of early 2025, accounting for 68% of the total across all Asian countries included in the analysis, with all ISSB-adopting countries having mandatory disclosure plans. Countries currently using GRI or other standards have a combined market capitalization of $8,162.84 billion USD, representing 30%, while those without sustainability disclosure guidelines have a total market cap of $407.26 billion USD, making up just 2%. It’s abundantly clear that regulating listed companies for sustainability in Asia is an irreversible trend, with the ISSB’s IFRS S1&S2 wielding significant influence in the region.
Figure 2.5: Market share of disclosure standards

We can observe that countries in Asia vary significantly in both the progress and the standards of their sustainability disclosure requirements. This divergence raises several important questions for further discussion. For instance, will the ISSB standards eventually play a role similar to the IFRS accounting standards, serving as a “license to operate” in global capital markets? From the corporate perspective, how should firms weigh the trade-off between compliance costs and the benefits of market recognition? This dilemma may be especially pronounced for multinational corporations that must navigate and reconcile different ESG disclosure requirements across Asian jurisdictions.
Another dimension to consider is the expectations of investors. Do they primarily value “compliance-oriented disclosure”, which ensures comparability and regulatory alignment, or do they place greater emphasis on “strategic disclosure”, which reflects a firm’s unique sustainability vision and long-term competitiveness? At the regional level, one may also ask what explains the differences in regulatory approaches across Asia—whether they stem from varying levels of market development, political priorities, or institutional capacity.
This leads to a broader question: does Asia need its own region-specific ESG disclosure framework, a “regional version” of unified standards? If so, what would be the potential benefits, such as improved comparability and reduced compliance costs, and what challenges might arise, including political frictions, economic disparities, and sovereignty concerns?
Taken together, these perspectives highlight the importance of examining not only the future trajectory of ISSB adoption but also the best practices for corporate disclosure in a diverse and dynamic Asian context.
ESG ratings comparison
We have explored how companies, using sustainability standards, identify material issues that pose risks and opportunities to their business, and disclose their performance, targets, governance structures, and risk management related to these key issues. The information disclosed in corporate sustainability reports—what we refer to as ESG raw data—can be directly used by investors to assess specific sustainability risks in an unstructured approach. Alternatively, rating agencies combine this ESG raw data with other relevant information sources to comprehensively evaluate a company’s sustainability performance and assign a rating, representing a structured method for measuring ESG performance. In this section, our focus shifts to a comparative analysis of sustainability rating agencies, with examples illustrating the full process of how they assign ratings to given companies.

In short, rating agencies assess a company’s sustainability performance across the three pillars of environment, social, and governance, with each pillar encompassing specific sustainability topics of interest. Taking LSEG as an example, it focuses on emissions, innovation, and resource use under the Environmental pillar; human rights, product responsibility, workforce, and community under the Social pillar; and management, shareholders, and CSR strategy under the Governance pillar. Each topic is broken down into key issues. For instance, when evaluating the workforce topic, issues such as diversity and inclusion, career development and training, working conditions, and health and safety are prioritized. To measure these key issues, specific qualitative or quantitative metrics are established, and data is collected. Thus, the ESG composite score, pillar scores, topic scores (sometimes we also call it theme scores), key issue scores, and underlying metrics (based on data points) form the five levels at which rating agencies evaluate a company’s sustainability performance. The table below provides a detailed view on the ESG key issues covered in each topic under LSEG.
Next, it’s crucial to begin with the foundational and most vital level—metrics (and the data sources for these metrics), and then, using LSEG and MSCI as examples, examine how performance on these metrics translates into scores for key issues and subsequently for topics. By calculating a weighted average of the scores for key issues or topics, we arrive at the overall ESG score. The weights assigned to these key issues or topics vary by industry, and we will also compare the differences in weighting approaches between LSEG and MSCI.
Table 2.5: Key issues and topics in LSEG ratings
Pillars

Metrics
In February 2025, the OECD (2025) published a report titled Behind ESG Ratings: Unpacking Sustainability Metrics. This study selected eight prominent ESG rating providers based on their market share for analysis. It mapped the topic classifications of these providers, using the terminology they employed, into a single classification framework, as shown in the table below. Subsequently, the report aggregated over 2,000 metrics from the eight rating agencies and reassigned them to these unified topics classification framework. The study provides a detailed comparative analysis of the total number of metrics covered under each topic and examines the varying levels of granularity with which different rating agencies measure the same topics.
The first key finding of this study is that, on average, each rating agency uses 87 E-related metrics, 74 S-related metrics, and 83 G-related metrics. Thus, at the pillar level, there is no significant difference in overall granularity. However, the level of detail in measuring individual topics varies considerably. The figure below illustrates the distribution of over 2,000 metrics across 23 ESG topics.

Figure 2.7: ESG metric coverage by ESG pillars and topics


Source: OECD
It reveals that Corporate Governance, Business Ethics, Environmental Management, GHG Emissions, and Pollution & Waste are the top five topics with the highest number of metrics, while Biodiversity & Land Use, Business Resilience, Community Relations, Taxation, and Competition are the five topics with the fewest metrics. The total number of metrics covered in these topics calculated is presented in the table below.
Table 2.6: Top and bottom topics

The second key finding of the study is that different rating agencies exhibit significant variations in the level of granularity when measuring the same topic. The figure below highlights the differences in the number of metrics used by the eight rating agencies when assessing each topic. The most pronounced disparities occur within in the Business Ethics and Corporate Governance topics under Governance pillar. For instance, one agency uses fewer than 4 metrics to evaluate Business Ethics, while another employs over 120 metrics. Similar variations are observed under the Environment pillar, with Environmental Management (ranging from 1 to 43 metrics), GHG Emissions (1 to 47 metrics), and Pollution & Waste (3 to 43 metrics) showing comparable differences. The variation in metrics used to assess the performance of the same topic is a key reason a company receives different ESG ratings from different agencies. 
Figure 2.8: Topic coverage across ESG rating products

Source: OECD
It’s particularly noteworthy that the study analyzed the characteristics of these 2,000+ ESG metrics and categorized them into four types: policy-based metrics, activity-based metrics, output-based metrics, and business environment metrics. Within each category, there is a further distinction between qualitative and quantitative metrics: 
Policy-based metrics focus on the policies, strategies, or commitments a company establishes to manage its ESG risks, impacts, and opportunities (such as net-zero commitments or board diversity targets). These metrics emphasize whether a company has formulated relevant sustainability policies, without directly measuring the outcomes of their implementation. During the rating process, this type of metric can be further evaluated based on the extent of its disclosure and/or the quality of the disclosed policy or target, benchmarked against peers or best practices. The table below summarizes the most frequently occurring policy-based metrics under the E, S, and G pillars.
Table 2.7: Policy-based metrics

Topic
Qualitative
Quantitative
Environment
GHG emissions
Existence of GHG emission targets
Absolute scope 1-3 GHG reduction targets
Society
Human rights
Policy on human rights
/
Governance
Corruption, bribery & fraud
Scope of corruption and bribery policy (e.g. are all suppliers required to have anti-corruption policies and programmes to verify compliance?)
/






Activity-based metrics evaluate the specific actions or measures a company takes to achieve its policies or goals. They focus on the management practices, processes, or projects implemented in operations, highlighting a company’s tangible efforts on ESG issues, though they do not necessarily prove the effectiveness of these actions. The table below summarizes the most frequently occurring activity-based metrics under the E, S, and G pillars.
Table 2.8: Activity-based metrics

Topic

Output-based metrics measure the results or impacts of a company’s strategies and operational activities, typically using quantifiable data to reflect its actual performance in ESG areas. These metrics provide a more direct indication of a company’s ESG outcomes. The table below summarizes the most frequently occurring output-based metrics under the E, S, and G pillars.
Table 2.9: Output-based metrics

Business environment metrics focus on external factors—such as geographic location, industry characteristics, or market conditions—that may influence a company’s ESG performance or risk exposure. These metrics shed light on the external challenges or opportunities a company faces regarding specific ESG issues, without directly assessing the company’s own efforts or performance. Here are examples of this kind of metrics:
Table 2.10: Business environment metrics
Type of Risk Factors
Metrics

Exposure to geographic risk factors
Exposure to water stress areas/ Headquarter location/ Level of regional corruption

The figure below illustrates the proportion of the four metric types among the 2,000+ metrics. We can see that ESG rating products predominantly rely on input-based metrics (68%), which encompass policy-based metrics (50%) and activity-based metrics (18%). Output-based metrics account for 30%, while business environment metrics make up just 2%. This indicates that rating agencies primarily assess companies’ sustainability performances based on their self-reported policies and activities implemented to address potential and actual ESG impacts, risks, and opportunities, with less emphasis on the outcomes of these policies and activities or the influence of the operating environment on companies themselves. The takeaway is that an overreliance on “input-based metrics” by ESG rating agencies may lead companies to prioritize superficial compliance over genuinely preventing or mitigating risks.
Figure 2.9: Distribution of metric characteristics

Source: OECD, https://www.oecd.org/content/dam/oecd/en/publications/reports/2025/02/behind-esg-ratings_4591b8bb/3f055f0c-en.pdf
Another graph below provides us with the details of the distribution of policy, activity, output and business environment metrics across topics. As you can see, activity-based metrics are used throughout 23 ESG topics, and are most prominent in the following five topics: Business Ethics (83%), Environmental Management (78%), Corporate Responsibility (73%), Product Stewardship (66%), and Corporate Governance (49%). 
Policy-based metrics, meanwhile, are most frequently used in Labor Rights (47%), Biodiversity & Land Use (41%), Corruption, Bribery & Fraud (36%), and Human Rights (31%). 
Output-based metrics, often quantitative (e.g., emission diversity, board gender diversity ratio), are applied to topics with measurable and standardized outputs, leading in Diversity, Equity, and Inclusion (57%), Energy Management (57%), Pollution & Waste (51%), GHG Emissions (48%), and Health & Safety (43%). 
As we’ve noted earlier, very few metrics fall under the business environment category, with their highest proportions found in Climate Resilience & Adaptation (51%) and Business Resilience (18%).
Figure 2.10: Distribution of metric characteristics

Source: OECD, https://www.oecd.org/content/dam/oecd/en/publications/reports/2025/02/behind-esg-ratings_4591b8bb/3f055f0c-en.pdf
As outlined in Table 1, each rating agency’s metrics encompass more or less 1,000+ data points. These qualitative data (e.g., a company’s sustainability policies and actions) and quantitative data (e.g., carbon emissions, employee diversity, and the policy strength of the company’s operating locations) are typically obtained from the following sources: 
Data sources for companies ESG risk management metrics (e.g. sustainable policies & activities & outputs).
Corporate documents: corporate websites, annual reports, proxy statement, annual general meeting voting results, other securities fillings sustainability disclosure reports (ESG reports) and CDP responses.
Government databases: for example U.S. Toxic Release Inventory, Comprehensive Environmental Response and Liability Information System (CERCLIS), Resource Conservation and Recovery Act (RCRA) Hazardous Waste Data Management System, etc.
Popular, trade and academic journals.
News media: major news publications globally, including local-language sources across a range of markets.
Data source for companies’ governance evaluation:
Mainly from regulatory sources and stock exchanges: Company-level and director-level information from regulatory databases including company registries and national securities-regulator databases, and from stock exchange websites. Relevant provisions of company and securities laws (including listing-related and takeover regulation) from government/state websites and databases
Data source for business environment metrics:
Rating agencies often use corporate documents (e.g. annual reports, investor presentations and financial & regulatory filings) to understand the business segments of companies and their characteristics. 
To assign macro-level risk exposure to companies’ geographies of operation and industries, we take MSCI’s data sources as an example, they include lots of publicly available database from NGOs and Governments.
