The divergence of ESG ratings
Institutional investors with a sustainability preference depend wholly or partly on external ESG rating providers (Dimson et al., 2020), making the reliability of these agencies critical for accurately identifying corporate sustainability risks and opportunities. However, through our earlier analysis, we’ve already observed initial differences in methodology among mainstream ESG ratings. This inconsistency can lead to confusion, potentially undermining the quality of investment decisions. 
A notable research conducted by Berg et al. (2022) examined the divergence of ESG ratings and drivers behind. The study analyzed the 2014 ESG rating data of 924 companies simultaneously rated by six major agencies (KLD, Sustainalytics, Moody’s ESG, S&P Global, LSEG, and MSCI), conducting a Pearson correlation test with results shown in the figure below. It found that, overall, the correlation between different ESG ratings is notably low. The average correlation for composite ESG ratings across agencies was just 0.54, while the average correlations for scores on the E, S, and G pillars were even lower, at 0.53, 0.42, and 0.30, respectively. Moreover, the authors conducted a robustness test using 2017 data and obtained similar results.


Figure 2.11: Correlations between ESG ratings


Source: Berg et al. (2022)
Berg et al. (2022) argues that the divergence among ESG ratings primarily stems from three sources:
Scope Divergence: This refers to differences in the attributes or categories that rating agencies select to evaluate ESG performance. As noted earlier, for instance, when assessing the E pillar performance of a bank-related industry, MSCI focuses on just one key issue, while LSEG includes 10 key issues.
Measurement Divergence: This occurs when agencies use different indicators or methods to measure the same attribute or category. As discussed previously, the number and type of metrics used to assess a single key issue vary across agencies, and MSCI and LSEG employ different processes to derive key issue performance from these metrics.
Weight Divergence: This pertains to variations in the relative importance (weights) that rating agencies assign to different categories or indicators when calculating an overall ESG score.
Berg et al. (2022) empirically decomposed the contributions of these three divergence drivers to rating divergence. The findings reveal that measurement divergence is the most significant factor, accounting for 56% of the divergence across rating products. Scope divergence contributes to 38% of the divergence, while weight divergence accounts for only 6% of the overall divergence.
Figure 2.12:  The source of divergence

Source: Berg et al. (2022)
Case: MSCI vs. LSEG
Next, we will use MSCI and LSEG as case studies to elaborate on the key steps in their company rating processes and the differences in their methodologies. The figure below illustrates the overall MSCI ESG rating process. In brief, MSCI selects a range of key issues under the environmental, social, and governance pillars for companies in a certain industry, assigns weights based on the significance of these key issues, and then evaluates their performance using the metrics discussed earlier. Each key issue is scored on a scale of 0 to 10, and these scores are weighted and averaged to derive the company’s final ESG rating. LSEG’s ESG rating process shares similarities with MSCI’s (so do other raters), but the two differ significantly in their selection of key issues or topics, their approaches to weight allocation, and their methods for measuring key issue. We will now distinguish these differences step by step.
Figure 2.13: Hierarchy of ESG scores

Source: MSCI, https://www.msci.com/documents/1296102/34424357/MSCI+ESG+Ratings+Methodology.pdf

Key issues or topics selection for MSCI and LSEG:
MSCI has developed the Global Industry Classification Standard (GICS), mapping all companies under evaluation to 163 GICS sub-industries based on their business nature. The figure below displays the 33 key issues in MSCI’s rating system. Depending on the extent to which a company’s business activities in each industry generate externalities affecting the environment and society, MSCI selects a unique combination of environmental and social key issues for that industry rather than incorporating all 33 key issues into the assessment. Since governance issues hold importance across all industries, the 6 governance-related key issues are integrated into the evaluation process for all 163 GICS sub-industries. 
For example, the GICS-10101010 Oil & Gas Drilling industry faces key issues such as Carbon Emissions, Biodiversity & Land Use, and Toxic Emissions & Waste under the E pillar; Health & Safety, Community Relations, and Human Capital Development under the S pillar; and all six key issues under the G pillar. In contrast, the GICS-40101010 Diversified Banks industry has only Financing Environmental Impact as a key issue under the E pillar; Human Capital Development, Consumer Financial Protection, Access to Finance, Privacy & Data Security, and Responsible Investment under the S pillar; and all six key issues under the G pillar.
Figure 2.14: MSCI ESG ratings key issue hierarchy 


Source: MSCI, https://www.msci.com/documents/1296102/34424357/MSCI+ESG+Ratings+Methodology.pdf
LSEG employs its Thomson Reuters Business Classification to categorize rated companies into different industries. However, regardless of the industry, the evaluation of a company’s ESG performance must cover all topics (see the Pillar-Topic-Key Issue hierarchy in the earlier table). For instance, while MSCI assesses the Diversified Banks industry using just one key issue under the E pillar (Financing Environmental Impact), LSEG’s Banking Services industry requires evaluation across three E pillar topics—Emissions, Innovation, and Resource Use (encompassing 10 key issues). Therefore, when assessing the ESG performance of companies within similar industries, the two rating agencies differ in the scope of key issues they select.
The approach to assigning weights among key issues/topics for MSCI and LSEG:
As mentioned earlier, MSCI tailors a unique combination of Environmental and Social key issues for each industry and assigns weights among only the selected key issues based on their significance. Taking the GICS-40101010 Diversified Banks industry as an example, the weight distribution among its key issues is shown in the table below. We can see that, for this industry, Access to Finance carries greater importance than Responsible Investment. Besides, after subtracting the weights assigned to the Environmental and Social pillars from 100%, the remaining weight is allocated to the Governance pillar. However, MSCI ensures that the Governance pillar receives a minimum weight of 33%.
Table 2.12: MSCI weights
Environmental
Social
Governance
Financing Environmental Impact-12.9%
Human Capital Development-15.2%
Consumer Financial Protection-14.5%
Access to Finance-12.1%
Privacy & Data Security-12.1%
Responsible Investment-0.2%
Overall-Governance-33%

While LSEG includes all ESG topics in its assessment for every industry, the distinction between industries lies in the varying weight allocations among topics. Unlike MSCI, LSEG does not distribute weights among key issues; instead, it assigns weights only among topics. It first calculates a total score by summing the scores of all key issues under a certain topic, then uses the percentile ranking of that total score within the industry as its topic’s score. The figure below illustrates LSEG’s weight allocation across different industries.
Obviously, MSCI assigns weights of 12.9%, 54.1%, and 33% to the E, S, and G pillars for the Diversified Banks industry, whereas LSEG allocates 14%, 50%, and 36% to the same pillars for the Banking Services industry. Thus, within similar industries, the two rating agencies also differ in the approach to assigning weights among key issues/topics.
Figure 2.15: MSCI ESG magnitude (materiality) matrix 

Source: LSEG, https://www.lseg.com/content/dam/data-analytics/en_us/documents/methodology/lseg-esg-scores-methodology.pdf
Methods of measuring key issues for MSCI and LSEG:
For assessing key issues under the Environmental and Social pillars for companies in a specific industry, MSCI employs a comprehensive approach that evaluates both a company’s risk exposure and its risk management, with specific metrics assigned to each. This method yields the following outcomes: if a company has high risk exposure in a particular key issue but performs poorly in risk management, the score for that key issue will be low. Conversely, if a company faces high risk exposure but implements strong risk management strategies, the key issue score will be high. Meanwhile, if a company’s risk exposure is low and this company doesn’t develop very detailed risk management strategies for this risk, its key issue score maybe still high. 
SOCIETE DES GRANDS PROJETS in MSCI 
MSCI has evaluated the performance of Health & Safety issue of a company called SOCIETE DES GRANDS PROJETS (responsible for construction and operation of Grand Paris Express) in 2024. MSCI first assesses its risk exposure to this key issue from two perspectives: Business Types and Business Locations. For Business Type, MSCI uses the metric “Percentage of operations in business segments with high/moderate/low levels of fatalities and industrial injuries”. Since 100% of this company’s activities involve the construction and operation of public transportation—an industry with high fatality/injury rates—MSCI assigns a score of 10 for Business Type. However, from the Business Location angle, the country where the company operates (France) has low employee fatality rates, leading MSCI to assign a score of 2.6. So the overall risk exposure score is 7.6. Next, MSCI evaluates the company’s risk management based on governance, strategy & targets, and performance, using metrics such as “Is there a board-level committee responsible for health & safety strategy?”, “Scope of health & safety policy”, “Target to improve health & safety performance”, “Number of work accidents leading to work stoppage”, and “Number of working days lost due to work accidents”. After a comprehensive assessment, the risk management score is 4.3. Given the company’s high risk exposure but average risk management performance, its overall score for the health & safety key issue is 3.7. By the way, we can observe that MSCI uses business environment metrics when assessing the risk exposure, while its evaluation of risk management incorporates a mix of policy-based metrics, activity-based metrics, and output-based metrics.
Besides, all companies are evaluated on the Governance Pillar, which includes six key issues within the Corporate Governance and Corporate Behavior topics. This evaluation adopts a deduction approach, starting with a preset score of 10 for each key issue under the G pillar, then subtracting points based on the gap between these key issues and best practices, ultimately yielding the final governance score.

Figure 2.16: Health and Safety issue 


Source: MSCI, https://www.societedesgrandsprojets.fr/sites/default/files/2024-09/ESG%20Ratings%20Full%20Report_ESG%20Ratings%20Report_2024-08-09T00_00_00.000Z.pdf#page=15.25
LSEG takes a simpler approach when using metrics to measure key issue performance. For example, when evaluating the “Career Development and Training” key issue with the metric “Average Training Hours”, it employs a percentile rank scoring methodology. This calculates where a company’s average training hours stand within its industry. If a company’s training hours rank in the top 10% of the industry, it receives a score of 0.9 or higher.
We can observe notable methodological differences in how ESG ratings providers approach the governance dimension. MSCI employs a multi-dimensional evaluation framework that integrates a company’s business environment (such as industry and geography), risk management policies and practices, as well as actual performance outcomes. Scores are initially set at 10 and then adjusted downward based on identified weaknesses, ultimately determining the company’s scores on specific governance issues. By contrast, LESG adopts a percentile ranking system for selected indicators, emphasizing a firm’s relative performance within its industry. This method is simpler and more intuitive, yet arguably less nuanced in capturing the complexity of governance risks.
These methodological contrasts raise several critical questions. Which approach more accurately reflects a company’s underlying ESG risk profile—the comprehensive, context-sensitive model of MSCI, or the relative, industry-benchmarking system of LESG? To what extent might these differences lead to divergent ratings for the same company, and how should investors reconcile such inconsistencies when making capital allocation decisions?
Another angle worth exploring is the potential for rating arbitrage. When discrepancies between rating systems are substantial, investors may selectively highlight the rating framework that best supports their investment thesis. Similarly, corporations may find themselves making strategic choices: should they align more closely with the sophisticated, globally recognized standards of MSCI, or should they concentrate on improving their relative standing under systems like LESG, which may offer more immediate reputational or market benefits within an industry?
The approach to incorporating ESG controversies
We have now clarified how ESG raters calculate composite ESG scores step by step and explored the differences in their methodologies, which can lead to variations in ESG rating outcomes. Before delving deeper into the divergence among ESG ratings, it’s necessary to briefly address the incorporation of ESG controversies. Beyond calculating the overall ESG score, rating agencies track sustainability-related controversial events a company faces over a period (e.g., environmental fines or lawsuits) and adjust the ESG score accordingly. For instance, in cases of significant controversies, MSCI deducts points from the risk management score of the relevant key issue, indirectly affecting the total ESG score. LSEG, on the other hand, employs a method similar to its ESG score calculation to independently compute an ESG Controversies Score, which is then weighted and averaged with the overall ESG score to produce an ESGC score. Using machine-learning method, RepRisk constructs a RepRisk Index database, offering real-time, event-driven assessments of corporate controversy risks, which is widely used in academic research and investment decisions.
Case: Tesla and the fragmented landscape of ESG ratings 
As discussed earlier, methodological differences among ESG rating providers often lead to inconsistent outcomes. This inconsistency is not merely inconvenient—it creates confusion and raises fundamental questions about the meaning and credibility of ESG assessments.
Tesla provides a particularly well-known case. As one of the world’s most prominent electric vehicle manufacturers, Tesla has undeniably accelerated the global shift toward cleaner transportation. Yet in 2022, S&P Global removed Tesla from its ESG index, citing concerns about workplace practices and corporate governance. Meanwhile, MSCI continued to assign Tesla strong ratings based on its substantial contributions to reducing global carbon emissions.
The divergence stemmed from different emphases. S&P Global flagged issues such as reported discrimination in Tesla factories and accidents related to autopilot technology, viewing these as serious governance and social responsibility shortcomings. MSCI, by contrast, prioritized Tesla’s role in decarbonization through its electric vehicles and energy products. However, in June 2023 Tesla was reinstated into the S&P 500 ESG Index after enhancing its environmental disclosures, particularly around material sourcing and hiring practices. 
This case underscores how differences in dimension weighting can lead to starkly divergent evaluations. Moreover, it illustrates how corporate disclosure and governance improvements can directly alter ESG ratings. These dynamics invite deeper reflection: Should ESG ratings emphasize environmental contributions above all, or should they place equal weight on governance and social responsibility? How should investors interpret conflicting ratings—might they selectively rely on whichever rating aligns with their preferred investment thesis, thereby weakening the disciplining effect of ESG investing?
From the corporate perspective, an even sharper dilemma emerges: should firms focus on genuinely improving their ESG performance, or simply play the “compliance disclosure game” to secure favorable scores? Ultimately, the Tesla example raises a broader structural question: do we need a globally unified ESG rating framework to prevent a single company from receiving contradictory evaluations, or can divergence itself be valuable, offering investors multiple lenses and encouraging richer debate?
Figure 2.17: The S&P 500 ESG index methodology summary

Source: S&P Global, https://www.spglobal.com/spdji/en/documents/education/education-sp-500-esg-index-defining-the-sustainable-core.pdf
ESG and corporate finance
In the previous section, we explored how companies disclose their performance on various material issues through sustainability standards and frameworks, and how ESG rating agencies use their methodologies to evaluate the sustainability performance of companies across different industries based on ESG reports and other information. We also noted that investors leverage this non-financial information to guide their investment decisions. However, a lingering question remains: What is the true significance of the array of sustainability actions, disclosures, ratings, and investments centered around ESG? In other words, can companies that embrace sustainability genuinely mitigate various risks, facilitate financing, operations, and profitability, and ultimately achieve the “long-term value creation” that ESG advocates passionately promote? In this section, we will draw on a range of empirical academic studies and real-world examples from policymakers, banks, and companies across industries to provide a deeper and clearer understanding of this question.
How a company’s sustainable practices affect its cost of capital
A company’s financing activities form the foundational step for undertaking various projects. The core objective of financing is to secure the necessary funds to achieve strategic and operational goals. Broadly, financing can be divided into two categories: equity financing and debt financing. Equity financing involves raising funds by selling company shares, allowing investors to become shareholders and share in the company’s profits and growth potential, though it dilutes existing shareholders’ ownership. For instance, in 2022, Chinese wind power company Sany Renewable Energy conducted an initial public offering, raising nearly 5.5 billion RMB to fund production line upgrades, turbine R&D, and wind power industrial park projects. Debt financing, on the other hand, involves raising funds through issuing bonds or borrowing from financial institutions, requiring the company to repay the principal and interest within an agreed timeframe. For example, in 2022, Danish turbine manufacturer Vestas secured a 475 million EUR green loan from the European Investment Bank to support R&D for large-megawatt turbines. The choice between these financing methods depends not only on a company’s financial health, market conditions, and industry characteristics but also on external factors such as macroeconomic policies, financial market volatility, and investor preferences. In recent years, as concepts like sustainability and climate change have gained prominence, regulators and financial systems worldwide have increasingly integrated companies’ ESG performance into regulatory and investment decisions. Consequently, a company’s sustainability performance has become a key factor influencing its cost of capital and capital structure. This section will delve into how ESG performance affects financing costs for companies across countries, exploring the underlying economic principles and market mechanisms driving this phenomenon.
Sustainability and cost of debt
Developed economies, particularly the European Union, have taken the lead in integrating sustainability into financial systems. The EU’s 2018 Sustainable Finance Action Plan highlighted that achieving the 2030 energy and climate goals—reducing greenhouse gas emissions by 40% compared to 1990 levels, ensuring at least 27% of final energy consumption from renewables, and achieving at least 30% energy savings—requires an annual investment of €180 billion. Extending to sectors like transport, water, and waste would necessitate €270 billion. To align such large-scale funding with EU sustainability objectives, the EU established the EU Taxonomy, a unified classification system for climate, environmental, and socially sustainable activities, defining what qualifies as sustainable and identifying investment areas with the greatest sustainability impac. Additionally, the EU’s Sustainable Finance Disclosure Regulation (SFDR) mandates disclosures at both product and entity levels. At the product level, portfolios managed by banks and investment firms, UCITS funds (Undertakings for Collective Investment in Transferable Securities), AIFs (Alternative Investment Funds), insurance-based investment products, and pension products must disclose their sustainability impacts (e.g., the proportion of the portfolio aligned with the EU Taxonomy) and the impact of sustainability risks on product returns. At the entity level, financial firms must disclose their sustainability risk policies and the Principal Adverse Impacts of their lending or investment decisions (e.g., GHG intensity of investee companies or exposure to controversial weapons). Clearly, these EU regulations have made it imperative for financial institutions to incorporate sustainability factors into lending or investment decisions (in this section, specifically bond investments).
To investigate whether corporate sustainability practices genuinely impact financing costs and to understand the mechanisms behind such effects, academic research has produced substantial empirical evidence from both developed and developing economies. Using a sample of global companies from 2002 to 2021
Houston and Shan (2022) show that banks are more likely to grant loans to borrowers with ESG profiles similar to their own and positively influence the borrower’s subsequent ESG performance.  Conditional on obtaining new loan financing within a 2-year period centered on the end date of the original loan, they find that borrowers are less likely to renew loans with the same lead lender(s) if there was a negative ESG-related reputational shock, increasing financial costs.  Apergis et al. (2022) examined bond issuance and used corporate bond yield spreads as a proxy for financing costs, reaching comparable conclusions. Eliwa et al. (2021) provided a more detailed exploration of the theoretical mechanisms driving this impact. According to stakeholder theory, stewardship theory, and transformational leadership theory, strong ESG practices signal to creditors a company’s management efficiency and integrity, demonstrating its ability to allocate resources effectively to meet stakeholder concerns while maintaining profitability. This reduces the likelihood of decisions that prioritize shareholders at the expense of creditors, mitigating agency conflict risks. Additionally, robust ESG performance lowers the reputational risks creditors face, leading to reduced interest rate demands. Furthermore, ESG disclosure itself reduces information asymmetry between lenders and companies, thereby decreasing risks associated with investment uncertainty. 
The mechanism by which ESG disclosure reduces information asymmetry, as well as the role of improved ESG performance in mitigating operational and reputational risks, will be analyzed in greater detail below when we examine the impact of corporate sustainability on the cost of equity financing. Besides, the authors of this study also highlighted the moderating role of the institutional environment in the relationship between corporate sustainability performance and the cost of debt. Institutional theory and legitimacy theory suggest that formal institutions (e.g., laws and policies) and informal institutions (e.g., norms, conventions, and value systems) shape the behavior of organizations and individuals. Companies seek to achieve “legitimacy status” to secure essential support and resources. In institutional environments that prioritize sustainability, companies that fail to adhere to sustainable social norms and regulations face a higher cost of debt.
Research focusing on Asian countries yields similar conclusions. Gracia and Siregar (2021) and Nguyen and Duong (2025) examined listed companies in ASEAN countries and Vietnam, respectively, using the ratio of a firm’s interest expense to its average net debt as a proxy for the cost of debt. Their findings still confirmed a negative relationship between ESG performance and debt financing costs. However, studies on China suggest that ESG performance significantly impacts cost of debt only when loan amounts are substantial. 
Most studies indicate that, among the three main sustainability factors—environment, social, and governance—bond investors and lenders prioritize environmental performance (Eliwa et al., 2021, Saharti et al., 2024, Attig et al., 2025). This is likely because environmental factors, such as climate risks and regulatory policies, are most likely to impact a company’s short-term financial performance or align with national strategic priorities. Moreover, banks are likely to promote bank-dependent borrowers’ ESG performance, and that their influence is predominantly concentrated among environmental and social issues that likely focus the spotlight on the lenders, leading to severe reputational and financial consequences (Houston and Shan, 2022) .
when a company has a weaker prior lending relationship with a lender, the lender tends to focus primarily on evaluating the company’s environmental performance(Saharti et al., 2024). Besides, Attig et al. (2025) tested the relationship between greenwashing (or selective environmental disclosure) and loan spreads from the banks, and found it will significantly increase the cost of debt.
Sustainability and cost of equity
In addition to issuing bonds in public markets or securing bank loans, companies can raise funds by selling equity. This can be done by attracting venture capital or private equity investments, or, for companies eligible to go public, through an initial public offering (IPO). And companies can further raise funds through secondary stock offerings. Consequently, the cost of equity financing is a critical factor for companies to consider. Academic research estimates the cost of equity financing (i.e., investors’ expected returns) using two main approaches: the Implied Cost of Capital (ICC) and factor models based on realized returns (e.g., CAPM). The ICC is an ex-ante measure of equity cost, calculated as the internal rate of return that equates the current stock price with the present value of expected future cash flows. In contrast, factor models like CAPM are ex-post methods, relying on historical return data from market indices, government bonds, and individual stocks to estimate the cost of equity. Similar to the mechanisms by which ESG impacts the cost of debt financing, a company’s sustainability practices can theoretically create long-term value for shareholders by reducing information asymmetry, mitigating various risks in business operations, and aligning with customers’ sustainable purchasing preferences, thereby lowering the cost of equity financing. In fact, a substantial body of empirical research has tested these influence mechanisms and their effects.
Li et al. (2024) used a sample of Chinese listed companies from 2009 to 2020 to empirically analyze changes in the cost of equity after firms received ESG ratings from SynTao, a Chinese rating agency. They measured the cost of equity using the Implied Cost of Capital method. The results showed that, after receiving a SynTao ESG rating, a company’s cost of equity decreased by 45.6 basis points compared to before. The authors attributed this reduction to two factors: first, a decrease in information asymmetry between investors and the company, which reduces investment uncertainty; second, the soft market constraints imposed by third-party ESG ratings, which establishes an effective external monitoring mechanism and then increases the cost of non-compliance, encouraging prudent business operations, reducing corporate operational risks, and consequently lowering investors’ risk premiums. Both mechanisms have been repeatedly discussed earlier. The study empirically validated the presence of these mechanisms. The authors used the number of securities analysts covering a company and the number of research reports about this company as proxies for information asymmetry, finding that both significantly increased after a company received an ESG rating, with the effect being more pronounced for firms with previously high information asymmetry. Besides, the authors measured operational risk using the standard deviation of return on assets volatility over a continuous three-year period and the annual standard deviation of daily individual stock returns, respectively, confirming that receiving an ESG rating can significantly reduce a company’s operational risk. Ng and Rezaee (2015) and Lynch and O'Hagan-Luff (2024), in their empirical studies of global listed companies, similarly concluded that improved corporate sustainability performance reduces the cost of equity financing, and only environmental and governance sustainability performance dimensions contribute to this relationship，while social sustainability performance is only marginally, related to cost of equity. And the relationship between ESG and cost of euquity is strengthened when  economic sustainability disclosure is strong.
with this effect being more pronounced in countries with stringent sustainability-focused institutional environments. Building on this, Lynch and O'Hagan-Luff (2024) further found that the suppressive effect of enhanced ESG ratings on the cost of equity exhibits a diminishing marginal trend.
Merton (1987) capital market equilibrium model posits that expanding a firm’s investor base leads to a lower cost of capital and higher market value. Therefore, it is necessary to examine whether a company’s sustainability performance influences institutional investor ownership, as this indirectly affects the cost of capital. Gibbons (2024) investigated changes in institutional investor ownership of global listed companies before and after the implementation of mandatory E&S disclosure regulations across more than 40 countries. Initially, the authors were skeptical about whether investors genuinely paid attention to corporate E&S information, as many institutional investors claim to invest only in companies with ESG ratings, yet most asset managers lack explicit mandates or policies to prioritize environmental and social goals. They might merely engage in “cheap talk” to enhance their reputation without truly valuing corporate sustainability information. However, empirical results showed that institutional investors do care about this information, with institutional ownership increasing by 0.66% following the implementation of disclosure regulations. This represents a 1.3% increase relative to the sample average. Moreover, the authors found that the additional investors were long-term-oriented institutional investors with ESG preferences, specifically those who are signatories to the Principles for Responsible Investment (PRI). 
Turning our focus back to Asia, Marshall et al. (2022) conducted a similar study on Indian listed companies and found that foreign investor ownership significantly increased after firms complied with mandatory corporate social responsibility (CSR) disclosure regulations. Notably, investors from civil law countries and long-term investors showed greater investment in firms subject to mandated CSR. Similarly, Feng et al. (2024) found that improved ESG performance among Chinese listed companies attracted significant and long-term investment from foreign institutional investors. This positive effect of sustainability on institutional investor ownership was more pronounced in non-state-owned enterprises, firms with higher business risks, and heavily polluting companies.
In the preceding sections, we have summarized academic research on how corporate sustainability performance affects the cost of debt and equity financing, as well as the mechanisms behind these effects. Moreover, some studies have explored the impact of sustainability on a company’s overall cost of capital, typically measured by the Weighted Average Cost of Capital (WACC), which reflects the average cost of debt and equity financing, weighted by their proportions in the capital structure. Most empirical studies confirm that companies with strong sustainability practices are rewarded with a lower WACC, and we won’t reiterate those findings here. However, it’s worth noting that some studies have produced inconsistent results. For instance, Tron et al. (2025) conducted regression analyses by grouping global listed companies by industry and found that improvements in sustainability performance did not significantly impact WACC across industries. Besides, Gjergji et al. (2021)’s study on small and medium-sized Italian listed companies revealed that sustainability disclosures actually increased WACC. The authors offered three explanations: 1) SMEs typically have simpler management structures with less information asymmetry between managers and owners, and banks, as the primary financing source, provide significant oversight, diminishing the role of ESG disclosure in reducing information asymmetry; 2) ESG disclosure requires technical expertise, advertising skills, organizational planning, and ongoing management, which are costly, and SMEs, with limited resources, may see their profitability squeezed by such investments; 3) SMEs often focus on niche markets, and disclosing ESG information could lead to the leakage of sensitive proprietary information, increasing the risk of imitation by competitors and threatening their competitive advantage. These findings offer a new perspective on tailoring sustainability disclosure requirements for companies of different sizes and types.
Sustainability and capital structure
A company’s sustainability performance not only affects its cost of capital but also influences its choice of financing methods, thereby shaping its capital structure. According to the pecking order theory(Myers and Majluf, 1984), differences in information asymmetry between different kinds of capital providers and the company, as well as varying sensitivities to the same information, lead to disparities in financing costs. As a result, companies follow a specific hierarchy when selecting financing methods to minimize costs. Specifically, companies prioritize using retained earnings. If internal funds are insufficient, they prefer debt financing, as creditors are less sensitive to the company’s information(Höck et al., 2023). This lower sensitivity means creditors focus primarily on the company’s ability to repay, without interpreting borrowing as a sign of operational issues that would prompt higher interest rates. Moreover, debt financing offers tax shield benefits. In contrast, equity financing (issuing new shares) is often perceived by external investors as a signal that the company’s value is overinflated or that it may be facing operational challenges, potentially leading to a stock price decline and higher financing costs. In other words, companies seek more internal financing sources (Denis and Mihov, 2003).
Asimakopoulos et al. (2023) used data from U.S. listed companies between 2002 and 2019 to examine the impact of corporate sustainability performance on debt structure. The findings revealed that companies with higher ESG ratings tend to shift their financing from public debt (bond issuance) to private debt (bank loans). Specifically, a one standard deviation increase in the natural logarithm of the ESG score leads to an approximately 3.8% increase in bank loans and a 6.7% decrease in bond issuance. Since bank loans are considered a more internal source of financing compared to bond issuance(Denis and Mihov, 2003), this finding aligns with the pecking order theory. The authors suggest that companies with strong ESG performance avoid debt overhang and the resulting underinvestment, opting for more flexible financing methods like bank loans. A similar conclusion was reached by Newton et al. (2024) , also using data from U.S. listed companies, which explored how negative media coverage of firm-level environmental, social, and governance practices influences a firm’s debt choice. This study used RepRisk’s ratings of corporate ESG reputational risk as a proxy for negative media coverage and found that companies with higher ESG reputational risk (i.e., poorer ESG performance) rely more on public bonds than bank loans. The authors explain that, compared to public bondholders, banks—through long-term lending relationships and stringent monitoring—can more effectively detect a borrower’s unsustainable practices. So companies with high ESG reputational risk have an incentive to conceal such behaviors to avoid the reputational or financial consequences of bank scrutiny. Public bondholders, because of their dispersed ownership, are less sensitive to ESG risk information and therefore have a smaller impact on companies’ debt financing costs. Meanwhile, aiming to mitigate deposit losses from their own reputational risks, banks may proactively reduce loan supply to high-ESG-risk companies, further pushing these firms toward public bond issuance. Bagh et al. (2024) analyzed the issue from the perspective of developing countries, using a sample of 3,420 listed firms from BRICS countries (Brazil, Russia, India, China, and South Africa) over the period 2013 to 2022. The study found that improved ESG performance significantly increases a firm’s book leverage. The author argues that companies with strong ESG performance typically secure more favorable borrowing terms from creditors, which encourages them to increase debt financing, thereby raising their book leverage. 
It’s particularly noteworthy that, Gibbons (2024) examined changes in the capital structure of listed companies across 40 countries before and after the implementation of mandatory environmental and social (E&S) disclosure requirements. The results showed a significant 0.39% increase in net equity issuance, equivalent to a 2.29% rise relative to the sample average, while net debt issuance significantly decreased. This suggests that companies tend to favor equity financing after disclosing E&S information. The study attributes this shift to a unique clientele mechanism, where an increase in institutional investors with specific E&S preferences alters the shareholder base. These investors influence corporate decision-making through engagement with management or by exercising voting rights. Through empirical testing, the authors confirmed that the rise in institutional investors who are signatories to the Principles for Responsible Investment (PRI) significantly impacts equity issuance, validating the existence of the clientele mechanism.
MSCI evidence: ESG and cost of capital
We have gained insights from extensive empirical research in academia about the impact of corporate sustainability practices (ESG disclosure or ESG practice) on the cost of capital and the underlying theoretical mechanisms. However, we believe what will spark greater interest is real-world examples from industry. For instance, if companies with better sustainability performance have a lower cost of capital, what is the specific difference in cost of capital between high-sustainability and low-sustainability companies? Does this difference vary in magnitude across regions or industries? How do banks in different countries incorporate a company’s sustainability into their lending decisions? How do leading asset management firms in sustainable investing identify companies resilient to sustainability-related risks? In this section, we will present a series of real-world cases to help everyone better understand the critical role of ESG in determining a company’s cost of capital.
A MSCI study allows us to clearly identify the differences in cost of capital among companies with varying ESG risk exposures. The study analyzed stocks from the MSCI ACWI Index and bonds from the MSCI Corporate Bond Index, covering 4,319 unique issuers. These issuers span developed and emerging markets across 11 industry sectors, with data from August 2015 to May 2024. The study measured cost of equity and cost of debt using two methods each. For cost of equity, the first method calculated expected stock returns using the Capital Asset Pricing Model, while the second used β (systematic risk exposure) as a proxy. For cost of debt, in addition to interest expense on debt, the study used bond issuance spreads relative to treasury yields as a proxy, as mentioned earlier. Both β and spreads are relative metrics, controlling for macro factors like differences in risk-free rates or equity-market risk premiums across regions. The cost of capital was calculated using the Weighted Average Cost of Capital (WACC). The issuers were divided into five groups based on their MSCI ESG ratings: top ESG rating (highest-scoring companies), high, mid, low (average-scoring companies), and bottom ESG rating (lowest-scoring companies), to compare the differences in their cost of capital.
From the picture below, we can see that for the overall cost of capital, companies with the lowest ESG ratings indeed faced the highest financing costs during the sample period, averaging 7.9%. In contrast, top-ESG-rated companies had an average cost of capital of only 6.8%.





Figure 2.18: MSCI ESG rating and cost of capital

Source: MSCI ESG Research, https://www.msci.com/research-and-insights/paper/msci-esg-ratings-and-cost-of-capital
The equity and debt components of the total financing costs confirmed these findings. Top-rated companies had a significantly lower cost of equity, with a median below 9%, compared to over 10% for the lowest ESG-rated group, even after accounting for home-market factors like local risk-free rates and equity risk premiums. Issuers in the top quintile also exhibited a significantly lower beta relative to their local stock market compared to those in the bottom quintile. The same pattern was observed for the reported cost of debt and its dynamic, market-determined proxy (credit spread). 
Figure 2.20: MSCI ESG rating and cost of equity

Source: MSCI ESG Research 
Figure 2.21: MSCI ESG rating and cost of debt

Source: MSCI ESG Research 
The study also analyzed the cost of capital differences between the highest and lowest ESG-rated company groups across 11 industry sectors. The consumer staples sector showed the most significant difference, with high ESG-rated companies having a cost of capital nearly 2.25% lower than low ESG-rated companies. In the health care, information technology, communication services, and materials sectors, this gap was also substantial, exceeding approximately 1.5%. A similar relationship was observed for the cost of equity of the highest-rated companies across all sectors and for the cost of debt in all sectors except consumer discretionary and health care. This was further confirmed by company-specific market proxies, such as stock beta and credit spreads, which were lower for companies in the top ESG-rating quintile across all sectors.
Developed markets and emerging markets differ dramatically in access to equity capital, but the negative relationship between ESG ratings and cost of equity is evident in both types of economies. In developed markets, the communication services sector shows the most significant difference, with high ESG-performing companies having a cost of equity over 1.5% lower than low ESG-performing companies. In other sectors, except materials and energy, the difference is mostly below 0.5%. In emerging markets, the information technology sector stands out, with high ESG-performing companies having a cost of equity about 2% lower than low ESG-rated companies. Most other sectors in emerging markets show a difference close to 1%, indicating that improving ESG performance and strengthening resilience to sustainability-related risks in emerging markets leads to greater marginal improvements in cost of equity. Notably, within our sample, companies with better ESG performance in the utilities, financials, and real estate sectors in developed markets actually faced higher costs of equity. Besides, a similar trend persists in both investment-grade and high-yield bond groups, with the cost of debt for high ESG-performing companies being lower than for low ESG-rated companies, and this difference is more pronounced in the investment-grade bond group compared to the high-yield bond group.
Figure 2.22: Differences within industry sectors

Source: MSCI ESG Research 
Figure 2.23: Differences within industry sectors

Source: MSCI ESG Research 
Due to varying economic and technological levels, differing intensities of sustainable policy implementation, and significant cultural differences across regions, it is necessary to explore how the relationship between ESG performance and cost of capital differs by region. MSCI’s study divided the sample companies’ regions into three groups: APAC (Asia-Pacific), EMEA (Europe, Middle East, and Africa), and the Americas. Overall, the phenomenon of high ESG ratings leading to lower cost of capital (both equity and debt) is significant across all regions. In APAC, companies in Taiwan with high ESG ratings had a cost of capital about 1.6% lower than those with low ESG ratings, followed by China (1.3%). The difference in South Korea, Hong Kong SAR, India, Australia, and Malaysia was within 0.5%, with Japan being the only country showing no significant difference. However, from the cost of debt perspective, China, India, Malaysia, and Hong Kong SAR followed the general trend, but high ESG-rated companies inin Japan, Taiwan, Australia, and South Korea faced higher costs of debt, which markedly differs from the patterns observed in EMEA and the Americas.
Figure 2.24: Differences among markets

Source: MSCI ESG Research 
MSCI’s research provides compelling evidence that corporate sustainability performance can indeed influence the cost of capital—an effect that appears particularly pronounced in certain industries and emerging markets. This insight is highly relevant for banks and asset managers as they refine methods to price ESG-related risks. 
A closer look reveals striking industry and regional variations. For example, the consumer staples sector shows the largest gap in financing costs between high- and low-ESG performers, while in financials and utilities the relationship can even reverse. What explains this divergence? Is it due to differences in regulation, investor scrutiny, or the difficulty of measuring ESG materiality in these sectors?
Regional patterns are equally thought-provoking. In countries such as Japan and South Korea, companies with stronger ESG profiles paradoxically face higher debt costs. This counterintuitive outcome may reflect cultural norms, policy frameworks, or unique features of local banking systems. For multinational banks and global asset managers, such variations raise an important question: how can they leverage ESG ratings to identify firms with stronger resilience to risk, while tailoring strategies differently for emerging and developed markets?
At the same time, if ESG ratings significantly affect financing costs, the risk of greenwashing becomes more pressing. Companies may feel tempted to enhance disclosures or emphasize selective achievements primarily to reduce their cost of capital, without delivering genuine sustainability improvements. This poses a challenge for both regulators and investors: what mechanisms—such as standardized disclosures, independent audits, or stricter penalty structures—can help ensure that lower capital costs are tied to real ESG progress rather than cosmetic compliance?
Case: Policy-driven green credit from China’s banks
We can gain a more intuitive understanding from a more micro perspective, such as the policies and practices of banks in different countries regarding sustainable lending. From the earlier chart, we can see that Chinese companies exhibit some of the largest gaps in the APAC region between high and low ESG-rated firms, whether in terms of cost of capital, cost of debt, or cost of equity. To support the national climate strategy of achieving carbon peak by 2030 and carbon neutrality by 2060, the former China Banking and Insurance Regulatory Commission issued the Guidelines for Green Finance in the Banking and Insurance Industries in 2022. These guidelines require banks to establish ESG risk assessment standards for clients and use the results as key factors in determining credit ratings, loan amounts, and interest rate pricing for enterprises. 
As early as 2021, the People’s Bank of China (PBoC), China’s central bank, introduced a structural monetary policy tool: the Carbon Emission Reduction Support Tool. This tool operates as follows: financial institutions first provide loans to enterprises in key carbon emission reduction sectors at rates aligned with the Loan Market Quotation Rate (LPR). They can then apply for funding from the PBoC’s Carbon Emission Reduction Support Tool, through which the central bank provides funds equivalent to 60% of the loan principal at an interest rate of 1.75%. For context, as of May 2025, China’s five-year interbank Loan Prime Rate is 3.50%, and the interest rate for borrowing from the PBoC’s Medium-term Lending Facility (MLF) is 2.5%. The low funding cost of 1.75% strongly incentivizes banks to offer loans for corporate carbon emission reduction projects, enhancing access to finance for green-focused enterprises, which typically perform well in the environmental dimension of ESG. 
According to the framework of the Carbon Emission Reduction Support Tool, national financial institutions utilizing this tool are required to prioritize carbon emission reduction loans for enterprises in three key areas: clean energy, energy conservation and environmental protection, and carbon reduction technologies. Additionally, the People’s Bank of China mandates that financial institutions applying for the tool provide carbon emission reduction data for the relevant loans. After receiving support, institutions must disclose quarterly to the public information including the supported carbon reduction sectors, number of projects, loan amounts, weighted average interest rates, and carbon reduction data. The following is the carbon emission reduction loan information disclosure table for the third quarter of 2024 from the China Development Bank, and all national financial institutions utilizing the Carbon Emission Reduction Support Tool can find similar tables.

These disclosure requirements ensure transparent and quantifiable monitoring and reporting of carbon emission reduction loans, guaranteeing that funds are effectively directed to carbon reduction initiatives. Furthermore, the central bank, in collaboration with relevant authorities, verifies the reliability of disclosures through methods such as third-party audits to ensure the tool’s operability, calculability, and verifiability. As of the end of June 2024, the Carbon Emission Reduction Support Tool has facilitated over 1.1 trillion RMB in carbon emission reduction loans, supporting more than 6,000 market entities and driving annual carbon emission reductions of nearly 200 million tons.
Moreover, in 2021, the PBoC issued the Green Finance Evaluation Plan for Banking Financial Institutions, requiring 24 major banks to incorporate metrics such as the proportion of green loan balances and green bond holdings in their total business into quarterly and annual assessments. Green loans can serve as collateral for the Standing Lending Facility (SLF), and green bonds rated AA or above can be used as collateral for the MLF. The chart below illustrates the quantitative indicators system of the evaluation plan. Such assessment mechanisms encourage banks to channel more funds toward “green” enterprises and those pursuing low-carbon transitions.

Taking China Minsheng Bank as an example, it is one of the first commercial banks in China to receive an MSCI AAA rating, and its ESG risk control policies hold significant reference value. Minsheng Bank has developed differentiated credit policies, entry standards, and risk control measures for over 50 key industries, including energy, transportation, mining, oil and gas, and raw materials, as well as critical issues like biodiversity conservation and rural revitalization. It prioritizes support for six major sectors: clean energy, clean production, energy conservation and environmental protection, ecological environment, green infrastructure upgrades, and green services. The bank is also gradually phasing out loans to projects with high energy consumption, high pollution, and excess capacity. The followings are some examples of credit policies for key industries: 
Energy Industry: Actively support the financing needs of clean energy and selectively support leading traditional energy companies in areas such as energy conservation, carbon reduction, and mergers or acquisitions. However, no credit support is provided for projects with ESG risks, such as those failing environmental impact assessments, not meeting pollutant emission standards, neglecting ecological protection or resettlement, or improperly handling hazardous waste. For instance, hydropower projects must implement resettlement and ecological protection plans and avoid sites in restricted development zones. Thermal power projects must address risks from rising environmental compliance costs and the potential substitution of traditional thermal power by rapidly developing renewable energy technologies.
Transportation Industry: No credit support is provided for warehousing and logistics enterprises involved with hazardous chemicals.
Agriculture and Forestry: No credit support is provided for enterprises with potential food safety risks, substandard environmental conditions at production sites, unqualified safety inspections, or those facing controversies in media or public opinion.
Oil and Gas Industry: No credit support is provided for projects with safety hazards, non-compliant production capacity, or failure to meet environmental standards.
Moreover, Minsheng Bank has clearly outlined ESG risk prevention measures for industries with significant potential environmental and social impacts, such as steel, cement, and non-ferrous metals. These ESG risks are integrated into due diligence, compliance reviews, credit approval, and post-loan management processes. Institutions applying for credit must provide an ESG due diligence checklist. If not provided, they cannot proceed to the next stage. For clients identified as medium or high ESG risk with negative review opinions, they are prohibited from entering the credit approval process.
Below are examples of these risk prevention measures: 
Thermal power, cogeneration, steel, cement, flat glass, non-ferrous metals, petrochemicals, chemicals, and aviation industries: Focus on total greenhouse gas emissions and emission intensity, as well as carbon market compliance for thermal power companies.
Thermal power, steel, petrochemicals, cement, non-ferrous metals, chemicals, and coal-fired boiler projects: Ensure compliance with air pollutant emission standards, installation of desulfurization and denitrification equipment, completion of ultra-low emission transformations for steel companies, and implementation of leak prevention measures for oil and gas in chemical companies. 
Paper, coking, nitrogen fertilizer, non-ferrous metals, printing and dyeing, agricultural and food processing, pharmaceutical raw materials, leather, pesticides, and electroplating industries: Monitor total wastewater emissions and compliance with emission concentration standards.
Agriculture, forestry, fisheries, infrastructure construction, transportation, and tourism industries: Assess the occurrence of climate-related disasters in operating locations and evaluate the adequacy of corporate emergency plans.
Sewage, garbage, sludge, and hazardous waste treatment companies: Verify business qualifications and regulatory compliance to prevent secondary pollution risks.
Chemicals, petrochemicals, non-ferrous metals, coking, paper, and electronics industries: Scrutinize the transfer and disposal of hazardous waste, with a focus on verifying the qualifications of waste disposal vendors.
Coal mining, petrochemicals, chemicals, and construction industries: Evaluate the establishment of safety management systems, safety measures, and occupational disease prevention for employees.
Trade, retail, and service industries: Review product quality management systems, product recall procedures, and customer complaint handling.
Construction project loans: Ensure compliance with environmental and safety approvals, monitor adherence to the “three simultaneities” system (simultaneous design, construction, and operation of environmental and safety facilities), verify safety management during construction, and address community concerns regarding land acquisition, relocation, and ecological compensation.
Special clients with potential significant impacts on social stability or public interest (e.g., nuclear power projects, waste incineration in densely populated areas, large hydropower projects): Focus on site selection, radioactive effluent and pollutant emissions, storage and transportation of hazardous materials and waste, and safety during project construction and operation.
From the ESG risk management sections of sustainability reports from numerous major banks, it is evident that they all have similar ESG risk monitoring processes and set corresponding entry thresholds for key industries to avoid projects with high energy consumption, high emissions, excess capacity, or significant social risks. Although these banks do not explicitly mention linking ESG risks to loan interest rates, the low capital cost incentives from the central bank’s Carbon Emission Reduction Support Tool encourage them in practice to set loan rates for borrowing companies who positively pursue decarbonization slightly below the Loan Prime Rate (LPR). For example, in 2022, China Merchants Bank issued carbon emission reduction loans to 181 projects with a weighted average interest rate of 3.58%, while China Everbright Bank provided 35 million yuan in financing support to a photovoltaic company at a project loan rate as low as 3.45%, compared to the year-end LPR of 3.65%. In 2023, the Industrial and Commercial Bank of China issued carbon emission reduction loan to 841 projects, with a weighted average loan interest rate of 2.69%, while the year-end LPR was 3.45%.
From the discussion above, we see that China has tightly integrated ESG considerations into its financial system through state strategy and regulatory guidance. The central bank has linked monetary tools with ESG objectives, established incentive mechanisms for banks to prioritize green finance, and implemented strict disclosure and evaluation frameworks. These measures are designed to channel credit toward low-carbon development and strengthen the management of ESG-related risks.
Yet, this strong regulatory push also raises critical questions. Under such a framework, could high-pollution industries face a financing drought, potentially triggering negative spillovers for economic stability? When the People’s Bank of China provides low-interest refinancing through its Carbon Emission Reduction Facility, might this create over-incentivization or moral hazard, where firms rely too heavily on subsidies without pursuing genuine transformation? What safeguards can mitigate such risks? Commercial banks also confront a delicate balancing act: how can they fulfill mandates to support the green transition while still preserving profitability and managing credit risks effectively? 
Case: Green lending from Europe’s banks
The EU’s 2021 revised Strategy for Financing the Transition to a Sustainable Economy states that, based on the European Green Deal, the EU is committed to becoming the first climate-neutral continent by 2050 and reducing greenhouse gas emissions by at least 55% by 2030 compared to 1990 levels. To achieve this, the EU requires an estimated EUR 350 billion in additional annual investment over this decade to meet its 2030 emissions-reduction target in energy systems alone, alongside EUR 130 billion needed for other environmental goals. The strategy emphasizes that investments in unsustainable activities and assets are increasingly likely to become stranded as climate and environmental challenges become more significant. Over the next decade, trillions of euros in sustainable investments will require collaboration between the public sector and private capital, including commercial banks. 
To align large-scale funding with sustainable goals, the EU developed the EU Taxonomy, which defines what constitutes sustainable economic activities, and adopted the Sustainable Finance Disclosure Regulation (SFDR), requiring financial institutions to disclose sustainability information about themselves and their financial products. This encourages institutions to identify sustainability-related risks and opportunities, motivating them to engage in sustainable finance activities. Financial institutions with a low proportion of sustainable activities in their portfolios risk losing competitive advantage in the EU’s sustainable transition trend (e.g., missing opportunities to issue green bonds to finance sustainable activities or facing divestment from large asset owners) and increasing exposure to stranded asset risks. 
As a result, major European banks have positively responded to the EU’s call to seize the commercial opportunities presented by the continent’s sustainable transition. In contrast to the top-down approach adopted by Chinese banks, as mentioned earlier, which involves leveraging the central bank’s Carbon Emission Reduction Support Tool to provide loans for sustainable activities, European banks generally adopt a self-driven approach, setting targets for sustainable investment and financing volumes, as well as mid- and long-term carbon reduction goals across all scopes. They adjust their business strategies, capital allocation, and stakeholder engagement methods based on these targets. Next, we will use Deutsche Bank as an example to elaborate on this characteristic in detail.
As a global financial institution, Deutsche Bank manages a loan portfolio of nearly 500 billion euros and assets under management in its private banking division reaching 518 billion euros (excluding DWS). The bank sees it as its responsibility to promote and accelerate the transition to a more sustainable society and economy. Deutsche Bank has integrated sustainability principles into its products, services, policies, and processes across all key areas. The bank has set quantitative targets for both sustainable finance and carbon emission reduction. In terms of sustainable finance, Deutsche Bank is committed to achieving cumulative sustainable financing and investment volumes of 500 billion euros from January 1, 2020, to the end of 2025 (excluding DWS). Under this target, any financing—including but not limited to asset-based lending, corporate-level lending, trade finance, and capital markets instruments such as certified sustainability-linked loans, sustainability-linked hedging, or other instruments—can be classified as sustainable finance. Specifically, by the end of 2025, the Corporate Bank division is expected to contribute 110-125 billion euros, the Investment Bank division 253-288 billion euros, and the Private Bank division 88-128 billion euros. Moreover, Deutsche Bank has projected revenues related to these sustainable finance activities, estimating a total of 4.4 billion euros from 2022 to 2025.
Figure 2.24: Deutsche Bank’s target

Source: Deutsche Bank, https://www.db.com/files/documents/csr/sustainability/sdd23/02-20230302-SDD-Strategy-Outlook-and-Ambition-for-2025.pdf?language_id=1
To achieve this sustainable finance target, Deutsche Bank developed a sustainable finance framework in 2020. This framework defines the scope of sustainable economic activities supported by the bank and establishes detailed entry criteria for what types of finance activities can be classified as sustainable finance, aligning with the climate goals of the Paris Agreement and the United Nations Sustainable Development Goals. 
First of all, Deutsche Bank defines six guiding principles for the classification of economic activities as environmentally and/or socially sustainable as the basis of the whole framework:
Principles for environmental sustainability: 
Climate change mitigation and adaptation: Economic activities enabling, directly or indirectly, a substantial reduction of GHG emissions and/or increase of energy efficiency. Measures adapting to acute and chronic physical risks caused or intensified by climate change.
Protection, restoration and promotion of natural resources and healthy ecosystems: Protection of marine and terrestrial [living] resources including water, critical and high-carbon stock ecosystems, and other primary resources. Restoration of biodiversity and ecosystems. Pollution protection and control and general reduction of resource use. 
Transition to a circular economy: Prevention of waste and promotion of recycling and reuse of material. 
Principles for social sustainability: 
Respect for human rights: Respect for, protection and enablement of basic human rights, e.g. food provision, labor protection. 
Enabling living conditions: Access to affordable housing and infrastructure for transportation, ICT, and energy provision. 
Access to essential services: Inclusive access to health, education, and financial services.
Based on these principles, Deutsche Bank defines three parameters for classifying a transaction as sustainable finance: (1) Use of proceeds; (2) Company profile; (3) Financial product itself. If any of these parameters contributes to achieving the Paris Agreement goals and the UN Sustainable Development Goals, and aligns with Deutsche Bank’s environmental and social sustainability principles outlined above, the transaction can be classified as sustainable finance. 
For the "Use of Proceeds", the bank has specified economic activities for each key sector and social theme aligned with its principles and established exemplary eligibility criteria for these environmental activities (or impact indicator for socially sustainable activities) (see tables below). These eligibility criteria adhere to recognized international market standards and practices, such as the ICMA Green Bond Principles, ICMA Social Bond Principles, and, where feasible, align with the technical screening criteria developed by the EU Taxonomy.
For the second parameter - Company profile:
If the use of proceed is not specified or dedicated to facilitate a certain activity (e. g. general corporate purposes), eligibility of a transaction for classification as sustainable finance will be assessed on the basis of the company profile. Transactions are eligible if the company (1) derives ≥ 90% of its revenues from activities eligible listed in the above tables and; (2) is not involved in any activities which may have material negative environmental and/or social impacts
For the third parameter – Sustainability-linked products
Sustainability-linked products are financial instruments offered by Deutsche Bank to help clients achieve sustainable goals and support environmental and social economic development. These products are not classified based on the use of proceeds but are tied to predefined Sustainability Performance Targets (SPTs). SPTs must meet the following requirements: (1) Ambitious: The targets should be challenging and drive substantial change. (2) Material for clients’ business and core economic activities: The targets must be relevant to the client’s core business and economic activities. (3) Compliant with the six core principles for environmental and social sustainability: The targets must align with the core environmental and social sustainability principles in Deutsche Bank’s framework.
The above Framework forms the foundation for Deutsche Bank to achieve its sustainable finance goals. Limited credit resources and financial services are prioritized for economic activities that meet environmental or social sustainability requirements, as well as companies whose primary business focuses on addressing environmental and social sustainability issues. Consequently, companies with strong sustainability performance or those “in high ESG risk industries but with ambitious transition plans” have greater access to finance.

In addition to the 500 billion EUR sustainable finance target by the end of 2025, in 2021, Deutsche Bank announced its membership in the Net-Zero Banking Alliance (NZBA), a global initiative convened by the United Nations Environment Programme Finance Initiative (UNEP FI) and led by banks. The NZBA aims to align banks’ lending, investment, and capital market activities with the goals of the Paris Agreement, achieving net-zero greenhouse gas emissions by 2050 or earlier and limiting global temperature rise to 1.5°C. Based on the NZBA commitment statement and the UNEP FI Guidelines for Climate Target Setting for Banks, alliance members must commit to achieving net-zero emissions for their operations and financed emissions by 2050. Within 36 months of joining, they must set science-based decarbonization targets for all high-emission sectors. Moreover, alliance members are required to annually disclose their progress on reducing Scope 1, 2, and 3 emissions (including financed emissions) in accordance with NZBA guidelines. Therefore, Deutsche Bank has set mid-term and long-term emission reduction targets for 2030 and 2050, using 2021 as the baseline, based on the Science Based Targets Initiative (SBTi) methodology. Here, we focus solely on Scope 3, Category 15—Financed Emissions—because achieving emission reduction targets for financed emissions will inevitably require structural adjustments to the bank’s loan portfolio, which will impact the financing capabilities of companies exposed to sustainability risks to varying degrees.
By the end of 2022, Deutsche Bank’s financed emissions totaled 34.4 Mt CO2e/y, with the majority (89%), or 30.5 Mt CO2e/y, stemming from its 106.6 billion EUR corporate loan portfolio, and 11%, or 3.9 Mt CO2e/y, from its 175 billion EUR European residential real estate portfolio. Thus, decarbonizing the corporate loan portfolio is a key priority for the bank. 





Figure 2.25: Loan exposure and financed emissions

Source: Deutsche Bank, https://www.db.com/what-we-do/responsibility/sustainability/transition-plan/documents/Transition-Plan.pdf
Deutsche Bank has set decarbonization targets for 2030 and 2050 for the eight most carbon-intensive sectors: Oil & Gas (Upstream), Power Generation, Automotive (Light Duty Vehicle), Steel, Coal Mining, Cement, Shipping, and Commercial Aviation. The focus on emissions scopes and the measurement of decarbonization progress vary by sector. For example, in the oil and gas sector, the bank targets Scope 3 emissions of upstream companies, measured by absolute financed emissions, while for the power generation sector, it focuses on the Scope 1 emission intensity of financed entities. To achieve its corporate loan decarbonization target, Deutsche Bank adopts a stakeholder engagement approach. It begins with a footprint analysis of clients in the eight key sectors to identify high-emission clients. The bank then assesses these clients’ decarbonization transition strategies, prioritizing engagement with those who disclose limited or no transition strategies, assisting them in developing such strategies and increasing disclosure. For clients unwilling to engage or commit, the bank implements measures such as phasing out or reducing exposure and limiting loan maturities.
Figure 2.26: Approach to engage with high-emitting clients 

Source: Deutsche Bank
When evaluating clients’ transition strategies, Deutsche Bank primarily focuses on the quality of their sustainability disclosures, such as alignment with the TCFD framework, whether climate targets are set and certified by the SBTi, the process of stakeholder engagement during the implementation of transition strategies, and how the company’s ESG policies are integrated with its transition strategy. The specific evaluation criteria are detailed in the table below.

From the eligibility criteria for providing financial services to environmentally and socially sustainable activities outlined in Deutsche Bank’s sustainable finance framework, as well as the evaluation dimensions for assessing clients’ transition strategies to achieve its Scope 3 decarbonization targets, it is clear that companies prioritizing sustainable or climate solutions as their core business, those with robust disclosures aligned with mainstream international sustainability frameworks/standards, and those with ambitious climate targets and feasible transition strategies are more likely to receive financing support from international banks. These real-world cases further confirm the conclusions drawn from prior academic research: strong sustainability performance can indeed reduce corporate financing constraints and lower the risk of financial distress.
From the above discussion, we see that the EU Green Deal has established a comprehensive sustainable finance framework to channel capital toward green investment. Unlike China’s “central bank guidance + policy incentives” model, European banks rely more on self-driven strategies. For example, Deutsche Bank has articulated six sustainability principles and three classification standards to guide its lending and investment practices, offering a paradigm for how European banks can reduce constraints on green credit while maintaining market flexibility.
These practices prompt several deeper reflections. Comparing global models, China’s regulator-driven approach ensures consistency, scale, and policy alignment, but risks creating rigidity and potential distortions. Europe’s market-driven model fosters flexibility, innovation, and competition, but may suffer from fragmentation or insufficient ambition without strong policy backing. Which approach is more suitable for emerging markets, where institutional capacity and capital market depth are often limited?
Focusing on Deutsche Bank’s strategy further raises critical questions. Under what conditions should banks opt for complete divestment from high-emission sectors versus offering transitional financing to support credible decarbonization plans? How can banks rigorously evaluate whether a traditional high-emission company—such as an oil and gas firm—has a credible transition strategy? What benchmarks or disclosure standards are necessary to distinguish genuine transformation from mere “greenwashing”?
Case: The rise of sustainability-linked loans (SLLs)
The cases of sustainable lending policies from Chinese and European banks discussed earlier essentially demonstrate that “companies actively pursuing sustainable transformation are more likely to access bank credit resources”. However, what we may be more interested in is whether achieving specific sustainability goals allows a company to secure a lower cost of capital, such as reduced bank loan interest rates. I can confirm that this is indeed the case. 
In recent years, sustainability-linked loans (SLL) have emerged as a new sustainable financing tool, gradually gaining public attention. The world’s first sustainability-linked loan was a 1 billion euro Revolving Credit Facility signed in 2017 between Philips and a consortium of 16 banks led by ING. The innovative aspect of this loan was that its interest rate was tied to Philips’ sustainability performance, evaluated through ESG ratings provided by the third-party agency Sustainalytics. If Philips’ Sustainalytics ESG rating improved (indicating better sustainability performance), the loan interest rate would decrease, lowering the cost of financing. Conversely, if the ESG rating declined, the financing cost would increase. According to ING, the interest rate discount or premium for such SLLs typically fluctuates between 5% and 10% of the interest rate margin. 
The introduction of this loan spurred rapid growth in the sustainability-linked loan market and led to the release of the Sustainability Linked Loan Principles (SLLP) in March 2019 by the Loan Market Association (LMA), the Loan Syndications and Trading Association (LSTA), and the Asia Pacific Loan Market Association (APLMA), providing a standardized framework for subsequent similar loans.
According to statistics from Japan’s Ministry of the Environment, the global sustainability-linked loan (SLL) market was only 6.5 billion USD in 2017 but grew to 673.7 billion USD by 2022. Although it declined to 489.3 million USD in 2024, the absolute value remains significant. In terms of regional distribution, Europe has consistently led the world in SLLs, followed by North America, while the Asia-Pacific (APAC) region has also gained considerable scale since 2021.
Figure 2.27: Global sustainability-linked loan amount

Source: the Ministry of Environment based on the Environmental Finance Database, Green Finance Portal, data as of August 20, 2025, https://greenfinanceportal.env.go.jp/en/loan/sll_issuance_data/sll_market_status.html
Figure 2.29: Global sustainability-linked loan amount (by region)

Source: the Ministry of Environment based on the Environmental Finance Database, Green Finance Portal, data as of August 20, 2025, https://greenfinanceportal.env.go.jp/en/loan/sll_issuance_data/sll_market_status.html
