From the preceding analysis, we can see that sustainability-linked loans (SLLs) have emerged as a fast-growing debt financing instrument in global markets. By linking loan pricing to a firm’s ESG performance, SLLs introduce differentiated cost of capital across companies, thereby encouraging emission reductions, energy transition, and broader sustainable development.
This innovation also raises several critical questions. Why might tying interest rates to corporate sustainability outcomes be more effective than merely increasing disclosure requirements? Europe has consistently taken the lead in the SLL market—does this stem from its regulatory environment, financial market structures, or investor preferences that are more aligned with sustainability objectives?
At the same time, SLLs pose potential risks. Could firms engage in greenwashing to inflate ESG scores and secure lower borrowing costs, thereby undermining the credibility of the market? Beyond interest rate adjustments, what other financial instruments—such as bonds, derivatives, or structured products—could be designed to incorporate sustainability performance triggers? These questions highlight both the promise and the complexity of embedding sustainability into financing.
How a company’s sustainability enhances its profitability and long-term value
Once a company secures financing, it embarks on formal business operations, channeling funds into specific investment projects. Through these projects, the company produces goods or services to meet customer demands, aiming to maximize revenue while minimizing costs and expenses to boost profits. A company’s profits—or more precisely, investors’ expectations of future profits—drive its market value growth. In essence, profitability and the corresponding rise in market value represent a company’s core objectives in the business world. However, enhancing profitability is far from a simple accounting exercise. The factors determining revenue, costs, and expenses are complex and intertwined with the diverse interests of multiple stakeholders. For instance, employees may seek more meaningful work, access to valuable training for long-term career growth, or greater cultural and background diversity within the company to spark innovation. Upstream suppliers often prefer to partner with reputable firms that demonstrate stable, long-term profitability. Meanwhile, downstream customers, increasingly influenced by environmental consciousness, may favor products made from biodegradable materials.
The success of a company’s operations thus hinges on the interplay of these stakeholders. A critical factor is the extent to which the company addresses their varied demands. In the context of our theme, sustainable practices—such as sustainability disclosures or improved ESG performance—naturally align with the interests of these stakeholders. But do these practices truly deliver the anticipated improvements in profitability and long-term value? Over the years, academic research has produced a wealth of empirical studies that offer valuable insights into this question.
Sustainability and profitability & market value
Numerous studies focusing on developed countries and select emerging markets have confirmed that corporate sustainability practices, including sustainability disclosures and performance, significantly enhance financial outcomes. These benefits are evident in both accounting-based metrics (typically measured by Return on Assets, Return on Equity, or occasionally Return on Capital and Return on Investment) and market-based metrics (commonly assessed using Tobin’s Q, though some studies use Earnings per Share or Dividend per Share). For instance, a study by Li et al. (2018) analyzing UK listed companies from 2004 to 2013 found that a one-standard-deviation increase in ESG disclosure scores led to a 17.872% rise in Tobin’s Q. Similar findings have emerged from research on the European Union (Sandberg et al., 2023, Qureshi et al., 2020) and the United States (Atif et al., 2022, Eccles et al., 2014). Building on this, Velte (2017) observed that for German listed companies, improvements in the governance pillar of sustainability had a stronger positive impact on ROA compared to the environmental or social pillars, though none of these pillars significantly affected Tobin’s Q. Meanwhile, Atif et al. (2022) identified a negative relationship between ESG disclosures and cash holdings in U.S. firms, with lower cash holdings correlating with higher financial performance. Turning to Asia, similar positive effects have been observed in listed companies in South Korea(Lee et al., 2016), Taiwan (China)(Lu et al., 2025), and mainland China(Marie et al., 2024, Zhou et al., 2022, Chen and Xie, 2022). When these companies are held by longer-term investors with ESG preferences, garner greater media attention, or face higher agency costs initially, the positive impact of sustainability practices on its profitability becomes even more pronounced(Chen and Xie, 2022).
Scholars such as Li et al. (2018) and Fafaliou et al. (2022) have outlined several mechanisms through which ESG disclosures enhance firm market value：
Improved Price Informativeness: Between 1975 and 2009, the contribution of tangible assets to market capitalization dropped from roughly 82% to 19%. As a result, investors increasingly demand enhanced non-financial disclosures. ESG disclosures provide additional, diversified information, enabling stock prices to reflect a company’s value more accurately and comprehensively.
Reduced myopic actions: Greater transparency in ESG disclosures narrows the information gap between corporate managers, shareholders, and other stakeholders. This discourages managerial rent-seeking or short-term opportunistic behaviors, such as aggressive earnings management, insider trading, or related-party transactions. Moreover, sustainability policies encourage management to adopt a long-term perspective, ultimately enhancing firm value over time.
Alignment with Stakeholder Interests: ESG practices can mitigate information asymmetry between corporate managers and various stakeholders, fostering stronger relationships with them. By doing so, these practices help balance and address the diverse demands of stakeholders, reducing agency costs and ultimately promoting the long-term value of the firm. From a consumer perspective, companies with strong ESG propositions tend to build customer loyalty and attract new market segments. From a partner perspective, large suppliers increasingly integrate ESG factors into their value chains, prioritizing sustainability in water use, packaging, and plastics. These suppliers prefer to collaborate with businesses that share their values, while resource-efficient firms can also reduce per-unit production costs. From an employee perspective, younger workers and millennials seek meaningful work, and companies with sustainability commitments are better positioned to attract and retain talent, often benefiting from higher productivity and efficiency. From a regulatory perspective, policymakers rely on the corporate sector to address issues like environmental pollution and workplace diversity, while, sustainable practices like a smaller environmental footprint can also reduce the likelihood of regulatory penalties. Finally, from an investor perspective, corporates’ ESG-focused projects—such as developing renewable energy plants—help avoid stranded assets (e.g. coal-fired power plants), mitigate downside risks, and attract long-term investors with ESG preferences, ultimately lowering the firm’s cost of capital.
Now we’ve explored the ways in which ESG practices contribute to a company’s long-term value, the next question is: whether companies prioritizing sustainability exhibit distinct characteristics in their governance structures, strategies, and operations compared to those with lower sustainability focus? 
Eccles et al. (2014) conducted an in-depth investigation into this question, leveraging nearly 30 environmental and social policies categorized in the Thomson Reuters ASSET4 database (now LSEG). By calculating the proportion of sustainability policies adopted by each company relative to the total available policies, they identified 90 “high-sustainability companies” with an average adoption rate exceeding 40% from 1993 to 2009. Using Propensity Score Matching, the authors paired these 90 firms with “low-sustainability companies” that shared similar characteristics—such as industry, firm size, profitability, market-to-book ratio, and leverage—but had lower sustainability policy adoption rates. This approach enabled a comparison of differences in governance structures, strategies, operations, and financial performance between the two groups.
The findings revealed that high-sustainability companies are far more likely to assign sustainability responsibilities to their board of directors and establish a dedicated board committee for sustainability. They also tend to link executive compensation to environmental and social performance metrics. These firms typically implement more comprehensive and engaged stakeholder management processes, attract a higher proportion of long-term-oriented investors, and emphasize long-term information during conference calls with sell-side analysts. Compared to their low-sustainability counterparts, high-sustainability companies are more proactive in measuring information related to key stakeholders—such as employees, customers, and suppliers—and enhancing the credibility of these metrics through auditing procedures. Moreover, they not only collect but also disclose a greater volume of nonfinancial data. In terms of profitability, high-sustainability companies outperform their low-sustainability counterparts in both stock market and accounting performance. Using the Fama-French four-factor model to measure alpha (the stock’s abnormal return), the author found that high-sustainability companies generate significantly higher alpha than low-sustainability firms. Similarly, in accounting metrics, the former exhibit notably higher Return on Equity (ROE) and Return on Assets (ROA) compared to the latter. This superior performance is particularly pronounced in business-to-consumer (B2C) companies. The author attributes these results to the stronger brand reputation and more efficient use of natural resources by high-sustainability firms, reinforcing the impact mechanisms discussed earlier.
The positive impact of sustainability on corporate profitability, as discussed earlier, is commonly observed in empirical studies focusing on developed countries. However, do these findings hold when we consider global firms as a whole or focus specifically on developing countries with lower socioeconomic levels? Yu et al. (2018) addressed this question using data from 1,996 companies in the MSCI All Country World Index from 2012 to 2016, employing the Bloomberg ESG disclosure score (ranging from 0 to 100) to measure corporate sustainability. The study found that, overall, higher ESG scores significantly boost a firm’s Tobin’s Q, but the relationship is not simply linear. For companies with ESG scores below 10.38, marginal increases in scores actually reduce Tobin’s Q, whereas scores above this threshold enhance market value. Given that the average disclosure score for MSCI World Index companies is 33, improving sustainability practices holds practical value. 




Figure 2.29: Impact of ESG disclosure on Tobin’s Q

Source: Yu et al. (2018), https://eprints.bbk.ac.uk/id/eprint/21605/
Xie et al. (2019) expanded the scope further, analyzing 6,631 companies across 74 countries and using the Bloomberg ESG disclosure score to assess sustainability’s impact. The results revealed a nonlinear effect on corporate efficiency. When ESG disclosure scores fall in the moderate range (approximately 25 to 50, with the current global average at 22), increases in scores significantly enhance corporate efficiency. However, when scores exceed 75, marginal improvements have a negative impact. For scores between 0 and 25, the effect is less pronounced.
Figure 2.30: Relationship between corporate efficiency and ESG scores

Source: Xie et al. (2019), https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/abs/10.1002/bse.2224
Building on this, Garcia and Orsato (2020) compared listed companies from 13 developed European and North American countries with those from developing countries like South Africa and Brazil, examining whether the impact of ESG performance (measured by LSEGESG scores) on Return on Assets (ROA) varies significantly due to differences in institutional environments. The results showed that findings for developed countries align consistently with prior studies, confirming a positive relationship between ESG performance and profitability. In contrast, for developing countries, improvements in ESG performance significantly and negatively affect firms’ profitability. Similar studies on Indian listed companies(Biju et al., 2025) and Latin American multinational enterprises(Duque-Grisales and Aguilera-Caracuel, 2021) also found that enhanced ESG performance either has no effect or negatively impacts profitability and market value. 
What explains these discrepancies? Buchanan et al. (2018)’s perspective may offer valuable explanations: They argues that the impact of corporate sustainability on profitability and market value hinges on the relative dominance of two competing mechanisms. The first mechanism, which promotes profitability, has been discussed earlier: investments in sustainability strengthen relationships with stakeholders, balancing their diverse needs—such as consumers’ demand for eco-friendly products, employees’ expectations for better welfare, and investors’ requirements for governance transparency and long-term profitability. This reduces agency conflict costs and enhances corporate reputation, ultimately boosting profitability and long-term value.
The second mechanism, however, negatively affects firm value. Drawing on overinvestment theory, authors suggests that in certain contexts—such as periods of severe financial constraints—sustainability investments become highly costly, with potential returns falling far below the firm’s cost of capital, leading to a loss of value. Through a study of US listed companies, they found that before the financial crisis, the “stakeholder engagement and agency cost reduction” mechanism prevailed, and ESG’s positive effects were evident. During the financial crisis, however, companies positively invested in sustainability lost more value compared to their control group, indicating that the overinvestment mechanism dominated.
Buchanan et al. (2018)’s explanation provides clearer insight into the conditions under which corporate ESG practices enhance profitability: When a society is not short of financial resources, has a relatively high level of legal system, and the government's image is more positive, and when all stakeholders hope to meet the needs of value realization at a higher level beyond basic survival needs, the investment of enterprises in ESG will play a positive role; conversely, if various resources in the society are scarce and the primary task of all stakeholders is to ensure the most basic survival rights and lack expectations for "additional self-actualization", the investment of enterprises in ESG will have a counterproductive effect.
A series of empirical studies on developing countries corroborates the perspective outlined earlier. Garcia and Orsato (2020) argues that institutional weaknesses in these nations—such as low-quality capital markets—lead companies more inclined to prioritize capital accumulation rather than invest their limited resources in CSR initiatives. Compared to firms in developed economies, companies in developing countries face less pressure to undertake CSR initiatives, whether from government agencies or nongovernmental organizations. This stems from the fact that residents in these countries are primarily focused on addressing fundamental needs, such as poverty alleviation, access to basic education and healthcare, and improved housing conditions, rather than sustainability-related issues. Moreover, institutional fragility limits governments’ ability to provide adequate infrastructure and services, further diverting attention from sustainability.
For companies in industries prone to ESG-related controversies—such as oil and gas, mining, paper and pulp, chemicals, and steelmaking, despite contributing to pollution, natural resource depletion, and labor disputes, such firms are often viewed as significant drivers of economic development and social welfare. For instance, they may support the construction of daycare facilities and hospitals, effectively assuming roles typically fulfilled by governments. In some South American multinational enterprises, where corruption, bribery, and information manipulation pervade government and business sectors, and investor protections are weak, corporate donations or social responsibility investments are frequently perceived as a form of bribery. Similarly, efforts to enhance corporate legitimacy—such as hiring external auditors, amending company bylaws, or granting greater independence to boards of directors—are often seen as costly overinvestments that undermine firm performance(Duque-Grisales and Aguilera-Caracuel, 2021).
Beyond the lack of societal focus on sustainability issues and constrained financial resources, cultural factors also play a significant role. For example, a 1983 study found that Malaysian companies were often reluctant to demonstrate even minimal concern for social and environmental issues (that is, they were unwilling to disclose their related social responsibility activities extensively). This reluctance stems from Malaysia’s predominantly Islamic culture, where a core principle emphasizes keeping meritorious deeds discreet. In this view, good deeds are performed for the sake of Allah, not for public display or societal recognition. Publicly disclosing such actions is often perceived as “showing off,” which conflicts with religious values and may even pose reputational risks.
In this section, we have examined the relationship between corporate sustainability performance, profitability, and market valuation, as well as the mechanisms through which these effects materialize across different economies. We also highlighted how regional and cultural factors shape this relationship, pointing to significant heterogeneity across markets.
Building on this discussion, several thought-provoking questions arise. Why does ESG disclosure enhance the informational content of stock prices, and how does it complement traditional financial metrics? Can governance mechanisms such as establishing a sustainability committee at the board level or linking executive compensation to ESG targets genuinely improve corporate performance—or do they risk becoming symbolic gestures of “box-ticking” governance?
Cultural and societal values add another layer of complexity. For instance, religious traditions or social norms may influence whether companies choose to disclose their social responsibility activities. In countries where resources are constrained and public awareness of ESG issues remains limited, should firms scale back ESG investments to prioritize core business operations? Or might such a short-term focus undermine their long-term competitiveness in global markets?
Looking ahead, will ESG investment gradually turn into a net positive in developing economies over the next decade? If so, what enabling conditions are required—such as stronger regulatory frameworks, improved disclosure standards, or growing investor demand for sustainable assets? These questions not only shed light on the dynamics between ESG and market value but also invite deeper reflection on how to align corporate strategies with the evolving expectations of global capital markets.
Next, we will adopt the perspective of stakeholders to explore how changes in consumer demand and new government procurement requirements create new growth opportunities for highly sustainable companies.
Opportunities from sustainable consumption
As mentioned earlier, strong ESG performance significantly enhances profitability, particularly in B2C business models. Consumption remains a key driver of global economic growth, and capturing and adapting to changes in consumer demands and preferences is critical for companies to gain a competitive edge. 
In 2022, Bain & Company surveyed over 16,000 consumers from Asia-Pacific countries (Singapore, Australia, Japan, South Korea, Thailand, Malaysia, Vietnam, China, India, Indonesia, and the Philippines) to explore whether sustainable consumption trends are emerging in the region. The survey first examined “how much they care about sustainability and their purchasing preferences”. The results (shown in the figure below) revealed that 51% of Asia-Pacific consumers are primarily focused on making healthier choices for themselves and their families, often viewing sustainable products as a path to better health—a proportion significantly higher than that of consumers in Europe and the US. Besides, 14% of consumers want to purchase healthy products that are good for the planet from companies that demonstrate concern for their employees and suppliers, a share comparable to developed regions in Europe and the US. In contrast, only 13% of Asia-Pacific consumers prioritize products with high cost-effectiveness. It is clear that Asia-Pacific consumers exhibit a strong, and even more pronounced, sustainable consumption trend compared to their counterparts in Europe and the US, with a greater demand for healthy products than for low-cost ones.



Figure 2.31: Percentage share of respondents

Source: Bain Asia-pacific Environmental, Social, and Corporate Governance Survey
This concern for the sustainability aspects of products also shows notable differences between developed and developing economies in the Asia-Pacific region. Emerging markets, overall, place greater emphasis on the health benefits of products and their environmental and social friendliness compared to mature markets. In Vietnam, the proportion of consumers prioritizing product health is as high as 66%, 27% more than in Japan. Meanwhile, Chinese consumers show the highest concern in the Asia-Pacific region for products’ environmental and social friendliness, at 25%, compared to just 5% in Japan. 
The motivations behind Asia-Pacific consumers’ purchases of sustainable products are also noteworthy. The survey results show that consumers buy sustainable products primarily for personal reasons rather than media influence. Over 30% of respondents purchase sustainable products to contribute to environmental issues, while 2,720 respondents are influenced by family or friends. This indicates a clear awareness of sustainable development among Asia-Pacific consumers.



Figure 2.31: Percentage share of respondents

Source: Bain Asia-pacific Environmental, Social, and Corporate Governance Survey
The motivations behind Asia-Pacific consumers’ purchases of sustainable products are also noteworthy. The survey results show that consumers buy sustainable products primarily for personal reasons rather than media influence. Over 30% of respondents purchase sustainable products to contribute to environmental issues, while 2,720 respondents are influenced by family or friends. This indicates a clear awareness of sustainable development among Asia-Pacific consumers.
Figure 2.32: Options for what promoted respondents to buy sustainable products

Source: Bain Asia-pacific Environmental, Social, and Corporate Governance Survey
Moreover, 90% of respondents are willing to pay a premium for sustainable products that have a positive environmental and social impact, with 42% willing to pay a 1-10% premium and nearly 30% willing to pay a premium of 25% or more. At the same time, 94% of consumers are willing to pay more for healthier products.
Figure 2.33: Percentage share of respondents willing to pay a premium for sustainable products

Source: Bain Asia-pacific Environmental, Social, and Corporate Governance Survey
Current consumer demand trends further prompt us to ask: Why do Asia-Pacific consumers tend to prioritize health over low price, while European and American consumers often place stronger emphasis on cost-efficiency? This divergence suggests that firms must adopt differentiated product and marketing strategies tailored to local sustainability preferences. For example, in the Asia-Pacific market, companies may find greater success by highlighting nutritional value, wellness benefits, and product safety, whereas in Western markets, strategies emphasizing affordability and accessibility of sustainable products may resonate more strongly.
Another critical challenge lies in narrowing the gap between consumers’ stated willingness to pay a premium for sustainable products and their actual purchasing behavior. Firms can address this discrepancy by improving transparency, strengthening trust through credible certifications, and communicating the long-term value of sustainability in terms that are tangible to everyday life. Digital tools, such as personalized recommendations or gamified loyalty programs, can also help translate sustainable intentions into consistent purchasing behavior.
Finally, the rise of sustainable consumption trends could intensify competition between multinational corporations and local enterprises. Multinationals often possess the resources to scale sustainable innovation rapidly, but local firms may enjoy an advantage in understanding cultural preferences and consumer psychology. This dynamic may accelerate both product innovation and market segmentation, reshaping the global competitive landscape in favor of firms that can most effectively integrate sustainability with local consumer expectations.
In summary, sustainable consumption trends have become mainstream particularly in the Asia-Pacific region, consumer goods companies should seize this opportunity to integrate sustainability into their value chains, producing safe, healthy, and green products. Companies that align with sustainable consumption demands will have the opportunity to significantly increase their income, enhance profitability, and boost long-term value.
Case: NaiSnow – green and healthy 
In recent years, growing consumer awareness of sustainability has fundamentally reshaped the strategic priorities of global brands. As environmental consciousness and health considerations increasingly influence purchasing behavior, many companies are rethinking how they design and market their products. NaiSnow (奈雪的茶), one of China’s leading tea beverage chains, has astutely identified the dual demand of Generation Z for healthier options and eco-friendly practices. This awareness has guided the firm to innovate both its product offerings and its environmental initiatives, positioning itself as a pioneer of “green and healthy” consumption in the competitive new tea beverage market.
From the outset, NaiSnow distinguished itself by prioritizing product quality and health benefits. Instead of relying on tea powder, creamers, or sugary syrups—ingredients commonly found in the industry—the company uses premium loose-leaf tea, fresh milk, and real fruits to craft its drinks. Its low-sugar recipes aim to offer consumers a more balanced and diverse nutritional experience. Over time, the firm has expanded this approach beyond beverages, launching nutritious meal-replacement shakes and bottled sparkling water to meet growing demand for health-oriented retail products. In 2022, NaiSnow replaced artificial sweeteners across all its stores with monk fruit extract, a natural sugar substitute. This move not only aligned with consumers’ health preferences but also addressed their concerns regarding the safety and nutritional value of synthetic sweeteners.
Figure 2.35: NaiSnow 66 Blueberry Mulberry Yogurt Shake

Source: NaiSnow, https://www.naixue.com/introduction/details?i=4
The intensifying competition in China’s tea beverage industry has further prompted NaiSnow to sharpen its “green and healthy” strategy. Rather than merely reducing additives, the firm has embraced what it calls a “strategy of addition,” enriching beverages with natural nutrients. A notable example occurred in June 2025, when NaiSnow collaborated with nutrition experts to launch the “No Added Sugar, Natural Nutrition+” initiative. Following the principle of “nutritious ingredients + freshly made + low-calorie,” the company introduced a series of light beverages and snacks, each made with at least three carefully selected green ingredients. Through expert-led campaigns, NaiSnow actively disseminated nutritional knowledge to consumers, reinforcing its brand association with health. For instance, nutritionist Dong Wencui analyzed the health benefits of NaiSnow’s “66 Blueberry Mulberry Yogurt Shake,” highlighting its antioxidant properties. The results were immediate: within just three days of launch, sales exceeded 500,000 cups, signaling strong market acceptance and the growing appeal of health-driven products.
Product innovation was accompanied by strategic adjustments to the company’s retail model. In March 2025, NaiSnow introduced a new store format—“NaiSnow Green”—centered on the theme of “natural freshness and a light lifestyle.” Targeting urban white-collar workers seeking low-calorie and nutritious meals for daily consumption, these stores offered lighter menu options such as grain bagels and energy bowls. The company rapidly expanded this format, opening more than 30 outlets across major cities like Beijing, Shanghai, Guangzhou, and Shenzhen. This expansion addressed consumers’ needs for healthy dining throughout the day, and the market responded positively: metrics such as average store order volume and same-store revenue have steadily increased since the strategy’s launch.
Figure 2.36: NaiSnow Green options

Source: NaiSnow, https://www.naixue.com/history
Environmental sustainability has also become a core operational principle for NaiSnow. The company has taken a series of steps to reduce packaging waste and carbon intensity. Beginning in 2020, it replaced plastic cup holders with recycled pulp alternatives, switched from plastic to paper straws, and later upgraded to PLA biodegradable straws by 2021. By 2022, these changes had been standardized across all its outlets. Other packaging components—such as bags, inner bread wrappers, and cutlery—were likewise replaced with recyclable or biodegradable materials. Furthermore, the “Little Green Bag” initiative encourages customers to reuse eco-friendly bags, fostering a culture of low-carbon living. Complementing these consumer-facing efforts, NaiSnow invested in back-end sustainability measures, including developing its own digital operations system, installing energy- and water-saving equipment, and establishing green logistics centers to integrate environmental practices into every stage of its operations.
Figure 2.37: NaiSnow recycled strategies

Source: NaiSnow, https://www.naixue.com/csr
Taken together, NaiSnow’s initiatives have played a catalytic role in steering the tea beverage sector toward healthier and greener business models. Yet despite these achievements, several challenges remain. The company has not disclosed its annual carbon emissions or set clear emission-reduction targets, leading to gaps in environmental transparency. Recurrent food safety issues have also affected its public image, while long-standing profitability pressures expose weaknesses in its financial management. These factors collectively pose obstacles to the company’s long-term sustainability and highlight the complex trade-offs between brand innovation, operational execution, and corporate accountability.
NaiSnow’s strategic initiatives illustrate how a new generation of tea beverage companies is rapidly adapting to shifting consumer preferences. By actively responding to the growing demand for healthier and more sustainable consumption, the company has not only opened new revenue streams but has also contributed to upgrading products and services across the industry, thereby enhancing consumer well-being. However, from an environmental perspective, NaiSnow’s practices remain insufficient. This asymmetric sustainability strategy—emphasizing health while neglecting environmental responsibility—invites broader reflection on whether differences in environmental information disclosure within the same industry could affect firms’ long-term competitiveness and capital market reputation. In the context of increasingly stringent regulations and the rapid development of green finance, companies that fail to provide adequate environmental disclosure may face heightened policy and reputational risks.
More fundamentally, this raises a critical question: can health- and sustainability-oriented strategies truly compensate for financial vulnerabilities in a highly competitive market? The tea beverage sector has relatively low entry barriers, intense brand competition, and strict demands for cost control and operational efficiency. If a firm’s “sustainability transition” remains limited to product innovation or branding narratives, without building structural advantages in areas such as supply chain management and operations, such strategies may struggle to support long-term profitability.
From the investor’s perspective, whether these strategic shifts are fully “priced in” is also an important and practical issue. On one hand, as sustainable development gains traction in capital markets, investors are paying increasing attention to corporate social responsibility and long-term value creation. Companies that emphasize health and sustainability may benefit from stronger brand premiums, more stable market shares, and easier access to financing. On the other hand, if their sustainability strategies lack substance or financial backing, investors may view them merely as short-term marketing tools rather than genuine sources of long-term competitiveness.
Ultimately, NaiSnow’s case reflects a broader trend: at the intersection of consumption upgrading and the sustainable movement, the ability of firms to strike a balance between health, environmental responsibility, and financial resilience is becoming a decisive factor shaping their future value.
Case: Arc'teryx ESG reputation crisis
Arc'teryx, a Canadian high-end outdoor sportswear brand, has long positioned itself as a champion of sustainability. The company proudly declares its commitment to “reducing its impact on the planet”, a message that resonates strongly with affluent, environmentally conscious outdoor enthusiasts. However, this carefully cultivated image was abruptly undermined on September 19, 2025, when Arc'teryx staged a fireworks art performance in the Himalayas—an event that drew intense public criticism for its ecological impact. The stark contrast between the company’s stated environmental values and its actual actions triggered a significant trust crisis, raising uncomfortable questions about corporate authenticity in the era of ESG branding.
The irony of this incident is amplified by Arc'teryx’s otherwise exemplary sustainability strategy. Over the years, the company has embedded sustainability into nearly every stage of its operations—from product design and material sourcing to supply chain management and brand storytelling. Unlike many fast-fashion competitors, Arc'teryx deliberately avoids frequent product launches. Instead, it emphasizes durability and technical excellence, using high-performance materials such as GORE-TEX waterproof layers and precision multi-stitching. Many of its products can last for over a decade, aligning with a growing consumer trend among outdoor enthusiasts who prefer long-term investment in premium, hard-wearing gear over disposable consumption patterns.







Figure 2.38: Arc'teryx’s aims

Source: Arc'teryx, https://arcteryx.com/us/en/explore/sustainability
Material innovation also plays a crucial role in the company’s sustainability narrative. Arc'teryx increasingly relies on bluesign®-certified fabrics and recycled fibers, thereby reducing chemical pollution during production. The gradual adoption of recycled nylon and polyester has helped lower carbon emissions across its manufacturing processes. Beyond production, Arc'teryx has developed the ReBird™ program, a repair and resale initiative that encourages customers to return worn gear for refurbishment and resale. This approach not only extends product lifecycles and minimizes waste but also fosters a sense of community among users, deepening customer loyalty through shared environmental values.









Figure 2.39: Bluesign®-certified fabrics and recycled fibers

Source: Arc'teryx
Figure 2.40: Rebird program

Source: Arc'teryx, https://arcteryx.com/us/en/explore/rebird
Arc'teryx complements these efforts with an impressive degree of climate transparency. The company publishes annual sustainability reports that disclose carbon emissions and track progress toward ambitious climate goals. It aims to reduce absolute emissions from its own operations by 65% before 2030, and it has actively pushed for net-zero transitions across its supply chain. Through these initiatives, Arc'teryx has consistently framed its brand around the values of professionalism, environmental stewardship, and reverence for nature. Its “Leave No Trace” ethos resonates with climbers and outdoor communities, while its stylish, high-performance products have gained popularity among younger consumers, transforming the brand into both a cultural symbol and a lifestyle statement.
Against this backdrop, the decision to host a pyrotechnic art event in the fragile ecosystem of the Himalayas appears almost incomprehensible. In collaboration with artists, Arc'teryx staged a fireworks display in the Jiangzi Relong area of the Qinghai–Tibet Plateau, describing it as a tribute to “mountain faith” and “artistic reverence for nature.” Yet the ecological reality of the region tells a different story. Characterized by cold, arid, and oxygen-deprived conditions, the plateau’s ecosystem is extraordinarily vulnerable. Dust and chemical residues from explosions can disrupt delicate ecological chains, and restoration is often slow, if not impossible. While the event met regulatory requirements, the environmental scars it left behind were far from negligible. For loyal customers and environmental advocates, the spectacle represented not artistic innovation but a betrayal of the very values the brand had long espoused.
Figure 2.41: Fireworks in the Qinghai–Tibet Plateau

Source: Global Times, https://www.globaltimes.cn/page/202509/1344056.shtml
The crisis was further compounded by corporate governance failures at the group level. Amer Sports and Anta, Arc'teryx’s parent companies, failed to detect or intervene during the event’s planning and the subsequent public backlash. The absence of an ESG risk monitoring and rapid response system allowed a single misguided marketing decision to reverberate across the entire corporate group, damaging its ESG reputation and stakeholder trust. Ironically, the values Arc'teryx violated—environmental stewardship and respect for nature—are precisely the values most cherished by its core and future customer base.
This incident illustrates a broader and increasingly visible phenomenon: the disconnect between corporate ESG narratives and actual practices. As sustainability becomes a global buzzword and a key factor in consumer decision-making, many large companies enthusiastically promote their environmental and social initiatives to shape an image of responsible leadership. Yet this enthusiasm sometimes crosses into “greenwashing” or “diversity washing”, where firms exaggerate or fabricate their achievements to gain reputational advantages. Such performative ESG strategies may generate short-term attention, but they ultimately resemble the fireworks Arc'teryx launched in the Himalayas—spectacular, fleeting, and damaging once the smoke clears.
The Arc'teryx case presents fertile ground for critical analysis at the graduate level. Although the company consistently promotes sustainability in its product design, production, and marketing, its practices do not fully reflect these values across all dimensions. Gaps remain in certain aspects of marketing decisions and risk management, leading to behaviors that are inconsistent with its stated corporate values.
This inconsistency offers an important lesson for companies seeking to build sustainable consumption products and brands: every internal and external action must be aligned with ESG principles to prevent potential risks that could undermine overall corporate reputation. The key challenge lies in how firms can balance genuine sustainable action with strategic communication, without falling into the trap of performative ESG—where sustainability becomes more of a branding exercise than a deeply embedded corporate practice. Achieving this balance requires not only coherent messaging but also robust governance structures and operational integration of ESG values. 
From an investor’s perspective, this case raises critical questions about ESG credibility. As more firms adopt various adaptation and mitigation strategies, sustainability reporting is becoming increasingly standardized, and investor scrutiny is intensifying. This context prompts two key reflections for investors: How can they distinguish between companies that genuinely integrate ESG principles into their operations and those that merely signal compliance? And how should they respond to sudden ESG-related risks that may not be apparent from standardized disclosures but can have significant reputational and financial impacts?
Arc'teryx’s experience demonstrates that ESG practices must be embedded in every aspect of corporate behavior. Incomplete or inconsistent implementation can result in severe and sometimes irreversible damage to reputational capital. For investors, this underscores the importance of clarifying and assessing a firm’s core values and ESG commitment, beyond surface-level reporting. A more nuanced understanding of corporate ESG authenticity is essential for effective investment decision-making in an era where sustainability narratives are both widespread and strategically significant. 
Opportunities from Sustainable Public Procurement
In 2002, the United Nations World Summit on Sustainable Development introduced the concept of Sustainable Public Procurement (SPP), which calls for public sector entities to consider the economic, social, and environmental (ESG) impacts of procurement activities throughout their lifecycle when purchasing projects, goods, and services. By establishing evaluation criteria that support independent innovation, labor protection, and energy conservation, SPP leverages public procurement to guide socioeconomic development and environmental protection. With its substantial purchasing power, the public sector (government) has long been a global focus. 
In OECD member countries, government procurement accounts for an average of 13% of GDP. In East Asian countries, government procurement represents about 9.3% of GDP, while in India and Pakistan, it is close to 20%. Globally, government procurement constitutes 13% of the world’s GDP, amounting to approximately USD 13 trillion annually. Therefore, if public sectors increasingly prioritize sustainability and prefer to purchase sustainable products, it will create new growth opportunities for companies that integrate sustainability into their strategies.
In fact, an increasing number of countries have established regulatory requirements for sustainable public procurement, with most related to green procurement. According to OECD statistics, 35 out of its 38 member countries (92%) have an active national Green Public Procurement (GPP) policy or framework. Among these, 29 countries (83%) explicitly reference GPP or public procurement in their national environmental commitments and consider it essential for achieving climate objectives, indicating that GPP is widely recognized as a powerful tool for meeting endorsed climate action goals. For example, Japan incorporates its national GPP policy into its Plan for Global Warming Countermeasures and National Action Plan. Similarly, Canada views GPP as a key means to achieve its net-zero emissions target by 2050. To ensure alignment with climate action commitments, most surveyed countries regularly revise their GPP policies. Over half (22 out of 35, or 63%) have updated their GPP frameworks in the past three years to target high-impact sectors and accelerate the transition to more sustainable production patterns.
Moreover, 24 countries have established mandatory requirements for their green public procurement policies, meaning that certain procurement categories must meet environmental standards, such as prioritizing energy-efficient products or recyclable materials. Statistics show that 12 OECD member countries have set mandatory GPP requirements for furniture and textiles, as well as hygiene and cleaning products, while 10 member countries have set mandatory requirements for paper and printing services, and IT equipment products. The figure below lists the most common categories with mandatory requirements.


Figure 2.42: Responses to 3 different questions

Source: OECD (2022), Survey on Green Public Procurement
Figure 2.43: Responses to the questions “Does the national policy or strategic framework include a mandatory requirement to use GPP in specific instances?”

Source: OECD (2022), Survey on Green Public Procurement
Let’s examine the specific practices of some countries for a clearer understanding. For example, starting in 2025, the Canadian government requires that 100% of new light-duty fleet vehicle purchases be zero-emission vehicles (ZEVs), with the goal of achieving a 100% ZEV light-duty fleet by 2030. This is supported by a series of green procurement initiatives, including clean technologies, environmental products, and services, to drive the transition to a net-zero and circular economy. The Italian government has developed a set of Minimum Environmental Criteria as the core foundation of its green procurement policy. By setting mandatory environmental requirements for various stages of the procurement process—including supplier selection, technical specifications, contract terms, and evaluation criteria—this framework helps government agencies identify the most environmentally friendly solutions. These Minimum Environmental Criteria apply to all public contracts, regardless of contract value, covering all government levels from central to local. This stringent mandatory policy not only effectively reduces the environmental impact of government procurement but also promotes the development of sustainable “circular” production and consumption systems. To date, these environmental criteria cover the following 20 procurement categories.
Table 2.19: Minimum environmental criteria in Italy
Interior furniture
Lighting, heating/cooling for buildings
Street furniture
Industrial washing and rental of textiles and mattresses
Incontinence aids
Municipal waste and street sweeping
Work shoes and leather accessories
