Public lighting (service)
Public green spaces

Turning our focus to Asia, Japan’s Green Procurement Law establishes the legal foundation for government green procurement through a legislative framework. It mandates that national institutions, independent administrative corporations, and local public entities develop and implement detailed green procurement plans annually, prioritizing sustainable products that meet stringent environmental standards. For example, copy paper must be 100% recycled with a whiteness of ≤70%, office furniture must use recyclable materials, and vehicles should prioritize low-emission or electric models. The policy covers over 20 product categories, including paper, office equipment, construction materials, vehicles, and newly added items like portable generators and emergency food. Formulated and regularly updated by the Ministry of the Environment, the policy has recently incorporated greenhouse gas emission standards.
China has increasingly implemented robust policy guidelines for green procurement and established strict quantitative indicators to ensure their enforcement. In 2022, China’s government procurement reached 3,499.31 billion RMB, accounting for 9.4% of national fiscal expenditure and 2.89% of that year’s GDP. Of this, environmentally friendly and energy- or water-saving products accounted for over 85% of the procurement scale for similar products. In 2022, the central government further integrated green procurement standards into government procurement projects for hospitals, schools, office buildings, mixed-use complexes, exhibition halls, convention centers, sports facilities, and affordable housing, launching pilot programs in 48 municipal administrative units. Various provinces and cities have also set quantitative green procurement targets. For example, Henan Province mandates that green products account for over 30% of government procurement by 2025. Hebei Province has incorporated the proportion of government green procurement into its “Ecological Province Construction Indicator System”, requiring an indicator value of at least 93%. Beijing’s Chaoyang District stipulates that the proportion of government green procurement must not fall below 80%.
Linking public procurement to climate goals raises further questions: How might mandatory green procurement requirements reshape competitive dynamics among firms? How should companies adjust product design and supply chains to comply with these standards? For small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs), green procurement presents a dual challenge: while the transition may impose higher compliance and technology costs, it also creates opportunities to differentiate through niche innovation and to gain access to public segments that reward sustainable practices. For multinational corporations, regional variations in green procurement policies imply the need for flexible strategic planning. Navigating these differences requires firms to harmonize global sustainability standards with local compliance requirements, balancing efficiency with adaptability. 
At a broader level, government-led green public procurement serves not only to reduce the environmental footprint of public spending but also to exert a powerful market-shaping effect. By setting higher thresholds for sustainability, governments effectively push firms to develop low-carbon, energy-efficient, and recyclable products, while also demanding greater supply chain transparency. Companies with a green competitive edge are therefore better positioned to capture this expanding public sector demand. Moreover, the “demonstration effect” of public procurement can enhance corporate credibility across the wider marketplace, signaling to private consumers and investors alike that a firm’s sustainability claims are both credible and scalable.
How a company’s sustainability contributes to its risk mitigation
Investments in sustainability not only impact a company’s profitability but also reduce its operating risks, helping to prevent financial distress, delisting, or bankruptcy. In terms of earnings risk or operating risk, Arif et al. (2024) use the level of analysts' earnings forecast dispersion as a measure of future earnings risk, and find that ESG disclosures have a significant negative impact on a firm's future earnings risk. Additionally, disaggregated analysis of ESG disclosures shows that the impact of the social dimension is economically more pronounced than the environmental and governance disclosures, indicating that the disclosure of social issues carries more value than the other dimensions of ESG disclosures in minimising the dispersion of future earnings risk for a firm. This highlight that transparent information environment could serve as a tool to minimise the agency problem arising from the information asymmetry between corporate managers and shareholders concerning firms' future earnings. examined US listed companies, assessing the impact of ESG disclosures on future risk from both accounting-based and market-based perspectives. The study measured accounting-based earnings risk using the volatility of Return on Assets (ROA), defined as the standard deviation of ROA over five-year overlapping windows. For market-based risk, it used the overall volatility of stock returns (calculated as the standard deviation of monthly stock returns) and the idiosyncratic volatility of stock returns (derived as the annualized standard deviation of residuals from the Fama-French three-factor model). The findings confirmed that ESG disclosures significantly reduce a company’s operating risk.
Besides, Jamil and Khan (2024)’s study of Pakistani listed companies measured downside operating risk by whether a firm’s annual EBITDA fell below the industry average, finding that sustainability practices mitigate such risks.

In terms of financial risk, utilize data from Chinese A-share listed companies, indicate that ESG rating disparities significantly increase corporate risk by exacerbating financing constraint discrepancies in ESG ratings significantly increase corporate risk, particularly in non-state-owned enterprises and heavily polluting industries, while having no significant impact on state-owned enterprises. Discrepancies in governance ratings exert the greatest impact on corporate risk, underscoring the critical role of corporate governance. Financing constraints further exacerbate the impact of rating discrepancies on corporate risk. These results provide new insights into enhancing the ESG rating system and mitigating corporate risk, offering a foundation for relevant policy-making.

Additional studies on global listed companies further support these findings. Whether measuring credit risk through credit default swap spreads(Kölbel et al., 2017, Wachtel et al., 2023) or assessing overall financial health with the Altman Z-score(Choi et al., 2024; Habermann and Fischer, 2023,), researchers found that improved sustainability performance—or a reduction in sustainability-related reputation risk—substantially mitigates the risk of financial distress.
The series of studies has thoroughly explored the mechanisms through which ESG practices mitigate various corporate risks. I’ve synthesized these findings into three key points:
Internal Risk Control Perspective: Higher levels of ESG disclosure compel management to better recognize ESG-related risks facing the firm, prompting the adoption of more robust risk management measures to curb corporate misconduct. For instance, research shows that companies subject to mine safety disclosure regulations are more likely to close hazardous facilities (Christensen et al., 2017).
External Oversight Perspective: ESG disclosures reduce information asymmetry between management, investors, and other stakeholders regarding business activities. This enables shareholders and stakeholders to more effectively monitor and govern corporate behavior, increasing market and societal pressure on firms with poor ESG performance. Such pressure may lead to penalties for companies engaging in high-ESG-risk projects, such as investor divestment or consumer boycotts of the firm’s products.
Internal and External Trust Mechanism: High levels of ESG disclosure enhance a company’s social capital, such as customer loyalty, and foster enforceable mutual trust among management, shareholders, and other stakeholders. This collective trust helps curb excessive risk-taking, lowers the cost of equity capital, and encourages shareholders to support low-risk projects that ensure sustainable development(Godfrey, 2005). Meanwhile, management benefits from more stable career prospects by avoiding high-risk initiatives(Hoi et al., 2019).
Notably, Fafaliou et al. (2022) leveraged data on bankruptcy and delisting among US listed companies, employing Kaplan-Meier Survival Estimates and Nelson-Aalen Cumulative Hazard Estimates to assess the relationship between ESG reputation risk and market longevity. The results, as illustrated in the accompanying figure, reveal significant insights. The Kaplan-Meier Survival Estimates generate survival curves to compare the survival probabilities of different groups (e.g., high-ESG-risk versus low-ESG-risk firms) over time. The analysis shows that firms with high ESG risk experience a markedly faster decline in survival probability as time progresses. Similarly, the Nelson-Aalen Cumulative Hazard Estimates capture the cumulative risk of market exit at specific time points, indicating that high-ESG-risk firms accumulate exit risk at a faster rate over time. These findings confirm that enhanced corporate sustainability contributes to extending market longevity.




Figure 2.44: Survival probability and ESG risks

Source: Fafaliou et al. (2022), https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0148296322004337
Figure 2.45: Exit risks and ESG risks

Source: Fafaliou et al. (2022), https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0148296322004337
However, some studies have reached the opposite conclusion. Hsiao et al. (2022), using the Z-score as a measure of financial health and analyzing Chinese listed companies between 2019 and the third quarter of 2021, observed that during the pandemic, firms with stronger ESG performance often exhibited weaker overall financial conditions. This counterintuitive result can be explained through the lens of overinvestment theory. From 2019 to 2022, the global economy faced structural recessionary pressures triggered by the COVID-19 crisis. Firms encountered shrinking revenues, heightened uncertainty, and severe constraints on financial resources. In this environment, companies that continued to allocate substantial funds to ESG initiatives faced a trade-off between long-term sustainability goals and short-term survival. During an acute economic downturn, ESG spending may crowd out the resources needed to stabilize core operations. 
These findings raise a critical question: Are ESG commitments universally value-enhancing, or are they highly context-dependent—particularly during periods of macroeconomic stress? When recessionary pressures or financial constraints dominate, firms may face a difficult dilemma: should they sustain high levels of ESG spending to protect long-term resilience, or scale back in order to preserve liquidity and safeguard core operations? 
The debate also has a geographic dimension. As noted earlier, the relationship between ESG performance and firm profitability differs across developed and developing markets. It is therefore plausible that the same divergence applies to operational and financial risks. In advanced economies with mature capital markets, ESG commitments may be more readily rewarded by investors and supported by policy incentives. In contrast, in developing economies where financial resources are scarcer and short-term survival pressures loom larger, aggressive ESG spending may strain balance sheets and amplify risk exposure.
From an investor’s perspective, ESG data remains a valuable lens for risk assessment—but the challenge lies in interpretation. Rather than assuming that higher ESG scores are universally desirable, investors must weigh the trade-off between long-term resilience and short-term fragility. A more nuanced strategy would be to evaluate whether a firm’s ESG initiatives are strategically aligned with its financial capacity, sectoral dynamics, and macroeconomic environment. Ultimately, the key lies not only in measuring ESG outcomes, but in asking whether firms are pursuing sustainability at a pace that is financially sustainable. 

Sustainable supply chain and risk mitigation 
Sustainable supply chain management involves integrating environmental and social considerations into the sourcing, production, and distribution of goods and services. Consider this example: if a company plans to build a wind power plant, sustainable supply chain issues might include whether the plant’s location disrupts local biodiversity, whether the wind turbine tower supplier exploits labor during manufacturing, or whether the transportation of generator blades relies on high-carbon-emission vehicle fleets. Failure to monitor and address these environmental and social sustainability issues can lead to reputational damage, litigation risks, and negative impacts on a company’s financial statements. 
Data from EY shows that supply chains account for 50% to 70% of an enterprise’s operating costs (including material sourcing, manufacturing, warehousing, and transportation) and more than 90% of an organization’s greenhouse gas emissions. To examine trends and the current state of the marketplace, EY teams conducted a survey on sustainable supply chains at 525 large corporations (over US$500 million in revenue) across the Western Hemisphere. Responses were received from senior supply chain executives in Argentina, Brazil, Canada, Mexico, and the United States, representing sectors including retail, consumer packaged goods, health providers, life sciences, government, technology, energy, manufacturing, mobility, and food and agriculture.
The first insightful finding is the motivation behind these large companies’ sustainable supply chain initiatives. Cost savings are by far the primary motivator for supply chain executives pursuing sustainability efforts (61%). Nearly 90% of these companies plan to or have already taken steps to conserve or reduce water in operations, use renewable energy for manufacturing, reduce material waste in production processes, ensure suppliers source sustainable materials, and use alternative fuels for vehicle fleets. Moreover, 51% of companies adopt sustainable supply chain practices to comply with regulatory requirements, while 41% do so due to pressure from partners or suppliers.


Figure 2.46: What are your top motivators for improving supply chain sustainability

Source: EY
Figure 2.47: Sustainability initiatives are prioritized by resource consumption, renewables, and supplier diversity

Source: EY
Besides, achieving net-zero commitments is also driving companies to adopt sustainable supply chain practices. The survey shows that 30% of companies have committed to achieving net-zero emissions in their supply chains by 2030, while 11% and 22% of companies have pledged to reach supply chain net-zero by 2035 and 2040, respectively. 
Figure 2.48: More than one-quarter of supply chains commit to becoming net zero by 2030

Source: EY
EY identified nearly 400 trailblazers in the sample with mature sustainable supply chain management approaches and asked whether they had realized benefits from implementing these practices. Among them, 49% of trailblazers reported improved employee quality of life, while nearly 40% noted increased customer loyalty and lower employee turnover rates due to sustainable supply chain practices. The vast majority of companies expect that sustainable supply chain practices will enhance efficiency and productivity in the short term (1-3 years) or long term (3-5 years), thereby increasing revenue and improving profitability.
The development of sustainable supply chains requires firms to proactively identify and cultivate green suppliers, establish structured sustainability programs, and implement rigorous auditing and monitoring mechanisms. From the perspective of different stakeholders, the spillover effects across stakeholders are equally important.
For customers, transparent and responsible supply chains enhance trust, reinforce brand loyalty, and justify a willingness to pay a premium. For employees, working within a company committed to ethical and sustainable sourcing fosters pride, strengthens engagement, and reduces turnover, while minimizing compliance and reputational risks linked to labor and human rights violations.
Figure 2.49: Sustainable supply chain benefits

Source: EY
Operationally, well-designed green supply chains can lower costs, improve efficiency, and enhance product quality. Firms that take the lead in achieving net-zero supply chain targets and aligning with international standards are better positioned to secure high-value partnerships, attract green financing, and strengthen resilience against disruptions such as resource shortages, geopolitical tensions, or regulatory shifts. 
Taken together, robust supply chain sustainability reduces operational and financial risks, builds stronger loyalty among customers and employees, and supports long-term profitability and market value. For investors, it acts as a clear signal of both effective governance and resilience in navigating environmental and social shocks, positioning the firm as a credible and forward-looking player in a rapidly evolving global economy.
The above discussion also raises further questions: Why is cost savings often the primary driver for companies to pursue sustainable supply chains, rather than a genuine sense of social responsibility? In the process of greening supply chains, which stages are most commonly overlooked—procurement, transportation, or supplier auditing?
Another important dimension is finance. How can firms leverage green supply chain transformation to attract sustainable financing instruments such as sustainability-linked loans or green bonds? Finally, in an era of global geopolitical tensions and frequent supply chain disruptions, can sustainable supply chains strengthen corporate resilience, not only by reducing environmental and social risks but also by enhancing adaptability in uncertain markets?
What kind of companies exhibit better sustainable performance
Through the empirical research and industry evidence discussed earlier, we can fully understand the importance of companies integrating sustainability into their strategic and operational decisions to reduce capital costs and operational risks while seizing the opportunities presented by today’s consumer and government sustainable purchasing trends. A company’s investment in sustainability also determines the extent to which it can capitalize on collaboration opportunities with other business partners in the supply chain. In this section, we aim to explore what types of companies exhibit stronger sustainability performance and identify the factors influencing their ESG performance, providing key insights for companies aspiring to make progress in ESG practices.
To address the question of “what types of companies exhibit better sustainability performance”, FTSE Russell (now part of the LSEG Group alongside LSEG, with its ESG scores primarily used for index construction and sustainable portfolio management) conducted a study in 2019. The study utilized its ESG score database, covering 4,684 share listings across 50 stock exchanges worldwide, to investigate whether there were significant differences in ESG performance among listed companies of varying sizes, industries, and countries. 
The researchers first explored the size factor, measuring it by market capitalization and then taking the natural logarithm of this metric. They performed a Pearson correlation regression on the companies’ ESG score data (ranging from 0 to 5). The results revealed a significant positive correlation between company size and ESG scores, with an R-squared value of 0.15, indicating that 15% of the variation in ESG scores among companies can be explained by size. This finding aligns with academic research by Baldini et al. (2018), whose empirical results showed that company size significantly and positively impacts Bloomberg ESG disclosure scores. The authors explained that larger firms face greater public scrutiny and pressure, leading to higher ESG disclosure to manage societal expectations, maintain legitimacy, and uphold a socially acceptable image.
Figure 2.50: Company size and ESG scores

Source：LSEG
To measure the influence of sectors on ESG scores, researchers used the ICB classification at level 3, which distinguishes 39 different sectors. The study calculated the average ESG score for each sector and divided it by the overall average ESG score of all sample companies to determine which industries performed above average in sustainability. For example, the Media industry had a value of 1.1, indicating its ESG score was 10% higher than the overall industry average. The results are shown in the figure below. The study found that the top-performing industries in sustainability were Life Insurance, Fixed Line Telecommunications, and Nonlife Insurance. Industries around the average included Automobiles & Parts, Software & Computer Services, and Chemicals, while the lowest-performing industries were Real Estate Investment & Services, Construction & Materials, and Leisure Goods. 
Figure 2.51: Sectors and ESG scores

Source：LSEG
Moreover, the authors compared and visualized the ESG score distribution between the top-performing industry, Life Insurance, and the lowest-performing industry, Real Estate Investment & Services (as shown in the figure below). The results showed that over 40% of Life Insurance companies scored above 3.4, while the majority of Real Estate Investment & Services companies scored below 1.8, highlighting significant differences in the emphasis on sustainability across industries.

Figure 2.52: ESG scores in the top-performing industry

Source：LSEG
The FTSE Russell study also analyzed sector ESG differences to examine variations in sustainability performance among listed companies from different countries. As shown in the figure below, we can observe that listed companies in EU countries significantly outperform the average in sustainability, with Finland, the Netherlands, France, and Italy having the best-performing companies. In contrast, companies with poorer sustainability performance are primarily from Asian countries, particularly in West Asia, including the UAE, Qatar, and Saudi Arabia.
Figure 2.53: Variations of sustainability performance in different countries

Source：LSEG
Comparing Finland and Saudi Arabia, we can clearly see a significant difference in their sustainability performance. All listed companies in Finland score above 2.8, with nearly 14% achieving an excellent range of 3.8–4 points. In contrast, ESG scores for listed companies in Saudi Arabia are generally below 2.8, with nearly 40% scoring below 1 point.
Figure 2.54: Sustainability performance in Filand and Saudi Arabia

Source：LSEG
Next, we will draw on academic research to explore the factors that may influence corporate sustainability performance. Baldini et al. (2018) investigated how national-level characteristics, such as political systems (including the strength of legal frameworks and corruption levels), labor systems (such as labor protection levels and unemployment rates), and cultural systems (including degrees of individualism and power distance), affect corporate ESG disclosure scores. The results showed that these factors have significant but heterogeneous impacts: some promote ESG disclosure scores (positive relationship), while others reduce them (negative relationship), with varying effects on each ESG pillar (environmental, social, and governance). The study also examined firm-level characteristics, such as analyst coverage, cross-listing, leverage, and size, finding that these factors have significantly positive and mostly homogeneous impacts on ESG disclosure. The authors primarily used institutional theory and legitimacy theory to interpret the regression results, suggesting that different institutional environments incentive or shape corporate sustainability disclosure behaviors to varying degrees and directions. Companies also tend to enhance their legitimacy through ESG disclosure to maintain a positive social reputation and secure resource support from governments and society in their regions. The table below provides a detailed breakdown of the influence mechanisms for each factor.
Table 2.20: National characteristics and ESG scores
Level
Category
Factor
Direction
Interpretation
Country
Policy system
Legal Framework
Significantly negative for ESG and environmental disclosure
In countries with a stronger legal framework (i.e., more formal rules and constraints), companies face less pressure to disclose ESG information beyond what is legally mandated. A robust legal system provides clear regulations, reducing the incentive for firms to voluntarily disclose additional non-financial information


Corruption
Significantly negative for ESG and all pillars
Higher levels of corruption in a country lead to lower ESG disclosure because firms in such environments are more likely to engage in unethical practices. These firms are reluctant to reveal such behaviors through transparent disclosure, as it could expose them to scrutiny or penalties

Labor system
Labor Protection
Significantly positive for social and governance disclosure
Stronger labor protection, measured by union density, increases social and governance disclosure because labor unions exert pressure on firms to address issues relevant to employees, such as workplace benefits, safety, and governance structures (e.g., career prospects and pension plans).


Unemployment Rate
Significantly positive for ESG and all pillars
Higher unemployment rates create competition among firms to attract skilled employees. ESG disclosure becomes a tool to signal better career prospects and corporate responsibility, appealing to a workforce that values such transparency. This positive effect is consistent across all pillars, as firms leverage comprehensive ESG disclosure to enhance their reputation and competitiveness in labor markets.

Cultural system
Social Cohesion
Significantly negative for ESG, environmental, and social pillars; significantly positive for governance pillar
In countries with high social cohesion (ie. High level of individualism, where individuals prioritize self-reliance over collective societal support), managers feel less obligated to disclose ESG, environmental, and social information, as societal pressure for such transparency is lower. Conversely, the positive effect on governance disclosure is interpreted as managers’ desire to demonstrate their authority and legitimacy in highly individualism societies, focusing disclosure efforts on governance to affirm their control and appropriateness, while releasing less information on other topics.


Equal Opportunities
Significantly negative for ESG, environmental, and social pillars; significantly positive for governance pillar
In societies where unequal distribution of power is accepted (high power distance), managers perceive less need to disclose ESG, environmental, and social information to meet stakeholder expectations. However, the positive relationship with governance disclosure reflects managers’ efforts to legitimize their leadership and power in such cultural contexts, prioritizing governance transparency over other areas.
Corporate
/
Analyst coverage
Significantly positive for ESG and all pillars

Greater analysts’ coverage increases a firm’s visibility to investors, prompting higher ESG disclosure to meet societal and stakeholder expectations. Firms use disclosure as a tool to appear socially responsible and legitimate, aligning with legitimacy theory’s premise that visibility drives transparency to secure societal acceptance.

/
Cross-listing
Significantly positive for ESG and environmental disclosure

Cross-listing increases a firm’s visibility by exposing it to multiple regulatory environments and a broader investor base. The authors suggest that this heightened scrutiny, particularly regarding environmental regulations (which vary across countries), drives higher ESG and environmental disclosure.

/
Leverage
Significantly positive for ESG and all pillars

Higher leverage increases a firm’s visibility to creditors and investors, who may demand greater transparency. The authors argue that firms respond by enhancing ESG disclosure across all pillars to legitimize their operations and reassure stakeholders of their stability and ethical conduct.

/
Size
Significantly positive for ESG and all pillars

Larger firms face greater public scrutiny and visibility, leading to higher ESG disclosure to manage societal expectations and maintain legitimacy.


In addition to the factors mentioned above, Tufano et al. (2024) argue that a company’s ownership structure significantly influences its sustainability performance. By analyzing 26,481 firm-year observations from 3,083 listed companies across 62 countries between 2002 and 2019, the study found that companies with founding families or other individual investors as material owners (i.e., where founding family members or individual investors are among the top ten shareholders) perform worse in ESG compared to firms with only institutional owners (which serve as the baseline category). This suggests that family or individual investors may prioritize short-term financial returns over long-term ESG goals. However, when family members serve as CEOs, the company’s ESG performance is significantly better than that of other firms, with an even stronger positive effect when descendants serve as CEOs (the coefficient for descendant CEOs is approximately double that of founding CEOs). This indicates that family involvement in management roles may drive longer-term, stakeholder-focused strategies, and generational succession in family firms may foster a stronger ESG orientation. Companies with non-family managers or government entities as major shareholders generally perform better in ESG, likely because government owners are driven by public policy or societal expectations to support environmental and social goals, while non-family managers may use ESG investments to reduce conflicts with stakeholders, thereby enhancing corporate reputation and operational stability. Companies with other listed firms as major shareholders tend to have poorer ESG performance, as these shareholder firms may prioritize short-term financial synergies or market competition over sustainable goals.
The above research findings deepen our understanding of the factors shaping corporate sustainability performance. They also provide useful guidance for firms seeking to adjust their strategies, optimize ownership structures, and strengthen ESG practices in order to secure a competitive edge within their industries and broader macroeconomic environments.
These insights naturally raise further questions. For small and medium-sized enterprises, how can ESG performance be improved despite resource constraints? Are there low-cost strategies—such as partnerships, technology adoption, or targeted disclosure—that can help them balance limited capacity with growing stakeholder expectations?
Industry-level differences are also noteworthy. Why do sectors like insurance and telecommunications typically score higher on ESG, while real estate and construction lag behind? Could this be due to differences in regulatory intensity, environmental externalities, or the visibility of social impacts?
Another dimension lies in the role of institutions and culture. Do institutional and cultural values exert a long-term influence on corporate ESG practices, or can targeted policy interventions quickly shift behavior? Similarly, ownership structure deserves closer scrutiny. Empirical evidence often shows that family-controlled firms underperform in ESG, perhaps due to a focus on preserving wealth or resisting external pressures. Yet paradoxically, when family members hold CEO positions, ESG outcomes sometimes improve, possibly reflecting a stronger sense of responsibility, reputation concerns, or long-term orientation.

Chapter 3
Sustainable Investing 
In the last chapter, we discussed that companies need to improve their sustainability performance to achieve lower capital costs and to capitalize on the current trend of sustainable purchasing by consumers, governments, and supply chain partners. This helps them maintain a competitive edge in the industry and promote long-term profitability. However, investment is the foundation of all economic activities in this world, and the mindset of investors determines the flow of capital. In the primary market, we can invest in a traditional power plant that uses fossil fuels. Although this may pollute the air, in some countries with less stringent environmental regulations, it might provide investors with a stable cash flow. Alternatively, we can invest in a wind power plant in the European Union, where the net-zero goal may give renewable energy generation a competitive advantage. In the secondary market, if investors use sustainable investment strategies and only buy stocks of companies with high ESG ratings in each industry, it may help avoid companies that could bring reputation risks, leading to more stable investment returns. The stock prices of those invested companies may also rise because of these sustainable investors. We can see that sustainable investment does indeed make the world a better place. As we mentioned in Chapter 1, according to GSIA, the amount of sustainable investment assets under management has increased from 13 trillion US dollars in 2012 to over 30 trillion in 2022. Bloomberg predicts that this figure will reach 40 trillion by 2030. From the trend, sustainable investment is indeed becoming popular. But we might wonder what advantages sustainable investment has over traditional investment methods, or what exactly drives investors to engage in sustainable investment. Also, what specific investment strategies does sustainable investment include? This section will focus on these specific questions and refer to some academic research findings and industry surveys, hoping to give everyone a clear understanding.
The motivation of sustainable investing

Individual investors
Let's start with a notable phenomenon in the fund market. In 2016, Morningstar, a leading financial research website, first released a fund sustainability rating. Over 20,000 mutual funds were ranked on a percentile basis and given a globe rating (1-5) based on their holdings. The worst 10% of funds were rated one globe (low sustainability), while the best 10% were rated five globes (high sustainability). Hartzmark and Sussman (2019)'s research found that before the release of the mutual funds' sustainability ratings (indicated by the vertical dashed line in the figure below), the accumulated flows of these funds, which inherently had different levels of sustainability, did not show a clear trend. However, in the 11 months following the rating release, funds labeled as the most sustainable (5-globe) experienced a net inflow of over 24 billion US dollars, equivalent to 4% of their fund size. In contrast, funds rated as the least sustainable (1-globe) saw a net outflow of over 12 billion US dollars during the same period, equivalent to 6% of their fund size. This pattern of capital flow indicates that overall, investors in the market tend to shift their funds from low-sustainability funds to high-sustainability funds, showing a positive preference for sustainability.
Figure 3.1: Cumulative fund flows in percent by sustainability rating for 9 months before and 11 months after rating publication (denoted by the dashed vertical line)

Source: Hartzmark and Sussman (2019), https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/abs/10.1111/jofi.12841
The author then conducted a survey among MBA students and participants on Amazon Mechanical Turk, collecting their expected returns, risk perceptions, and investment decision data for funds with different sustainability ratings (star ratings). The results showed that participants expected significantly higher returns and significantly lower risks for 5-globe funds compared to 1-globe funds. This "high return, low risk" expectation contradicts the risk-return trade-off in financial theory, suggesting that such judgments might be driven by emotions rather than rational analysis. The author further analyzed the actual return data of funds in the 11 months following the rating release and found that the returns of high-sustainability funds (five stars) were not significantly better than those of low-sustainability funds (one star). This indicates that investors' optimistic expectations lack empirical support, proving the existence of irrational beliefs.
Moreover, the author conducted an experiment among participants, asking them to allocate capital among different types of funds and controlling for their expectations of future fund performance and risk in a regression analysis. The results showed that even after controlling for risk and return expectations, participants still tended to allocate more funds to high-sustainability (five-star) funds and reduce their investments in low-sustainability (one-star) funds. For MBA students, five-star funds received about 48 US dollars more in allocation than one-star funds, while for participants on Amazon Mechanical Turk, this figure was 71 US dollars. The author speculated that participants might choose high-rated funds due to non-monetary motives such as supporting environmental protection or social justice (altruism), the psychological satisfaction of investing sustainably (warm-glow effect), or social expectations (social norms). These motives are unrelated to financial returns but significantly influence investment decisions. 
The second notable driving factor was social signaling, which refers to investors signaling their concern for social or environmental issues by holding SRI funds to enhance their social image or reputation. The study captured the degree of social signaling through the survey question "I often talk about investments with others" (on a scale from 1 "strongly disagree" to 7 "strongly agree"). The empirical results showed that investors who frequently discussed investments (i.e., those with stronger "signals") were more likely to hold SRI funds to shape a positive social image (with a regression coefficient of 0.0228). Another surprising finding of the study was that nearly half of the SRI investors in the sample believed that SRI funds might underperform traditional funds and were willing to pay higher management fees. This indicates that investors are willing to sacrifice some financial returns for social goals. In addition, the study found that long-term investors (0.0023), investors with larger portfolio sizes (0.039), and investors with college degrees (0.0553) were more likely to hold SRI funds. 
Figure 3.2: Drivers of holding socially responsible mutual funds

The above two well-known studies mainly focused on individual investors, and we can summarize their motives for sustainable investment as follows: 
Socially responsible investors mainly hope to express their intrinsic values regarding environmental and social issues through sustainable investment, even if such investments may yield lower returns than traditional investments. This is essentially an altruistic behavior. 
Some investors hope to enhance their external image and reputation through sustainable investment, even if they may not care about the positive impact of sustainable investment. 
There are also investors who have irrational beliefs about sustainable investment, thinking that it will bring higher returns and lower risks. 
Institutional investors
However, compared to individual investors, institutional investors or asset managers may have significantly different motivations for sustainable investment.
Edmans.et.al (2024) conducted an in-depth survey of 509 stock portfolio managers from traditional and sustainable funds to explore how sustainable investment operates in the investment practices of asset managers and to carefully examine their motives for engaging in sustainable investment. The study found that the primary driver for these asset managers to incorporate environmental and social (ES) factors into their investment decisions is financial return, rather than social or environmental impact. This finding overturns the traditional notion that sustainable investment mainly focuses on non-financial goals. Even managers of funds labeled as sustainable prioritize financial performance. The survey data showed that 77% of investors (including 66% of traditional investors and 91% of sustainable investors) frequently integrate ES performance into stock selection. However, their motivation is more about avoiding downside risk (74% of sustainable investors and 51% of traditional investors) or enhancing returns rather than solely pursuing ES impact (only 20% of traditional investors and 41% of sustainable investors focus on this goal). Unlike individual investors who are willing to sacrifice some financial returns for the impact of sustainable investment, due to their fiduciary duty, only 5% of sustainable investors and 2% of traditional investors are willing to give up more than 50 basis points of return per year. Moreover, just 27% of investors (24% of traditional investors and 30% of sustainable investors) are willing to tolerate a company sacrificing even one basis point of annual return for ES performance. This indicates that the vast majority of investors (whether focused on sustainable investment or not) value financial returns far more than ES goals.
In addition to considerations of risk and return, the study pointed out that ES constraints significantly influence asset managers' stock selection, voting, and engagement decisions. The data showed that 72% of investors (62% of traditional investors and 85% of sustainable investors) reported that ES constraints led them to make choices different from their original intentions. These constraints include mandate constraints (some sustainable funds are required to avoid investing in specific industries, such as fossil fuels), company-level policies (some firms may establish universal ES standards that require traditional funds to consider in their investment decisions), client preferences (as seen in the earlier example of individual investors' preference for sustainable investment), and concerns about fund sustainability ratings or reputation.
From the above discussion, it is clear that sustainable investing is driven by both non-monetary and financial motives, and that it carries different meanings for different players. This raises several important questions. Why did flows shift sharply after labels appeared? Do investors believe “high return, low risk”? Is this rational? Does “green investing for image” strengthen or undermine the credibility of ESG as a whole? Considering fiduciary duty, can asset managers ever truly place ESG impact ahead of financial returns? Finally, will individual motives rooted in values eventually converge with institutional motives focused on returns, or will this divide persist?
Sustainable investing strategies
Individual investors hope to make a positive impact on the environment and society through sustainable investment, or to enhance their personal reputation. Asset managers, on the other hand, view sustainable factors as tools to avoid downside risk and improve investment performance. The task of this section is to explore the widely used sustainable investment strategies by investors and the respective amounts of assets under management (AUM) based on these strategies in recent years.
You might have heard many similar terms for investment strategies, such as Responsible Investing, Socially Responsible Investing, Sustainable Investing, and ESG Investing. It doesn't make much sense to distinguish these phrases separately because although their definitions might be slightly different in some textbooks, their essential meanings overlap and intersect with each other. Overall, sustainable investing generally includes any investment strategies in the primary or secondary markets that integrate environmental, social, and governance factors into the investment decision-making process, aiming to achieve long-term financial returns while promoting the sustainable development of the economy, society, and environment. In the following text, we will use the term "sustainable investing" consistently.
Under the broad umbrella of sustainable investing, asset owners and managers have developed several distinct investment strategies and reached a basic consensus on their specific meanings. Let’s start by introducing the Global Sustainable Investment Alliance (GSIA). Founded in 2010, the GSIA is responsible for aggregating responsible investment market data from its members, such as the European Sustainable Investment Forum, Japan Sustainable Investment Forum, and China Sustainable Investment Forum. Its goal is to analyze the global sustainable investment market and the evolution of ESG trends. In particular, the GSIA has detailed the different types of mainstream sustainable investment strategies in its reports. Specifically, these strategies are: Negative Screening (or Worst-in-Class) & Exclusion Policy; Norms-based screening; Positive (or best-in-class) screening; ESG Integration; Thematic investing; Active ownership (voting policy & shareholder engagement); Impact investing. Next, I will explain the specific meanings of these sustainable investment strategies one by one and use real-world examples to help everyone fully understand how these strategies are applied in actual investment environments. 
Negative screening
Negative screening is an investment strategy that removes certain investments from the investable universe based on specific criteria. This method is often used to exclude companies, sectors, or countries that fail to meet certain ESG standards. When applied to companies, it is also known as a worst-in-class exclusion strategy. For example, investors may exclude companies with the lowest ESG ratings, such as those rated CCC. This approach can also involve sector or sub-industry exclusions, such as coal and consumable fuels, fossil fuel production, conventional weapons, and civilian firearms. Besides, it may exclude certain activities that are inconsistent with the investor’s values, such as pornography, tobacco, alcohol, gambling, and genetically modified organisms。In most cases, investors will define an exclusion list of individual issuers to implement this strategy.
Norms-based screening
Norm-based investing is a sustainable investment strategy that screens investments against minimum standards of business practice based on widely recognized international norms. These norms are typically established by international organizations such as the UN Global Compact, the International Labour Organization (ILO), and the OECD. This approach excludes companies that have been called into question for violating international standards and norms on social or environmental issues. For example, companies that violate human rights, engage in corruption, or fail to meet labor standards set by the ILO may be excluded from investment portfolios. In Europe, some of the top exclusion criteria based on norms include controversial weapons (Ottawa and Oslo treaties), tobacco, all weapons, gambling, pornography, nuclear energy, alcohol, genetically modified organisms, and animal testing. This strategy helps investors align their portfolios with broader ethical and sustainability goals while avoiding companies that do not meet minimum standards of business conduct.
Negative screening and norm-based screening are often used together by investors because both involve exclusionary screening, which means excluding assets that have the worst sustainability performance, do not align with the investor's sustainable investment philosophy, or fail to meet the minimum global business practice standards. 
Active ownership
Active ownership seeks to influence corporate behavior and promote improvements in environmental, social, and governance practices through shareholder engagement and voting. This is done through direct communication with company management or boards, submitting or co-filing shareholder proposals, and proxy voting in line with ESG guidelines. For example, investors might engage with target companies on specific topics such as climate target-setting, implementation of emissions reduction strategies, employee pay ratios, or avoiding animal testing. They may also file shareholder resolutions or even participate in public litigation. If a company refuses to engage or fails to change its unsustainable practices—such as continuing to invest in coal-fired power plants—investors may divest. This strategy not only helps improve a company’s ESG performance but also creates long-term value for investors.
Case: Norwegian GPFG
A prime example of using these two exclusionary screening methods is the Norwegian Government Global Pension Fund, one of the world's largest sovereign wealth funds, which manages Norway's oil revenues to preserve long-term wealth. As of 2024, its market value is approximately 15.76 trillion Norwegian kroner (about 1.5 trillion US dollars). These assets are held by the Norwegian Ministry of Finance and managed by Norges Bank Investment Management (the central bank of Norway). The Ministry of Finance has established a set of ethical guidelines to guide the observation and exclusion decisions of the Government Pension Fund Global (GPFG). These guidelines specify two types of exclusion criteria based on company products or conduct. The Ministry has also set up an independent Ethics Council to conduct ethical assessments of investee companies and make recommendations to the Executive Board of Norges Bank. The Executive Board ultimately decides whether to exclude a company from the fund, place it on a watchlist, or follow up through active ownership. 
Under the product-based exclusions criteria, the fund is required to prohibit investment in the following types of companies: 
Weapon-related companies: Investments are banned in companies that directly or through controlled entities produce weapons that violate fundamental humanitarian principles, such as nuclear weapons, cluster munitions, or anti-personnel landmines; or companies that sell weapons or military materials to certain specific countries. 
Tobacco and recreational cannabis companies: Investments are prohibited in companies that produce tobacco or cannabis for recreational use, as their products have negative impacts on health or society. 
Mining companies with 30% or more of their revenue from coal, or those that produce more than 20 million tons of thermal coal annually. 
Power companies with 30% or more of their revenue from coal-fired power generation, or those with a coal-fired power generation capacity of more than 10,000 megawatts. 
As of the end of 2024, a total of 104 companies have been excluded from the fund's portfolio due to the production of specific weapons, tobacco, recreational cannabis, coal, or involvement in coal-fired power generation. 
Under the conduct-based exclusion criteria, companies may also be excluded if there is an unacceptable risk of them contributing to or being responsible for particularly serious violations of fundamental ethical norms. The Executive Board of Norges Bank bases its decisions on an assessment of the likelihood of future violations, the severity and extent of the violations, and the connection between the violations and the company in which the fund is invested. As of the end of 2024, a total of 67 companies were excluded due to their conduct.
	Companies may be excluded or placed under observation if there is an unacceptable risk that the company contributes to or is responsible for:
Table 3.1: Unacceptable risks
Environmental
severe environmental damage

acts or omissions that on an aggregate company level lead to unacceptable greenhouse gas emissions
Social
serious or systematic human rights violations

serious violations of individual's rights in situations of war or conflict

the sale of weapons to states engaged in armed conflict that use the weapons in ways that constitute serious and systematic violations of the international rules on the conduct of hostilities

the sale of weapons or military materiel to states that are subject to investment restrictions on government bonds

other particularly serious violations of fundamental ethical norms
Governmental
other particularly serious violations of fundamental ethical norms

Considering the active ownership of Norges Bank, the bank maintains regular contact with the companies it invests in, with the dual aim of providing support and communicating its views as an investor. They discuss a wide range of topics that could impact the fund's long-term value and follow up with companies over several years. Through engagement, the bank seeks to understand three key aspects: the company's business model, how the company manages sustainability-related risks, and how it captures sustainability-related opportunities.
When the bank wants to discuss specific issues with companies, their dialogues take a targeted form, following up on governance and strategy through several approaches:
Strategic Board Dialogues: The bank meets with the boards of the largest companies in its portfolio to enhance its understanding of the company and improve investment performance.
Net Zero Dialogues: The bank engages with companies that have the largest emissions, supporting them on their journey to net zero.
Thematic Dialogues: The bank raises specific sustainability issues with companies, prioritizing topics that could affect their ability to create long-term value and following them up over a specific period.
Incident-Based Dialogues: The bank follows up on unwanted incidents that could indicate weak corporate governance or poor management of environmental and social risks.
Ethical Dialogues: These are decided by Norges Bank's Executive Board following recommendations from the independent Council on Ethics.
From the above discussion, we see that the GPFG has excluded industries such as tobacco, cannabis, and coal. While this reduces exposure to controversial sectors, it may also limit potential returns. This raises a key question: should sovereign wealth funds prioritize maximizing national wealth, or should they bear ethical responsibility? Do different countries place emphasis on different objectives? 
We also noted in the previous chapter the case of Deutsche Bank’s engagement with high–carbon-intensity companies in its loan portfolio as part of its medium- and long-term decarbonization goals. Compared with Norges Bank’s approach, there is an interesting contrast: Deutsche Bank applies pressure through creditor–borrower relationships, whereas the GPFG, as a global investor, exercises influence through shareholder rights. Which approach is more effective in driving ESG outcomes—and why?
Positive screening
Positive screening is the complete opposite of negative screening. It is an investment strategy that actively includes companies, sectors, or projects in an investment portfolio based on their superior ESG performance relative to industry peers (such as the companies rated above A). This approach seeks to identify and invest in entities that demonstrate strong environmental, social, and governance practices, thereby aligning the investment portfolio with sustainability goals while potentially enhancing long-term financial returns.
It can be said that this sustainable investment strategy is the most commonly used. Fund managers will focus on the ESG ratings of companies and select those with better sustainability performance to include in their equity portfolios. Well-known index providers also construct ESG or sustainability stock indexes based on companies' ESG scores to track their long-term market performance and compare them with benchmark indexes (for example, the MSCI USA ESG Leader Index vs. the MSCI USA Index).
In May 2019, BlackRock launched an ESG-themed fund called the iShares ESG MSCI USA Leaders ETF. The fund seeks to track the investment results of the MSCI USA Extended ESG Leaders Index and will generally invest at least 90% of its assets in the component securities of the underlying index. The fund obtains exposure to large- and mid-cap US stocks with high environmental, social, and governance (ESG) ratings relative to their sector peers and helps investors avoid exposure to companies with low ESG ratings and severe controversies, thereby pursuing longer-term returns. Since its inception, the fund's total return has reached 15.30%. Below are some sustainable-related details of the fund.



Figure 3.3: Data about MSCI ESG Fund Ratings as of 20-Jun-2025, based on holdings as of 31-May-2025

ESG integration
ESG integration is a strategy that systematically and explicitly incorporates environmental, social, and governance factors into investment analysis and asset allocation. It goes beyond traditional exclusionary practices and best-in-class strategies by integrating ESG factors with traditional financial metrics to provide investors with a more comprehensive assessment of investment opportunities and risks. For example, investment managers may combine ESG scores with fundamental ratings to select stocks or bonds or even require their portfolio's ESG score to exceed that of a benchmark index. Moreover, ESG integration involves identifying ESG issues that have a material impact on a company's finances and incorporating them into valuation models to adjust investment decisions. This approach not only helps identify potential long-term value creation opportunities but also enhances risk management and portfolio sustainability.
Case: ESG Integration at J.P. Morgan Asset Management 
In April 2023, J.P. Morgan Asset Management (JPMAM) released a document outlining its ESG integration approach. JPMAM primarily focuses on the financial materiality of sustainability issues for investee companies, believing that companies with strong ESG performance typically have better risk management and operational efficiency, leading to superior financial performance and lower cost of capital. Additionally, ESG leaders are better positioned to adapt to policy changes during the global transition to net zero (such as carbon pricing) and are more likely to win consumer favor. We have already explored in more detail the impact of ESG performance on company fundamentals in Chapter 2, so we won't repeat it here.
JPMAM's process for integrating ESG factors into investment decisions is shown in the figure below. It is important to note that this process only applies to investments that claim to incorporate ESG considerations into their fund management strategies and is not a mandatory requirement for all JPMAM investments. 
Figure 3.4: ESG integration process at J.P. Morgan Asset Management

Source: J.P. Morgan Asset Management, as of December 31, 2022, https://am.jpmorgan.com/content/dam/jpm-am-aem/emea/regional/de/investment-themes/esg-integration-approach.pdf
First, each Investment Group designs a suitable ESG integration method based on its investment style and strategy. This method is then presented to the ESG & Climate Research Working Group. The Working Group evaluates each ESG integration strategy using the company's 10-metric framework for ESG integration. This framework is used to measure the extent of ESG integration by the Investment Group, as shown in the figure below. Each metric is scored out of 5 points. Only investment strategies that score more than 30 points in total (with at least 2 points for each metric) are recognized as having adopted the ESG Integration method and are permitted to use it. Otherwise, the process is repeated based on feedback from the Working Group.
From JPMAM’s ESG integration strategy, several questions arise. JPMAM focuses primarily on financial materiality rather than the intrinsic social or environmental value of ESG factors. Does this approach risk overlooking the true purpose of ESG? Moreover, for a fund to carry the “ESG integration” label, it must achieve a minimum score of 30. Can such a threshold effectively prevent greenwashing, or does it risk becoming a box-ticking exercise?
By comparison, the Norwegian sovereign wealth fund relies on exclusions and active ownership as its ESG tools, while JPMAM emphasizes internal integration and scoring mechanisms. Which of these approaches is likely to exert greater influence on promoting sustainability across global capital markets—and why?
Figure 3.5: J.P. Morgan Asset Management 10-metric framework for ESG integration

Source: J.P. Morgan Asset Management Sustainable Investing Team and ESG & Climate Research Working Group, https://am.jpmorgan.com/content/dam/jpm-am-aem/emea/regional/de/investment-themes/esg-integration-approach.pdf
Thematic investing
Thematic investing is an investment strategy that focuses on specific themes or trends. It aims to generate returns by investing in companies or projects related to these themes while also aligning with the investors' values and social goals. Unlike traditional asset class or sector-based investing, thematic investing takes a macro view, identifying assets that can benefit from long-term trends. In the ESG (environmental, social, and governance) space, thematic investing includes investments in clean energy, green technology, sustainable agriculture, sustainable infrastructure, natural resource conservation, and biodiversity. It also covers social and governance issues such as health, food security, gender equality, and inclusive boards. These investments are often made through thematic funds, such as mutual funds or ETFs, which focus on specific ESG themes and help investors direct their capital towards areas aligned with sustainable development goals.
Case: Allianz Smart Energy thematic fund 
A practical example is that Allianz Global Investors is set to launch a thematic investment fund this year called "Allianz Smart Energy". At least 70% of the fund's assets will be invested in equities of companies engaged in the area of smart energy or the transition of energy usage. Specifically, these are companies that, through technology, offer products and/or services that actively contribute to the shift away from fossil fuels, enhance the resilience of sustainable energy infrastructure, create renewable sources of energy generation, develop or manufacture energy storage systems, and improve energy efficiency and access to sustainable energy consumption.
The fund's investment strategy stipulates that more than 50% of the weighted average revenue of all invested companies, as well as at least 20% of each individual company's revenue, must come from economic activities that promote the following United Nations Sustainable Development Goals or EU Taxonomy objectives: 
	•	Affordable and Clean Energy (e.g. ensure access to affordable, reliable, sustainable and modern energy for all)
	•	Industry Innovation and Infrastructure (e.g. build resilient infrastructure with renewable sources of energy generation, improve energy efficiency to promote sustainable industrialization and foster innovation)
	•	Sustainable Cities and Communities (e.g. make cities and human settlements inclusive, safe, resilient and sustainable)
	•	Responsible Consumption and Production (e.g. ensure sustainable consumption and production patterns)
	•	Climate Action (e.g. take urgent action to combat climate change and its impacts)
In addition, the investment manager will qualitatively assess the invested companies' GHG emissions, product carbon footprint, energy consumption, biodiversity, hazardous waste, as well as social and employee matters to measure how these business activities negatively impact sustainability factors. The fund will also consider whether the invested companies adhere to good corporate governance practices. These criteria fall under the ESG integration method we mentioned earlier.
Moreover, the investment manager will employ an exclusion policy (such as negative screening or norm-based screening) to avoid investing in companies that do not meet the minimum international business practice standards and those with ESG controversy risks, such as companies involved in the production and distribution of weapons or tobacco, or power generation companies that heavily rely on fossil fuels.
The Securities and Futures Commission (SFC) of Hong Kong has listed all the authorized investment funds that use ESG investing strategies on its official website, including both unlisted and listed funds. Each fund specifies the specific category of ESG investment strategy it uses. If you are interested, you can refer to these funds' specific sustainability criteria for selecting investee companies.
From our discussion of thematic investing, an important question emerges: do investors in these funds care more about financial returns or about their contributions to climate and social goals? Looking ahead, if carbon taxes and carbon pricing rise significantly, would funds such as Allianz Smart Energy become more competitive? Furthermore, are there differences in how markets like the EU, Hong Kong, and Mainland China classify and disclose ESG funds, and how might these regulatory variations shape cross-border investment flows?

Figure 3.6: List of ESG funds

Source: SFC, https://www.sfc.hk/en/Regulatory-functions/Products/List-of-ESG-funds
Impact investing
Impact investing is an investment strategy that aims to generate positive and measurable social and environmental impacts alongside financial returns. Unlike thematic investing, which focuses primarily on stocks and bonds of companies, impact investing is more about providing finance for specific projects. These projects can include microfinance, community investing, social venture funds, funds with social impact objectives, and green and social bonds. Because its goal is to create positive social and environmental impacts, measuring and reporting these non-financial impacts is a key element of impact investing. For example, it requires clearly defining and measuring ESG objectives such as the amount of greenhouse gas emissions avoided per €1 million invested each year, the percentage of water consumption saved, improvements in biodiversity, and the increase in access to financial services in remote areas.
Case: Impact investing in practice: Ethias’ strategy 
A Belgium-based insurance company, Ethias, has developed an internal guidance document for its impact investing. They aim to support a just transition (addressing environmental issues without neglecting social equity), reduce inequality, promote health and well-being, and drive a low-carbon and sustainable economy through their investments. Ethias has established four principles for its impact investing:
Balancing Profit and Risk: Ethias is willing to sacrifice some economic returns in exchange for positive social impact.
Intentionality: Ethias explicitly aims to drive social and environmental change.
Measurability: The social or environmental impact of the investment can be quantified.
Additionality: The investment brings about positive changes that would not have occurred otherwise.
Ethias's impact investing themes are linked to the United Nations Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs), including SDG 1 (No Poverty), SDG 3 (Good Health and Well-being), SDG 7 (Affordable and Clean Energy), SDG 11 (Sustainable Cities and Communities), SDG 12 (Responsible Consumption and Production), and SDG 13 (Climate Action).
Given that some returns may be sacrificed for social impact, Ethias places particular emphasis on impact measurement. They have categorized the intentionality, measurability, and additionality of an investment into three levels (below). For the company to proceed with an impact investment, it must score at least at the medium level in all three aspects.
From our earlier discussion of investment strategies, a key question arises: how does impact investing truly differ from other approaches? From an investor’s perspective, if impact investing requires sacrificing part of the financial return, which types of investors—such as pension funds, insurance companies, or family offices—are more likely to adopt it, and why? 

Table 3.2: 3 levels in Ethias’ evaluation
Principle
Level
Content
Intentionality
Low Level
Investments are made without a clear intention to generate positive social or environmental impact. Investment decisions are primarily driven by financial returns, with no explicit consideration of ESG or sustainability factors

Medium Level
Investments are motivated by a positive intention, but this intention is not necessarily at the core of the investment strategy. Investors may seek to align their personal values with their investments but without a strong commitment to impact

High Level
Investments are deliberately designed to generate positive social or environmental impact. Intention is at the core of the investment strategy, and investors actively seek opportunities that address specific sustainability or social inclusion needs.
Measurability
Low Level
Investments are not measured or evaluated in terms of impact. There is no system in place to track or quantify the social or environmental outcomes of investments.

Medium Level
Investments are accompanied by basic measures to assess impact, but these measures are not necessarily thorough or sophisticated. Investors may have basic performance indicators without in-depth evaluation.

High Level
Investments come with a robust impact measurement. Investors use quantitative and qualitative metrics to continuously assess the social and environmental outcomes of their investments. Data is collected, analyzed, and transparently communicated.
Additionality
Low Level
Investments are not necessarily designed to be additional, meaning they may not necessarily contribute to outcomes that would not have occurred otherwise. They may follow a business as usual approach without seeking to generate additional impact.

Medium Level
Investments aim to have an impact, but they could still be considered “additional” only to a certain extent. They seek to improve sustainability or social responsibility compared to non-sustainable alternatives but without bringing about radical changes.

High Level
Investments are designed to be clearly additional, meaning they aim to solve problems or create social or environmental benefits that would not have occurred without this investment. They go beyond the traditional approach and contribute to significant positive change. As example, this covers investments that go beyond traditional approaches, actively contributing to significant positive change by empowering underprivileged populations through financial inclusion and micro-credits.
Source: Ethias
Market dynamics
Since 2012, the GSIA has published a report on sustainable investment trends every two years, providing detailed statistics on the assets under management (AUM) for each sustainable investment strategy. Using their data, we have created the following chart. It is important to note that when GSIA conducts its statistics, a single investment may employ several sustainable investment strategies simultaneously. Therefore, the sum of the AUM for each strategy may be larger than the actual total sustainable investment AUM. In addition, we didn’t include the 2022 data because there is significant decrease in sustainable investment AUM from 2020 to 2022, due to changes in the US statistical methodology for the 2022 report and the unavailability of EU data. 
From the chart, we can see that ESG integration, Negative Screening, and Active Ownership are the most dominant ESG integration, Negative Screening, and Active Ownership are the most dominant sustainable investment strategies. Before the change in statistical methodology in 2020, their AUM all exceeded 10 trillion USD. Other types of sustainable investment strategies, such as Positive Screening, Thematic Investing, and Norms-Based Screening, have maintained a stable scale. Impact Investing has the smallest scale, with only 0.3 trillion USD in 2020.
Why does impact investing remain much smaller in scale compared with ESG integration or negative screening? Do you think impact investing could ever become mainstream, or will it remain a niche area reserved for a small pool of “altruistic capital”? Finally, compared with philanthropic giving, what is the greatest value of impact investing—its ability to combine measurable social and environmental outcomes with financial discipline?

Source: GSIA
The impact of sustainability on stock returns or asset pricing
Beyond impact investing, which focuses on primary market investments, the ESG investment strategies we introduced earlier can be broadly summarized into two types. The first involves screening stocks based on sustainability factors such as ESG ratings, international norms, industry characteristics, or carbon emission intensity. The second entails engaging with investee companies on ESG issues after implementing investment strategies to improve their ESG behaviors. In this section, we will primarily explore whether and how these ESG investment strategies contribute positively to stock or portfolio returns. 
Three types of empirical studies
In fact, numerous empirical studies have identified a significant correlation between sustainability and positive stock or portfolio returns. These findings can generally be categorized into three types:

Empirical regression
This approach uses regression analysis to examine the differences in stock returns between companies with high and low sustainability or to quantify how much return increases with a one-unit improvement in ESG rating. A 2019 study by Khan (2019) provides profound insights. The study used constituent stocks from the MSCI All Country World Investable Market Index (ACWI IMI) as its sample, covering stocks from 23 developed and 19 emerging markets between 2009 and 2017, representing approximately 99% of global equity investment opportunities, making it highly representative. Instead of relying on existing ESG ratings, the study constructed two key metrics: a composite governance indicator and a new ESG indicator. The composite governance indicator was a weighted combination of four factors: 
Equity dispersion: Measured by the proportion of free-float shares to total shares, where higher dispersion typically indicates a more transparent governance structure and better alignment between management and shareholder interests.
Shareholder orientation: Based on the classification of national legal traditions (common law, Scandinavian/German law, French law, socialist law), with common law countries scoring higher due to their emphasis on shareholder protection.
Institutional strength: Assessed using Bloomberg’s country political risk scores, evaluating factors like rule of law, regulatory quality, and government efficiency.
MSCI governance score: The G Pillar score from MSCI ESG ratings.
The new ESG indicator was constructed as follows: based on SASB guidelines, the study identified material environmental and social (E&S) issues for each industry and scored companies on these issues. These E&S scores were then weighted and averaged with the composite governance score, using MSCI’s industry-specific E/S/G pillar weightings, to derive a new ESG score. The sample was divided into quartiles based on composite governance scores and new ESG scores, and regression analysis was conducted on the stocks’ monthly returns, controlling for style, time, and sector differences. The results showed that, during the sample period, companies in the top quartile for composite governance scores (representing the highest governance quality) outperformed those in the bottom quartile by an average of 33 basis points (bps) per month. For the new ESG indicator, this figure was 36 bps. This suggests that a company’s governance quality or ESG score can serve as a strong signal for predicting stock performance.
Quintile portfolio analysis
This method groups stocks by ESG ratings or other sustainability metrics and examines the return differences between high-sustainability and low-sustainability portfolios over a specific period.
Building on the regression analysis exploring the impact of improved composite governance or ESG scores on stock returns, Khan(2019) constructed portfolios based on the quartiles of the sample stocks’ composite governance or ESG scores. The study summarized the differences in cumulative returns between the highest and lowest quartile portfolios over the sample period. The quartile portfolios were formed monthly, and one-month-ahead capitalization-weighted returns were calculated. The monthly portfolio returns were cumulated to derive the growth curves. The results, as shown in the figures below, indicate that the top quartile of the composite governance score had a cumulative return spread of 122 percentage points (pps) over the bottom quartile by the end of the period (from 2009-2017). For the ESG score, this figure was 41 pps (from 2013-2017).



Figure 3.8: Performance of the Composite Governance Score, January 2009–November 2017 

Source: Khan(2019), https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/0015198X.2019.1654299
Figure 3.9: Material ESG Score, January 2013–November 2017

Source: Khan(2019), https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/0015198X.2019.1654299
Similarly, MSCI released a study in 2024 that employed quintile analysis. The study adjusted the ESG scores of all stocks in the MSCI ACWI universe for industry, size, and region, then sorted and categorized them based on their quintile positions. For each quintile category, equal-weighted quintiles were constructed to ensure performance results were not skewed by a few large-cap stocks, such as Apple Inc. or Microsoft Corp. This allowed a comparison of the long-term cumulative return differences between companies in the highest MSCI ESG ratings (or E/S/G single pillar scores) quintiles and those in the lowest MSCI ESG quintiles (or E/S/G single pillar scores) within the MSCI ACWI index. The results, shown in the figure below, indicate that from 2013 to 2023, the highest-rated ESG quintiles outperformed the lowest-rated quintiles by nearly 40 percentage points (pps) in cumulative returns (notably, Khan’s results using a newly constructed ESG score were 41 pps), demonstrating the strongest performance effect. A similar pattern was observed for the individual E/S/G pillars, with the highest quintiles consistently outperforming their lowest counterparts. The largest cumulative return gap was in the S pillar, approaching 30 pps, followed by the E pillar (over 15 pps), and the G pillar (about 13 pps). In addition, the performance of the total MSCI ESG Ratings was more consistent over time compared to the individual pillars, which exhibited greater variation.
