This variation at the pillar level can be attributed to the stronger residual exposures of pillar scores to other financially relevant factors, such as industry or countries, compared to the total ESG score. 
Figure 3.10: Cumulative performance of highest- vs. lowest-rated ESG quintiles and cumulative return in the MSCI ACWI index

Source: MSCI
The study then conducted the same analysis by region. It is worth noting that the outperformance of top-ESG-rated companies over their bottom-rated peers was consistent across both developed markets (DM)—Europe, North America, and Pacific—and emerging markets (EM), as shown in the figure below. Across different regions, the Pacific region’s highest-ESG quintiles outperformed the lowest-ESG quintiles by nearly 55 percentage points (pps), followed by Europe. Despite significant variation, emerging markets also demonstrated an outperformance trend in cumulative returns over the 11-year period, with figures similar to but slightly lower than those of developed markets (MSCI World Index).
Figure 3.11: Cumulative performance of highest- vs. lowest-rated ESG quintiles
ESG score and cumulative return in the regions

Source: MSCI
MSCI ESG ratings adopt a highly industry-tailored approach, selecting only the key issues with the most significant financial impact in each industry to calculate ratings. Therefore, by examining the actual performance of MSCI ESG ratings across industries, we can assess whether these key issues truly translate into stock price performance. As shown in the figure below, highest ESG-rated stocks outperformed their lowest-rated counterparts in cumulative returns across nearly all industries during the sample period. In industries such as information technology, the cumulative return gap exceeded 50 percentage points (pps). Consumer staples showed flat performance, while energy and real estate exhibited slight underperformance.
Figure 3.12: Cumulative performance of highest- vs. lowest-rated ESG quintiles
ESG score and cumulative return in the industries

Source: MSCI
Index comparison
This more straightforward approach compares the long-term returns of ESG-related indices, such as the MSCI ESG Leaders Index, with their parent indices to analyze the performance differences.
Khan (2019) examined the performance of pure governance strategies and ESG strategies relative to the full MSCI ACWI IMI sample (including all companies, regardless of whether they have governance or ESG data). The figure below illustrates the cumulative return performance of the top 25% governance portfolio (based on composite governance scores), calculated using market capitalization weighting, compared to the full MSCI ACWI IMI sample. The top 25% governance portfolio and the full sample’s market-cap-weighted future returns were calculated monthly, and the return series were cumulated to produce the growth curve shown in Figure 10. As depicted, the best-governance companies outperformed the full sample by 40 percentage points over the test period. Similarly, the cumulative return performance of the top 25% ESG portfolio (based on material ESG scores), calculated using market capitalization weighting, was compared to the full MSCI ACWI IMI sample. The results show that the best-ESG companies outperformed the full sample by 17 percentage points over the test period.
Figure 3.13: Corporate governance long-only strategy vs. universe, January 2009–November 2017

Source: Khan(2019), https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/0015198X.2019.1654299
Figure 3.14: ESG long-only strategy vs. universe, January 2013–November 2017

Source: Khan(2019), https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/0015198X.2019.1654299
In addition, MSCI analyzed the cumulative return performance of its various ESG indices (which are based on the parent MSCI ACWI Index or a regional sub-index, with companies selected or weight-tilted based on their MSCI ESG Ratings) compared to the MSCI ACWI Index from 2013 to 2023. The results showed that these ESG indices outperformed the MSCI ACWI Index by 2 to 10 pps in cumulative returns.
Figure 3.15: Cumulative performance of highest- vs. lowest-rated ESG quintiles and cumulative return in various indices

Source: MSCI
Although most academic and industry research tends to use quintile analysis to explore the advantages of sustainability investing strategies over traditional ones, the statistics above show that ESG screening investing strategies indeed outperform low-ESG portfolios or benchmark indices (such as the MSCI ACWI Index) in the long term. However, a limitation of quintile analysis is its inability to control for all other factor exposures (common investment factors are listed in the table below). 
For instance, Gupta. et al. (2016) found that MSCI ESG ratings are positively correlated with quality and residual volatility factors, raising doubts about whether ESG can independently contribute to portfolio performance as a standalone factor. MSCI standardized the ESG scores of all stocks in the global MSCI ACWI IMI universe into Z-scores (ranging from -3 to 3) and incorporated them into the MSCI GEMLT ESG model (Global Equity Model Long-Term ESG model). This model includes the 16 style factors shown in the figure below. 

Table 3.3: Common investment factor exposures
Factor
Content
Value
Selecting stocks with low valuations can generate excess returns.
Dividend
Choosing companies with high dividend yields can generate excess returns.
Momentum
If a stock’s price has risen significantly in the recent period, selecting such stocks while shorting stocks that have declined significantly in price during the same period may generate excess returns.
Low Volatility
Consistently buying and holding stocks with historically low price volatility can, over the long term, yield returns for the investment portfolio that exceed the market average.
Quality
Consistently selecting companies with high fundamental quality (e.g., those with high ROE) can, over the long term, provide investors with returns better than the market average.
Size
Consistently choosing smaller-cap companies can, over the long term, enable investors to achieve returns above the market average.

Figure 3.16: 16 style factors in the MSCI GEMLT ESG model 
Source: Gupta. et al. (2016)
By including ESG as an independent factor, the model isolates the standalone impact of ESG on stock returns, facilitating performance attribution. The analysis results, as shown in the figure, reveal that even after controlling for other factors, the ESG factor still independently contributes positively to performance. From 2013 to 2023, each unit of factor exposure from MSCI ESG Z-scores generated a cumulative return of 5%.

Figure 3.17: the Cumulative performance of the MSCI ESG factor

Source: MSCI
How exactly the ESG factor influences stock returns
Since the ESG factor can independently contribute to the returns of stocks or investment portfolios, it is worth exploring how exactly the ESG factor influences stock returns. We should first analyze the possible drivers of a stock’s return and then examine how the ESG factor impacts these drivers to contribute to stock returns. Gupta. et al. (2016) decomposed the total return for the MSCI ACWI Index into valuation adjustment (or stock price increases), dividends, and dividend growth. Navneet Kumar (2021) proposed decomposing total equity returns of ESG portfolios into three fundamental drivers: earnings growth, multiple expansion (or P/E expansion), and dividend yields. Rangvid (2021) broke down stock returns into dividend yield, growth (referring to the growth rate of a fundamental, such as company earnings, dividends, or GDP), and valuation change (growth in the ratio of stock prices to the fundamental). It is clear that scholars share similar views on the drivers of stock returns: 
First, They all identify dividend yield as a key driver; Second, Whether described as valuation adjustment, stock price increases, or multiple expansion, these terms essentially refer to changes in the price-to-earnings (P/E) ratio, as stock price can be expressed as P = P/E * EPS. When earnings per share (EPS) remains constant, stock price changes are primarily driven by variations in the P/E ratio; Then, They all highlight the contribution of fundamental improvements, or “growth” (e.g., earnings per share growth). Although Gupta. et al. (2016) refers to this as dividend growth, dividend growth typically depends on earnings growth, as companies can only sustainably increase dividend payments when profits rise. Therefore, in our following explanation, we will consistently adopt decomposition approach to avoid confusion (i.e., stock returns = earnings growth + P/E expansion + dividend yield). Building on this, our next task is to deeply analyze how the ESG factor influences these drivers through specific mechanisms or channels, ultimately contributing to final returns.
We can all understand that improvements in a company’s profitability (earnings growth) and dividend increases (dividend yield) fundamentally stem from sustained enhancements in its fundamentals. As discussed in Chapter 2, these enhancements include reduced financing costs, lower costs from increased employee efficiency, decreased agency costs with stakeholders, or improved profitability from capitalizing on the purchasing preferences of customers and supply chain partners. Broadly, these can be summarized as an increased likelihood of generating profits (higher cash flows) and a reduced likelihood of losses (or lower risks). According to Giese et al. (2019)’s research, we can use the standard Discounted Cash Flow (DCF) model to understand how ESG influences the probabilities of earning profits and avoiding losses. Giese et al. (2019) decomposes the impact of ESG on corporate profitability and dividend yield into three transmission mechanisms: the cash flow transmission mechanism (affecting the numerator of the DCF model), the idiosyncratic risk transmission mechanism (also affecting the numerator), and the systematic risk transmission mechanism (affecting the denominator). We will now examine each of these mechanisms in detail.
Cash flow transmission mechanism
Companies with higher ESG ratings tend to be more competitive than their peers (Godfrey et al., 2009, Gregory et al., 2014). This competitive advantage may arise from various factors, such as efficient resource utilization, superior human capital development, and outstanding innovation management. In addition, these companies are often better at formulating long-term business strategies and incentivizing executive performance through long-term compensation plans. As a result, the competitive advantage brought by ESG factors translates into higher profitability and dividend payments, ultimately boosting stock returns (as shown in the figure below). 
Figure 3.18: DCF model


Figure 3.19: Cash flow channel

Source: Godfrey et al.(2009), https://www.msci.com/downloads/web/msci-com/research-and-insights/blog-post/msci-esg-indexes-underperformed-but-met-esg-objectives/Foundations-of-ESG-Investing-Part-1.pdf
To validate this mechanism, researchers analyzed data from over 1,600 stocks in the MSCI World Index between 2007 and 2017. They first divided the stocks into five groups based on industry- and size-neutralized ESG scores, with Q5 representing the highest ESG-rated companies and Q1 the lowest. The study then compared the profitability (defined as sales revenue minus cost of sales divided by total assets) of different ESG groups using standardized Z-scores. The results showed that the profitability of the Q5 group (high ESG ratings) was significantly higher than that of the Q1 group (low ESG ratings), confirming that high ESG-rated companies leverage their competitive advantages to achieve greater profitability. 
Figure 3.20: Gross profitability (Z-score) of size-adjusted ESG quintiles 
Source: Godfrey et al.(2009)
Furthermore, dividend yield statistics reinforced this finding: Q5 companies exhibited significantly higher dividend payments than Q1 companies, reflecting not only their financial stability but also their alignment with the long-term preferences of sustainable investors. Thus, the cash flow transmission mechanism directly drives the long-term return growth of high ESG-rated companies by enhancing profitability and dividend payments.
Idiosyncratic risk transmission mechanism
Study by Godfrey et al. (2009) indicates that companies with higher ESG ratings typically maintain higher compliance standards and risk management capabilities in corporate governance and supply chain management. This reduces their exposure to severe negative events such as fraud, embezzlement, corruption, or litigation(Hong and Kacperczyk, 2009), thereby protecting company value and stock price stability. Hoepner et al. (2011) further suggest that this protective effect resembles an “insurance effect,” mitigating firm-specific downside or tail risks. 
Figure 3.21: Trailing dividend yield (Z-score) of size-adjusted ESG quintiles 

Source: Godfrey et al.(2009)
Figure 3.22: Idiosyncratic risk channel

Source: Godfrey et al.(2009)
To test this mechanism, the study defined “idiosyncratic risk events” as instances where a stock experienced a drawdown exceeding 95% or bankruptcy within three years and compared the frequency of such events between the Q5 and Q1 groups over a 10-year period. The results showed that high ESG-rated companies (Q5) had significantly fewer risk events than low ESG-rated companies (Q1), confirming their stronger risk management capabilities. 
Figure 3.23: The number of stocks that realized a more than 95% cumulative loss

Source: Godfrey et al.(2009)
To further quantify idiosyncratic risk, the study used residual volatility as a metric, defined as the portion of stock returns that cannot be explained by common factors in the MSCI Barra Global Equity Model Long-Term (GEMLT). This metric reflects company-specific volatility unrelated to overall market or industry trends, serving as a key tool for assessing unique stock risks. The findings showed that the Q5 group, with the highest ESG ratings, exhibited significantly lower residual volatility than the Q1 group, with the lowest ESG ratings, indicating that high ESG-rated companies experience lower volatility when facing firm-specific risks, thereby enhancing their financial stability.
Moreover, the study measured tail risk using the kurtosis of stock returns. Kurtosis, a statistical measure, describes the shape of a distribution, reflecting the degree of peakedness and the thickness of the tails. In finance, high kurtosis typically indicates a thicker-tailed distribution, meaning a higher probability of extreme gains or losses (tail risk). The results revealed that the kurtosis of stock returns for the Q5 group was significantly lower than that of the Q1 group, suggesting that high ESG-rated companies have thinner-tailed return distributions, with a lower likelihood of extreme negative events, such as significant price drops.
Figure 3.24: Residual capital asset pricing model (CAPM) volatility (Z-score)

Source: Godfrey et al.(2009)
Figure 3.25: Kurtosis (Z-score)

Source: Godfrey et al.(2009)
Discuss: ESG engagement and downside risk
The ESG screening strategy is indeed effective in reducing idiosyncratic risk and extreme tail risk associated with individual stocks. Building on this, scholars have also found that engaging with investee companies on sustainability issues (active ownership) can further reduce downside risk. Downside risk refers to the potential loss when investment returns fall below a certain expected level, such as zero, the average return, or the risk-free rate. It focuses on negative deviations, ignoring positive gains, and reflects investors’ concerns about losses.  Hoepner et al. (2024) utilized data from a large asset management firm, covering 1,443 ESG engagement cases involving 485 global companies, to analyze how companies’ downside risk performance compares to a control group after undergoing varying levels of ESG engagement. The study measured downside risk using two metrics:
VaR (Value at Risk): This measures the maximum potential loss of a portfolio or stock at a specific confidence level (e.g., 95% or 99%) over a given time horizon. For example, a 95% VaR of $1 million indicates a 95% probability that losses will not exceed $1 million.
LPM (Lower Partial Moment): This calculates the moments of returns below a target level (e.g., zero, average return, or risk-free rate), quantifying the probability, magnitude, or intensity of negative deviations. A higher LPM value indicates greater downside risk.
And the 1,443 ESG engagement cases were categorized into four levels of intensity:
M1: Investors raise ESG issues with the company.
M2: The company acknowledges the ESG issues.
M3: The company begins taking action to address the issues.
M4: Shareholders consider the issues resolved.
In addition, the study provided detailed statistical analysis of the themes, industries, regional distribution, and average time required for these engagement events. 
The overall regression results showed that, compared to the control group, all companies undergoing ESG engagement experienced a significant reduction in downside risk. The reduction was particularly pronounced when engagement progressed to M2 or higher (i.e., the company acknowledges the issue) or M3 or higher (i.e., the company takes action). However, engagement limited to the M1 stage (i.e., merely raising issues) did not significantly reduce downside risk. This indicates that deeper investor ESG engagement is critical to achieving risk mitigation.
In regional analysis, the study found that only ESG engagement events in North America significantly reduced corporate downside risk, while engagement in Europe and other regions showed no significant impact. This difference may be attributed to stronger investor rights protections and more effective engagement mechanisms in North America. Further analysis of the effectiveness of different ESG themes revealed that, compared to the control group, engagement focused solely on environmental themes significantly reduced downside risk, while engagement on social or governance themes showed no significant effect. This underscores the unique role of environmental factors in ESG engagement.
Figure 3.26: Engagements sorted by the target firm’s country of incorporation

Source: Hoepner et al. (2024), https://academic.oup.com/view-large/figure/443921797/rfad034f1.tif
Moreover, the study found that companies with significantly reduced downside risk following shareholder engagement experienced a 26% reduction in environmental risk events. This data not only validates the effectiveness of ESG engagement in reducing investment downside risk but also demonstrates its substantial contribution to improving corporate environmental performance.
This study prompts us to further explore the transmission mechanisms between corporate ESG acknowledgment or actions and the reduction of downside risk. Why do environmental issues appear to be more effective than social or governance issues? Could this be related to differences in data measurability, externalities of risk, or investor attention? From the perspective of asset managers, how can ESG engagement be leveraged across different stages (M1–M4) to optimize resource allocation? Is there an optimal intervention path that maximizes risk mitigation benefits while minimizing costs?
Systematic risk transmission mechanism
Let’s now examine the impact of ESG on the denominator of the DCF model. Giese et al. (2019) points out that companies with higher ESG ratings are less sensitive to market systemic shocks, thus exhibiting lower systematic risk. In the Capital Asset Pricing Model (CAPM) framework, systematic risk is reflected through the beta value, where a lower beta indicates a lower required rate of return for investors. In the DCF model, a lower required rate of return corresponds to a reduced cost of capital, thereby increasing company valuation. 
Figure 3.27: Capital asset pricing model (CAPM) 
For example, companies with higher energy or commodity usage efficiency are more resilient to price fluctuations, which reduces the systematic risk of their stock prices (Eccles et al., 2014). To validate this mechanism, the study used the GEMLT model to calculate the systematic volatility of stock portfolios across different ESG quintiles and standardized the results. Systematic volatility refers to the portion of an asset’s return volatility associated with the overall market or common factors (such as economic conditions, interest rate changes, macroeconomic policies, or market sentiment), reflecting the extent to which asset prices are affected by systemic market risks. Additionally, the study analyzed the profit volatility of different ESG rating groups. The results showed that both systematic volatility and profit volatility were lower for the Q5 group (highest ESG ratings) compared to the Q1 group (lowest ESG ratings), indicating that high ESG-rated companies have lower systematic risk.
Figure 3.27: Systematic volatility and profit volatility

Source: Godfrey et al.(2009)
Furthermore, historical beta value statistics confirmed this: the Q5 group consistently had lower beta values than the Q1 group, reflecting reduced systematic risk exposure. This risk reduction directly lowers the cost of capital, thereby boosting company valuation. This finding aligns with El Ghoul et al. (2011), who found that high ESG-rated companies exhibit lower financing costs across various cost of capital metrics, as we discussed in detail in Chapter 2.
The researchers analyzed the earnings-to-price (E/P) data, which is the inverse of the P/E ratio, for different ESG stock portfolios. A lower E/P ratio indicates a higher valuation for the stocks. The results showed that, on average, from the high ESG group to the low ESG group, the earnings-to-price ratio exhibited an increasing trend, indicating that groups with better sustainability performance had higher valuations.


Figure 3.28: Historical beta (Z-score) of ESG quintiles

Source: Godfrey et al.(2009)
Figure 3.29: Predicted earnings-to-price ratio of ESG quintiles

Source: Godfrey et al.(2009)
P/E expansion and time horizons
We previously discussed that the drivers of stock returns include not only earnings growth and dividend yield but also P/E expansion. As shown in the figure below, earnings growth and dividend yield stem from improvements in a company’s fundamentals. The ESG factor enhances these fundamentals through three channels: improving corporate cash flows, reducing idiosyncratic risk, and lowering sensitivity to systemic risk. However, for the third driver, P/E expansion, its positive contribution to stock returns may arise from increased market demand for companies with high MSCI ESG ratings. This could indicate investor herding or sentiment-driven capital inflows into ESG-related assets, potentially inflating valuations. If the positive returns of high ESG stock portfolios are primarily driven by P/E expansion, ESG investors may face risks, as elevated P/E ratios fueled by market enthusiasm rather than fundamental improvements could signal overvaluation or speculative bubbles, making returns susceptible to corrections if market sentiment shifts. 
Figure 3.30: Impact mechanism of ESG factor 

This raises a critical question worth exploring: which driver—earnings growth, dividend yield, or P/E expansion—plays the dominant role in driving the returns of high ESG stock portfolios? Moreover, does the contribution of these three drivers vary in the short term versus the long term? To address the first question, Giese and Shah (2024) divided the earnings per unit of market capitalization (earnings/price) of a high ESG-rated stock portfolio (Q5) by that of a low ESG-rated portfolio (Q1). If this ratio increases over time, it indicates that the earnings of the high ESG portfolio are growing faster than those of the low ESG portfolio, suggesting that returns are driven by earnings growth. Conversely, a declining ratio would indicate the presence of P/E expansion. The findings, as shown in the figures below, reveal that while the Q5/Q1 ratio fluctuated over the decade, it generally trended upward. This suggests that, in the long term, earnings growth indeed plays a dominant role in boosting stock portfolio returns. Similarly, the researchers compared the dividend yields of Q5 and Q1, which also showed an upward trend, indicating that the contribution of dividend yield to returns is, in the long term, greater than that of P/E expansion.
Figure 3.31: Trends of earnings / price ratio and dividend yield

Source: Giese and Shah (2024)
For the second question, Gupta. et al. (2016) decomposed the contributions of these three factors to stock returns across different time horizons, as shown in the figure. In the short term (1-year period), P/E expansion contributed 51.1%, earnings growth 37.5%, and dividend yield only 11.4%. However, from the 1-year to the 20-year period, the contribution of P/E expansion to stock returns steadily declined. By the 3-year mark, returns driven by improvements in company fundamentals surpassed those driven by market sentiment. Over the 20-year long-term investment horizon, the vast majority of investment returns were driven by company fundamentals, with P/E expansion contributing less than 7%. In summary, the distribution of the three drivers’ contributions to returns over the long term aligns with our expectations. However, due to the dominant role of valuation adjustments in the short term, investors with shorter holding periods must carefully assess whether ESG portfolios carry bubble risks. 
Here, it is necessary to closely examine whether there is empirical evidence supporting the dominance of P/E expansion in the short term. Bennani et al. (2018) constructed equal-weighted portfolios by dividing the constituent stocks of MSCI indices for North America, the Eurozone, non-Eurozone Europe, Japan, and global developed markets into five groups based on ESG ratings, rebalancing the portfolios quarterly according to rating changes, and analyzing annualized returns from 2014 to 2017. The results, as shown in the figure, indicate that in most regions during the sample period, high ESG portfolios indeed delivered significantly higher returns. The authors attribute these positive short- to medium-term returns primarily to demand effects driven by market sentiment rather than actual corporate financial performance. They noted that during this period, institutional investor capital flowed heavily into high ESG assets. For example, in November 2013, Norway’s sovereign wealth fund adopted a new responsible investment policy, while major European institutional investors (such as APG, PGGM, ERAFP, and FRR) also actively invested heavily in sustainable stocks in European and U.S. markets. Therefore, the authors infer that the superior performance of ESG investments from 2014 to 2017 was primarily driven by portfolio rebalancing from these leading European institutional investors.
Figure 3.32: Contributions of the three factors to stock returns

Source: Godfrey et al.(2009), https://www.msci.com/downloads/web/msci-com/research-and-insights/blog-post/msci-esg-indexes-underperformed-but-met-esg-objectives/Foundations-of-ESG-Investing-Part-1.pdf

Figure 3.33: Impact of ESG screening on sorted portfolio returns 

Source: Bennani et al. (2018), https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=3316862

The performance of sustainable financial products
The assets under management based on sustainable investment are growing larger and larger. By the end of 2024, the size of sustainable investment funds had reached 3.56 trillion USD. So we may be curious about the performance of sustainable investment and want to know whether sustainable investment can bring differentiated advantages compared with traditional investment methods. Morgan Stanley once conducted an interesting study, which, based on Morningstar's methodology, divided 10723 mutual funds and exchange traded funds into sustainable funds or non-sustainable funds to compare their performance in terms of returns and risks from 2004 to 2018.
Figure 3.34: Sustainable investment funds AUM


In a past survey by Morgan Stanley, although 75% of individual investors are interested in sustainable investing, 53% of investors believe that sustainable investing may require investors to trade off impact for investment returns. Moreover, 76% of US asset managers think that the sacrifice of investment returns may be the greatest challenge of sustainable investing. But is this really the case? The study's comparison results of the returns of sustainable funds and traditional funds are shown in the figure below. Here, the measure of returns is the median return of all funds. We can see that there are arithmetic differences in returns between sustainable funds and traditional funds across the years. However, most of these differences are not statistically significant, which means that the two sets of data can be regarded as equivalent in practical application. In addition, the occasional statistical differences lack consistency in direction or magnitude.
Subsequently, the author categorized the funds into four asset classes to test the robustness of the conclusion, namely funds investing in International Equity, Sector Equity, Taxable Bond, and U.S. Equity. The results revealed that, across all asset classes, the differences in returns between sustainable funds and traditional funds were minimal and inconsistent in direction. However, the magnitude of these differences has been gradually diminishing over time. This finding further corroborates the previous conclusion and is consistent with existing academic research.
Figure 3.35: Median total returns of sustainable and traditional funds, 2004-2018

Source: Morgan Stanley analysis of Morningstar data, 2019, https://www.morganstanley.com/content/dam/msdotcom/ideas/sustainable-investing-offers-financial-performance-lowered-risk/Sustainable_Reality_Analyzing_Risk_and_Returns_of_Sustainable_Funds.pdf
Figure 3.36: Annual median total returns (%) pf sustainable and traditional funds by asset class, 2004-2018

Source: Morgan Stanley analysis of Morningstar data, 2019
Although there was no significant difference in returns between sustainable and traditional funds, an important finding of the study was that sustainable funds demonstrated stronger risk resilience from 2004 to 2018. The researchers used downside deviation as a measure of investment risk during market downturns, where a more negative value indicates greater downside risk. They then calculated the median downside deviation, the difference in downside risk, and the significance level of the difference between sustainable and non-sustainable funds. The figure shows the difference in downside deviation between sustainable and traditional funds each year from 2004 to 2018. We found that the average difference in downside deviation over these 15 years was 0.6%, meaning that, on average, sustainable funds had lower downside risk than traditional funds each year. This phenomenon was statistically significant in almost every year. Moreover, as we can see from the figure, in the years widely recognized as turbulent markets, such as 2008, 2009, 2015, and 2018, sustainable funds had significantly lower downside deviation.
Figure 3.37: Median downside deviation of sustainable and traditional funds, 2004-2018

Source: Morgan Stanley analysis of Morningstar data, 2019
We still categorize the funds into four types. From the figure below, we can see that the risk reduction brought by sustainable funds is most pronounced in the fields of international equity and broad US equity funds. These two types of assets consistently show that sustainable funds have much smaller downside deviation compared to traditional funds. Bonds and sector equity show differentiated results, but the differences between the medians of these two types of assets are almost or completely statistically insignificant. The differences in risk indicators of the various asset classes shown in the figure highlight the consistent pattern that sustainable funds have lower downside deviation. Therefore, our conclusion is that regardless of the type of asset held, sustainable funds always show significantly lower downside risk than traditional funds. That is to say, sustainable funds may provide downside risk protection for investors.
Figure 3.38: Annual median in downside deviation (%) of sustainable and traditional funds by asset class, 2004-2018

Source: Morgan Stanley analysis of Morningstar data, 2019
So how did sustainable funds perform during periods of high volatility? Researchers selected the period from October 1, 2018, to December 31, 2018, as their research subject. During this time, the volatility of the US stock market significantly increased. For example, from October 1 to October 31, 2018, nearly half of the trading days saw intraday price swings of the major stock indices exceeding +/-1%. On some trading days, the Dow Jones Industrial Average fluctuated by nearly 1,000 points in a single day. This pattern of volatility continued into November—by November 20, the Dow had given back all its gains for the year, but then it rose consecutively again, closing above the 25,000-point mark by the end of the month. During this period (from October 1 to December 31), the returns of almost all collective investment vehicles declined. However, among US equity funds in this quarter, the median performance of sustainable funds was 1.39% higher than that of traditional funds. This difference was statistically significant at the 99th percentile. In other words, we can be almost certain that, during periods of high volatility, sustainable investment strategies may provide downside risk protection for investors. This quarterly analysis further supports our annual findings that sustainable funds offer lower downside deviation and strong risk-adjusted returns.
Figure 3.38: Returns of sustainable and traditional funds during recent high market volatility (October – December 2018)

Source: Morgan Stanley analysis of Morningstar data, 2019
We have observed that, in terms of returns, sustainable funds generally perform on par with traditional funds. However, when it comes to risk management and downside protection, sustainable funds show a clear advantage. This raises several important questions: If sustainable funds deliver similar returns but stronger risk performance, is that enough to drive more investors toward sustainable investing? In practice, do you think investors care more about long-term returns or about risk protection—and why? Looking ahead, if climate risks and policy uncertainty intensify over the next decade, will the risk-pricing advantage of sustainable investing become even more pronounced? Finally, how might differences between institutional investors (such as pension funds or insurance companies) and retail investors shape their willingness to prioritize risk protection over potential excess returns?

Chapter 4
Climate Finance 
Observed climate change and impacts
Global warming and causes
In 2024, the global average surface temperature was recorded at 1.18 °C above the 20th-century average and 1.35 °C above the pre-industrial baseline of 1850–1900. This marks the warmest year since records began in 1850, with the pace of warming having more than doubled since 1981.
Key reflection: Could 2024 be remembered as the coldest year of the future?
Figure 4.1: Global average surface temperature

Source: National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration (NOAA), https://www.climate.gov/news-features/understanding-climate/climate-change-global-temperature
When it comes to the reasons of this warming trend, human-induced greenhouse gases, particularly carbon dioxide emissions are the main cause. Since the onset of industrialization, the large-scale combustion of fossil fuels has released unprecedented quantities of anthropogenic CO₂ into the atmosphere. Annual emissions from fossil fuel combustion have risen each decade since the mid-20th century—from approximately 11 billion tons of CO₂ per year in the 1960s to an estimated 37.4 billion tons in 2024, according to the Global Carbon Budget 2024. 
Consequently, data from NOAA’s Global Monitoring Laboratory indicate that the global average atmospheric concentration of carbon dioxide reached a record 422.8 parts per million (ppm) in 2024. Moreover, the annual increase in 2024 was 3.75 ppm, the largest year-to-year rise ever recorded, underscoring the intensifying accumulation of greenhouse gases in the atmosphere.
Figure 4.2: Global carbon dioxide emissions and atmospheric carbon dioxide (1751-2024)

 Source: NOAA Climate.gov, https://www.climate.gov/media/12990
In parallel, human-induced emissions and global warming have profoundly affected air quality, meteorological hazards, and the frequency of extreme climate events. On the one hand, gases and particulate matter released directly into the atmosphere from human activities contribute to air pollution. On the other hand, a warmer climate amplifies these challenges by increasing the likelihood of wildfires, which elevate the concentration of harmful fine particulate matter (PM2.5). Rising temperatures also enhance tropospheric ozone formation, exacerbating ozone pollution and posing new public health risks.
Moreover, the frequency and intensity of several types of extreme events—including heatwaves, heavy precipitation, droughts, and tropical cyclones—have been amplified as a direct consequence of human influence on the climate system. This growing body of evidence underscores the interconnected nature of emissions, atmospheric composition, and extreme climate dynamics. 
Case: Beijing haze
More than a decade ago, China was still undergoing rapid industrialization and urbanization, a developmental trajectory that in many ways paralleled London in the 18th century and Los Angeles in the 19th century. Similar to those historical cases, China’s accelerated economic growth came with significant environmental costs. In Beijing, during the autumn and winter months, the formation of temperature inversions and an increased frequency of stagnant weather conditions severely reduced the atmosphere’s capacity to disperse pollutants. At the same time, winter heating driven by scattered coal combustion, rising demand for coal-fired power, high-intensity industrial activity, and heavy traffic emissions combined to create severe haze episodes.
In 2013, the annual average concentration of PM2.5 in Beijing reached 89.5 μg/m³, exceeding China’s national secondary standard of 35 μg/m³ by more than 1.5 times. Over the course of that year, there were 58 days classified as heavily polluted—on average, at least one severe pollution day per week. During daytime hours, nitrogen dioxide (NO₂) concentrations reached high levels, accompanied by elevated nitrate concentrations. At night, these nitrates underwent chemical transformation into nitrites, facilitated by the presence of high levels of sulfur dioxide (SO₂) and sulfates. Because nitrites are carcinogenic, this process posed a significant threat to public health. These conditions highlighted the urgent necessity for stringent air quality management and pollution control measures in Beijing. 
In September 2013, the Chinese government introduced the Air Pollution Prevention and Control Action Plan. In response, Beijing formulated and implemented its 2013–2017 Clean Air Action Plan, which marked the beginning of a comprehensive campaign to reduce PM2.5 concentrations. Guided by detailed source apportionment analyses of PM2.5, the city pursued phased control measures at the pollution source, while simultaneously advancing energy structure adjustments, cleaner industrial production, energy conservation and emissions reduction.
Over the course of a decade, these efforts produced remarkable results. By 2024, Beijing’s annual average PM2.5 concentration had declined to 30.5 μg/m³, representing a 66% reduction from 2013 levels. The number of “good air quality” days increased to 290, 114 more than in 2013, while the number of heavily polluted days fell to just two, a 96.6% decrease. Such achievements within a relatively short period highlight the scale and effectiveness of coordinated environmental governance. 
The economic dimension of these improvements is equally significant. Zhang et al. (2019) estimated the costs and benefits of the Action Plan during its first five years (2013–2018). Nationwide expenditures on air pollution control steadily increased, amounting to a total of 1,651 billion yuan, of which Beijing contributed approximately 46.9 billion yuan. At the same time, improved air quality generated extensive benefits, including reduced public health risks, higher agricultural yields, slower material degradation, and lower cleaning costs. These benefits were valued at 2,469 billion yuan nationwide. As a result, the net benefit reached 818 billion yuan, with Beijing alone achieving net benefits exceeding 10 billion yuan. 
From a cost–benefit perspective, Beijing’s air quality campaign offers an important policy lesson. While the expenditures on pollution control were substantial, the overall societal benefits clearly outweighed the costs. This raises a broader question: how can policymakers effectively persuade the public and businesses to accept such high levels of upfront investment in environmental governance? One possible avenue lies in leveraging financial innovations such as green bonds or public–private partnerships (PPPs), which can mobilize private capital, distribute risks more efficiently, and enhance the long-term sustainability of pollution control initiatives.
Moreover, the cost–benefit evaluation framework used in Beijing provides a useful analytical tool that may extend beyond air pollution management. Specifically, it prompts further reflection on whether similar approaches could be applied to climate mitigation and adaptation policies, where the costs of resilience-building today may generate substantial long-term benefits in avoided damages, enhanced productivity, and improved public health.
Figure 4.3: Beijing air quality 2008 - 2015

Source: BBC News, https://www.bbc.com/news/world-asia-china-35026363
Case: Pakistan floods
South Asia, home to nearly two billion people, is among the world’s most climate-sensitive regions. The annual summer monsoon (June–September) delivers vital rainfall from the Indian Ocean, sustaining agriculture and replenishing reservoirs and aquifers. On average, monsoons traditionally account for about 70% of the region’s annual water supply. However, global warming is increasingly disrupting these patterns. Because warmer air retains more moisture, monsoon rainfall has become both more intense and more erratic, heightening the risks of droughts, extreme precipitation, and flash flooding.
The human and economic toll of these changes has been profound. In 2022, Pakistan suffered one of its most devastating floods in history: at least 33 million people were affected, more than 1,700 lives were lost, and economic damages exceeded $30 billion, with an additional $16.3 billion. estimated for rehabilitation and reconstruction. More recently, in August 2025, heavy monsoon rains and flash floods killed at least 700 people, displaced thousands, and destroyed homes and crops, while further extreme weather events were anticipated.
This sequence of disasters underscores how climate change amplifies South Asia’s vulnerability to erratic monsoons. Intensified and unpredictable rainfall not only threatens lives and livelihoods in the short term but also undermines long-term economic recovery and sustainable development. In turn, this mounting climate risk has fueled rising demand for disaster financing, affordable insurance solutions, and large-scale investments in resilience building.
From a financing perspective, several pressing questions emerge. Why do floods inflict disproportionately greater damage on low-income countries? How should international climate finance mechanisms—such as the Loss and Damage Fund—be designed and allocated to ensure fairness and effectiveness? And, if one were in the position of a development bank official, what kinds of financial instruments could be crafted to support rapid post-disaster recovery while also laying the foundation for long-term resilience?

Figure 4.4: Children wade through a flooded canal in Pakistan

Source: UN News, https://news.un.org/en/story/2025/08/1165699
Wide but uneven impacts
Climate change has already inflicted substantial damage on global water and food security, critical infrastructure, and human livelihoods. Beyond these immediate impacts, it poses profound challenges to economic and social systems that transcend national borders. The most visible economic losses are concentrated in climate-sensitive sectors such as agriculture, forestry, fisheries, energy, and tourism—sectors that are not only highly dependent on climate conditions but also vulnerable to reduced outdoor labor productivity.
Figure 4.5: Country-level vulnerability in relation to 2019 per capita CO₂ emissions 
Source: IPCC, https://www.ipcc.ch/report/ar6/syr/downloads/figures/IPCC_AR6_SYR_Figure_2_3_2.png
While climate change has widespread impacts across countries, Least Developed Countries (LDCs) and Small Island Developing States (SIDS)—though contributing little to global carbon emissions—face disproportionately high exposure to climate-related hazards. Regions most at risk include West, Central, and East Africa; South Asia; Central and South America; SIDS; and the Arctic. These areas are often characterized by poverty, weak governance, limited access to essential services, violent conflict, and reliance on climate-sensitive livelihoods such as smallholder farming, pastoralism, and fishing. Social inequities—linked to gender, ethnicity, and income—further compound vulnerability, especially for Indigenous peoples and local communities. With constrained adaptive capacity and persistent development challenges, these regions face intensified risks from climate change. This disparity underscores the uneven burden of climate change and highlights the urgency of addressing intertwined social, economic, and environmental challenges.
The economics of climate actions
Compared with the direct physical impacts of global warming and the immediate consequences of extreme weather events, the broader economic repercussions of climate change are far more difficult to quantify. This complexity stems from their indirect nature, delayed manifestation, and the interconnections across regions and sectors. As a result, policymakers face a critical dilemma: should scarce resources be invested heavily in climate governance now, or should they be directed toward other pressing policy priorities? 
Recent research underscores the scale of potential economic losses from climate change. Kotz et al. (2024) evaluated the lagged effects of climate variables—including annual mean temperature, daily temperature variability, total annual precipitation, the number of wet days, and extreme daily precipitation—on per capita GDP. Drawing on future climate projections from CMIP6 models, they estimated the magnitude of possible damages. Their findings suggest that by 2049, under two extreme emissions pathways (RCP 2.6 and RCP 8.5), global losses are statistically indistinguishable. Compared with a counterfactual scenario without climate change, these damages translate into a permanent reduction of approximately 19% in global average per capita income (with a likely range of 11–29%).
Although per capita income is projected to continue rising relative to today’s levels, most regions will nonetheless experience permanent income losses. The estimated declines are around 11% in North America and Europe, while South Asia and Africa—the most severely affected regions—face losses of approximately 22%. These results highlight the uneven global distribution of climate damages and underscore the disproportionate vulnerability of low- and middle-income regions.
Comparisons between projected global climate damages and the costs of achieving the Paris Agreement’s mitigation targets reveal a striking imbalance. By 2050, the median projected economic damages from climate change are estimated to be about six times higher than the median costs of mitigation. This underscores that the economic damages of inaction far exceed the costs of mitigation, even though the net benefits of mitigation appear delayed. This delay arises because differences in climate damages between alternative emissions pathways are not statistically distinguishable until after mid-century. Beyond 2049, however, the estimates begin to diverge significantly, clearly demonstrating the long-term economic advantages of mitigation.
Figure 4.6: The commitment and divergence of economic climate damages versus mitigation costs

Source: Kotz et al. (2024), https://www.nature.com/articles/s41586-024-07219-0#Fig1

Mitigation vs. inaction costs
Looking further ahead, projections highlight even more dramatic risks. According to BCG (2025), if the global average temperature were to rise by 3°C by 2100, cumulative global economic output could decline by 15% to 34%. In contrast, under a best-case scenario where warming is limited to below 2°C, global GDP growth would slow by only about 0.02% per year relative to the baseline, leading to a cumulative GDP loss of no more than 4%.
Figure 4.7: On a 3°C warming trajectory, climate change could inflict economic damages amounting to as much as 34% of cumulative global GDP by 2100 
Source: BCG, https://web-assets.bcg.com/d7/d0/303ec1174bd5ab5aaeabb4c657b2/why-investing-in-climate-action-makes-good-economic-sense.pdf
Importantly, the costs of mitigation and adaptation are modest compared with the damages avoided. An investment equivalent to 1–2% of cumulative global economic output by 2100 in mitigation (through emissions reductions) and adaptation (through resilience-building measures) could place the world on a “below 2°C” pathway. Such an investment would allow societies to adapt to most, though not all, climate-related impacts. By contrast, the net cost of inaction—that is, the residual damages after accounting for the costs of mitigation and adaptation—ranges from 11% to 27% of cumulative global output.
This evidence makes clear that climate investment is not only environmentally necessary but also economically rational. Although the upfront costs of mitigation and adaptation may seem substantial, the long-term damages from inaction—spanning infrastructure destruction, productivity decline, and systemic financial instability—are likely to be far more severe. By curbing the scale of future losses, climate action ultimately frees resources that can be reallocated to other societal priorities such as health care, infrastructure development, and national security. The central policy challenge, therefore, is not whether climate change should be addressed, but how to design governance and financial mechanisms that translate today’s expenditures into sustainable economic and social benefits over the long run.
Figure 4.8: The projected net cost of inaction is estimated at 11% to 27% of cumulative economic output between 2025 and 2100

Source: BCG
Climate change is not merely an environmental challenge—it represents a systemic risk that penetrates and destabilizes entire social and economic systems, with far-reaching implications for policymakers, business leaders, and investors alike. The World Economic Forum has identified extreme weather events, critical changes to Earth systems, and pollution as key long-term global risks. 
From a financial and economic perspective, these risks manifest in multiple ways: climate shocks can undermine financial market stability by increasing volatility and threatening asset prices; they can erode corporate value through physical damages, supply chain disruptions, and reputational risks; and they reshape investment decisions as capital increasingly shifts toward sustainable assets and climate-resilient sectors. Taken together, these dynamics highlight how climate change is no longer a peripheral concern but a central factor in economic and financial stability—thus laying the foundation for a deeper exploration of the nexus between climate change and financial systems in the following section.
Climate change and financial systems
In the previous section, we briefly discussed the widespread impacts of global warming, air pollution, and extreme weather, examining how climate change affects economic output and highlighting the economic rationale and positive externalities of climate action. In this section, we delve deeper into the interactions between climate change and financial systems, the mechanisms through which climate risks are transmitted, and how financial instruments can be used to hedge these risks. 
Measuring climate risk
Current research increasingly converges on the view that climate-related risk factors can be broadly classified into two categories: physical climate risk and transition risk. 
Physical climate risk refers to the direct economic damage to productive assets caused by climate change. It can be further subdivided into:
Acute physical risks, driven by extreme weather events such as floods, hurricanes, and droughts.
Chronic physical risks, driven by longer-term shifts in climate patterns, including rising sea levels, temperature changes, and altered precipitation patterns.
When physical risks materialize, physical capital such as factories, infrastructure, and housing may be damaged. For example, global warming and sea-level rise are likely to reduce the value of coastal real estate, while potentially increasing the productivity of farmland or fisheries in colder regions.
Transition risk arises from the process of moving toward a low-carbon economy. Key drivers include policy changes, technological advances, and shifts in social norms and consumer preferences away from high-carbon activities. A common example is the introduction of a carbon tax to reduce greenhouse gas emissions. Such measures can significantly affect firms that hold stranded assets—for instance, oil and coal companies—by reducing profitability and market valuation. Conversely, renewable energy companies may benefit from these transitions.
When these risks materialize, the financial system—including assets across sectors—is affected through two primary channels: direct channels and indirect channels via the real economy (Furukawa et al., 2020). Although physical and transition risks are conceptually distinct—affecting different sectors and assets in different ways and not necessarily occurring simultaneously—they often interact. On one hand, the materialization of physical risks, such as natural disasters or gradual warming, can raise public awareness of climate change, prompting voters to demand abrupt or radical policy actions. These policy shifts, in turn, can amplify transition risks. On the other hand, transition risks can influence physical risks through their effects on the real economy and financial system. For example, the implementation of a carbon tax may incentivize firms and households to reduce emissions, thereby lowering the likelihood or severity of future physical climate impacts. 
The diverse types of climate risks, their dynamic interactions, and sectoral heterogeneity create significant challenges for quantifying exposures across financial assets. Moreover, because financial systems have only recently begun systematically integrating climate considerations, long-term time series data are limited, constraining empirical analysis.

Figure 4.9: Interaction between climate change and the financial system

Source: Furukawa et al. (2020), https://www.boj.or.jp/en/research/wps_rev/wps_2020/data/wp20e08.pdf
Climate risk modeling must contend with substantial uncertainty in climate outcomes, economic activity, and their feedback effects. Of particular concern are fat-tail risks—low-probability, high-impact events—which further complicate modeling and risk assessment. Consequently, our understanding of the effects, valuation, and pricing of climate risks within financial systems remains incomplete. As more comprehensive and timely data become available, research on climate-finance interactions is expected to advance substantially in the coming years. 
Climate risk and financial assets
Climate risks have significant implications for the prices of financial assets, which in turn affect the stability of the broader economy and financial system. When substantial climate risks exist, asset prices may experience pronounced volatility, negatively impacting the balance sheets of households, firms, and financial institutions. These effects can propagate to the real economy, potentially destabilizing financial and economic systems.
If climate risks are not properly incorporated into asset pricing, market valuations may become distorted, leading to misallocation of resources and market inefficiencies. Conversely, accurately pricing climate risks can help reduce the economic costs of climate change and incentivize climate action.
Understanding how and to what extent climate risks influence asset prices is therefore crucial, not only for individual portfolio management but also for the sustainable functioning of the entire financial system. In the following sections, we will explore whether—and to what degree—prices of various asset classes reflect climate change risks, as well as how financial institutions integrate these risks into their decision-making processes.
Climate risks in stock markets
In stock markets, share prices reflect both a firm’s fundamentals and investors’ expectations. If investors misprice or misevaluate firms’ climate risks, it may lead to suboptimal investment decisions, distorted market valuations, and reduced incentives for firms to adopt sustainable practices. 
Most research on firms’ climate risk exposure has focused on transition risks, using proxies such as carbon intensity, environmental performance, and regulatory risk. Studies examining the relationship between climate risk exposure and stock prices have employed various methodologies, including firm-level or portfolio-level analyses, event studies, and other empirical approaches. 
Currently, there is no consensus on whether climate risks are associated with a positive or negative equity premium. Some argue that if a firm is more exposed to climate change risks than its peers—such as through higher carbon emissions—investors should demand higher expected returns as compensation for bearing this additional risk. Empirical studies provide support for the existence of a positive carbon premium, suggesting that stock markets do, to some extent, price in climate-related risks. 
Using firm-level data, many researchers have examined the relationship between firms’ carbon emissions and their stock returns. For example, Bolton and Kacperczyk (2020) analyze over 14,400 firms across 77 countries during the period 2005–2018. They find a widespread carbon premium—that is, higher stock returns for companies with higher carbon emissions—across all sectors in Asia, Europe, and North America. Importantly, stock returns are not only related to firms’ direct emissions but also to indirect emissions along the supply chain. Moreover, the carbon premium has continued to rise in recent years, reflecting increased awareness of climate change risks and the growth of the sustainable investment movement. 
Figure 4.10: Firm-level stock returns (RET) and carbon emission levels

Source: Bolton and Kacperczyk (2020),  https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=3594188
In portfolio-level analyses, Hsu et al. (2023) document a positive spread in average returns between firms with high and low toxic emission intensity. This positive emission–return relationship is observed as a fairly persistent pattern across most years and appears largely unaffected by economic downturns, as indicated by NBER-designated recession periods. The authors propose a new systematic risk factor related to environmental policy uncertainty to explain this “pollution premium.” They find that the growth of environmental litigation penalties—representing regulatory regime change risk—helps price the cross-section of returns in emission-sorted portfolios.
Figure 4.11: Cumulative abnormal returns (CARs) for the high-minus-low emission portfolio

Source: Hsu et al. (2023), https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=3578215




Figure 4.12: Total emissions and the log growth in civil penalties

Source: Hsu et al. (2023)
A widely used method in climate finance research is the event study approach, which examines the causal impact of political events on asset prices. The introduction of climate-related policies or the occurrence of critical political events often depends heavily on the political and environmental context, causing transition risks to evolve discontinuously. Notable examples include the adoption of the Paris Agreement in 2015 and the election of U.S. President Donald Trump in 2016. 
For instance, Ramelli et al. (2021) analyzed the 2016 U.S. presidential election and the nomination of climate skeptic Scott Pruitt as head of the Environmental Protection Agency. These events drastically lowered expectations regarding U.S. climate policy. Consistent with conventional theory, the study found that stock prices of carbon-intensive firms—across nine “dirty” industries including power generation, petroleum and natural gas, refineries, chemicals, waste, metals, minerals, pulp and paper, coal mining, and electronics manufacturing—experienced a short-term price bump in response to anticipated laxer regulation. However, the cumulative abnormal returns (CARs) through the end of 2016 differed substantially from the immediate market reaction, suggesting that investors’ initial optimism under the new administration was excessive. 
Figure 4.13: The industry coefficients when regressing CAPM-adjusted returns on the day after the election (light blue bars) and through year-end 2016 (red bars) on Fama-French 30-industry dummies and firm characteristics

Source: Ramelli et al. (2021)
Interestingly, the study also observed relatively high returns for firms with strong climate responsibility, measured using net CSR scores and Energy Transition scores from two ESG providers. The authors argue that this pattern reflects long-horizon investors shifting toward climate-responsible stocks, anticipating a post-Trump rebound to green policies. 
This research highlights that political and policy shocks can have complex and sometimes counterintuitive effects on asset prices, with implications for both transition risk pricing and investment strategies. 
Figure 4.14: Cumulative CAPM-adjusted returns for high- and low-responsibility firms

Source: Ramelli et al. (2021), https://academic.oup.com/rcfs/article/10/4/748/6303621
While some studies document a positive carbon premium, other research finds evidence of a negative premium, particularly associated with physical climate risks. In such cases, sudden changes in investor perceptions of climate risk can lead to overestimation of potential losses, triggering a shift away from “risky” assets and a drop in stock prices. 
For instance, Choi et al. (2020) investigate investor attention to climate change at both city and country levels, using the number of Google searches on global warming-related topics as a proxy. They find that attention to climate change rises significantly following abnormally warm temperatures, indicating that individual investors update their beliefs about climate risk in response to local weather anomalies. 
The authors further examine the impact of local weather on stock prices. They find that stocks of carbon-intensive firms underperform those of low-carbon firms during periods of unusually warm weather. Using the EMC metric (Emission sectors minus Clean sectors) and analyzing the period 2001–2017, they compute abnormal returns and find abnormal returns decrease steadily across increasing temperature quintiles, with statistically significant underperformance observed in the warmest quintile. 
Figure 4.15: EMC on abnormal temperature, 2001–2017

Source: Choi et al. (2020), https://academic.oup.com/rfs/article/33/3/1112/5735304
Mechanistically, the study suggests that Google search activity is mostly generated by households and retail investors, who are often local and have limited attention. These investors react to extreme local weather rather than fundamental firm information. Upon observing unusually warm conditions, they update their beliefs about the value of carbon-intensive firms and may sell off such stocks. This research highlights that individual investors can collectively shape market beliefs and behavior regarding climate change, amplifying the pricing of physical climate risks. 
While individual investors often exhibit an exaggerated response to climate change risks, how do institutional investors react? Specifically, does climate change influence the behavior of institutional investors in the stock market? Many institutional investors, particularly long-term investors, large funds, and those oriented towards Environmental, Social, and Governance (ESG) factors, tend to prioritize addressing climate-related risks.
In this context, Alok et al. (2020) explore how the proximity of investment funds to disaster zones influences their response to climate-related events. They use the distance of funds from disaster-stricken areas to gauge the salience of the event for fund managers. Their study focuses on changes in portfolio weights, specifically examining how the stock holdings of firms headquartered in disaster zones, such as those affected by hurricanes or tornadoes, are adjusted post-disaster. To assess this, the authors apply a difference-in-differences approach, comparing the portfolio decisions of mutual funds headquartered within 100 miles of firms in the disaster zone (treatment group) with those of funds located further away (control group).
Figure 4.16: disaster zone stocks (e.g., stocks of firm A) in the portfolios of funds located near the disaster zone (e.g., fund 1) versus those located further away (e.g., fund 2) around the time of disaster events

Source: Alok et al. (2020), https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=3427903
Their findings reveal that, post-disaster, all funds—both close and distant—reduce their portfolio weights in disaster zone stocks. However, this reduction is significantly greater for funds located closer to the disaster zone (the treatment group). This behavior is attributed to a salience bias, which diminishes over time and with increasing distance from the disaster, rather than superior information possessed by managers of funds in proximity to the disaster. 
Figure 4.17: The average portfolio weights of disaster zone stocks for both the close fund-firm and far fund-firm groups (i.e., the treatment and control groups) across different quarters surrounding the disaster events

Source: Alok et al. (2020), https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=3427903
Specifically, a portfolio that takes a long position in disaster zone stocks, which experience the most significant reduction in weights in the portfolios of funds located close to the disaster zone, and a short position in those stocks that experience the least reduction, generates statistically and economically significant positive risk-adjusted returns after the disaster. This evidence supports the notion that mutual fund managers, influenced by salience bias, tend to oversell stocks within the disaster zone. As a result, they likely impose a performance "cost" on their investors by selling these stocks beyond what is justified by the value-relevant information of the disaster, driven by the additional psychological influence of salience.
Figure 4.18: Quarterly equal-weighted portfolio DGTW-adjusted returns

Source: Alok et al. (2020), https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=3427903
Climate risks in bond markets
Climate risks may also be priced in bond markets, particularly for municipal bonds. Municipal bonds are backed by local tax revenues, so when a municipality is more exposed to physical climate risks—such as rising sea levels or wildfires—its bonds are expected to reflect these risks in their pricing. 
Painter (2020) examines this phenomenon and finds that at-issuance yields and underwriting fees are higher for counties more likely to be affected by sea level rise. This effect is concentrated in long-term bonds and absent in short-term municipal bonds, indicating that climate risk pricing is primarily reflected in long-duration debt. 
Investor attention appears to be an important driver. On October 30, 2006, economist Nicholas Stern published a landmark report assessing the economic costs of climate change, known as the Stern Review. As one of the earliest and most comprehensive analyses of the economics of climate change, the Stern Review significantly heightened market and public attention to climate-related risks. 
Figure 4.19: Quarterly average search frequency for the term “climate change” surged following the Stern Review

Source: Painter (2020), https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=3167379
The difference in issuance costs between climate-exposed and non-exposed counties increases following the release of the 2006 Stern Review on Climate Change. Regression analysis of total annualized issuance costs on the interaction between climate risk and year-quarter dummies shows a clear increase in issuance costs for climate-exposed counties relative to non-exposed counties after the Stern Review’s release. 
These findings suggest that physical climate risks are reflected in bond market pricing, particularly for long-term municipal bonds, and that investor attention plays a critical role in amplifying the market’s recognition of climate-related risks. 
Figure 4.20: Climate risk and issuances costs around the Stern Review 
Source: Painter (2020)
Similar to equity markets, most studies on corporate bonds focus on transition risks, since firms’ exposure to physical climate risks may be limited by their location and their ability to relocate operations.
Seltzer et al. (2022) examine the relationship between corporate bond yields and firms’ environmental performance as well as regulatory exposure. They find that U.S. firms with poor environmental performance—including a larger carbon footprint—tend to have lower credit ratings and higher bond yield spreads. 
The authors further exploit the Paris Agreement (December 12, 2015) as a natural experiment, representing a shock to expected climate regulation. This event increased climate regulatory risks for high-emission firms or firms with generally poor environmental performance. Their analysis also provides evidence of a causal link from environmental profiles to both credit ratings and bond yield spreads. 



Figure 4.21: Environmental profiles and credit ratings 

 Source: Seltzer et al. (2022), https://www.nber.org/system/files/working_papers/w29994/w29994.pdf#page=39.75
Figure 4.22: Environmental profiles and yield spreads 


Source: Seltzer et al. (2022)
Importantly, this effect is especially pronounced for firms located in states with more stringent environmental regulations. The authors further note that changes in bond yield spreads are driven primarily by shifts in asset volatility rather than by changes in asset values, emphasizing that climate regulatory uncertainty directly influences bond rating and pricing.
Increasing awareness of carbon emissions has impacted both the trading behavior of institutional investors and the liquidity conditions of corporate bonds. Cao et al. (2021) found that institutional investors, such as mutual funds and insurance companies, tend to exhibit "sell herding" behavior by offloading bonds issued by high-carbon-emitting companies. Further analysis of two quasi-natural experiments—the Paris Agreement and the election of President Trump—revealed that institutional investors' levels of sell herding towards high-emission bonds were significantly intensified following the Paris Agreement, but diminished after Trump’s election. 
For insurance companies, a downgrade of investment-grade corporate bonds to high-yield status (often referred to as “fallen angels”) can trigger widespread selling. In the case of mutual funds, this behavior is partly driven by the sensitivity of end investors to the fund's carbon exposure. In response to redemption pressures, mutual fund managers tend to sell high-carbon bonds, which, in turn, leads to worse liquidity conditions for these bonds.
Figure 4.23: High carbon dummy and sell herding measure (SHM) of mutual funds and insurance companies  

Source: Cao et al. (2021), https://waf-e.dubuplus.com/apjfs.dubuplus.com/anonymous/O18C3YA/DubuDisk/public/cafm/2021/2-2%20Carbon%20Emissions,%20Institutional%20Trading,%20and%20the%20Liquidity%20of%20Corporate%20Bonds.pdf
Case: U.S. policy uncertainty and financial institutions
In the preceding discussion, we have seen that shifts in climate policy constitute a critical dimension of climate risk, exerting significant influence on stock markets, bond markets, and institutional investors. The United States provides a particularly illustrative case, given its electoral cycles and federal system, which often produce policy inconsistency and regional divergence. When political winds shift and institutional “red lines” are drawn, financial market participants must adapt, and climate commitments may evolve—or unravel—in response.
A striking example is the trajectory of BlackRock. In his 2020 letter to CEOs, Larry Fink, Chairman and CEO of BlackRock, famously stated that “our investment conviction is that sustainability- and climate-integrated portfolios can provide better risk-adjusted returns to investors” (Fink, 2020). Once a vocal advocate of ESG investing, Fink’s stance began to soften under rising anti-ESG pressure. By March 2022, his annual letter led with a personal anecdote about financial security and retirement, with “climate” mentioned only sparingly. By 2023, references to ESG disappeared entirely from his public statements, and in 2024 BlackRock formally dropped the term “ESG” from its communications and withdrew from the Climate Action 100+ coalition.
The retreat accelerated in January 2025, when BlackRock exited the Net Zero Asset Managers (NZAM) initiative. Around the same time, major U.S. banks—including JPMorgan, Citi, Bank of America, Morgan Stanley, Goldman Sachs, and Wells Fargo—withdrew from the Net-Zero Banking Alliance (NZBA). While many of these firms emphasized that their individual financed emissions targets remain in place, the exodus underscored the fragility of collective climate commitments under shifting political conditions.
Political risk has been a central driver. Fossil fuel industries, concentrated in Republican stronghold states, view ESG integration as a direct threat. In response, several states have introduced “anti-ESG” legislation, framing ESG considerations as discriminatory investment practices. According to U.S. law firm Ropes & Gray, by April 2025 more than 30 states had enacted or proposed restrictions, ranging from bans on ESG-based investment screening to sanctions on financial institutions perceived to boycott politically sensitive industries.
Figure 4.24: Navigating state regulation of ESG

Source: Ropes & Gray, https://www.ropesgray.com/en/sites/navigating-state-regulation-of-esg
This backlash highlights the polarization of climate finance in the United States. Just as issues such as abortion rights, immigration, and gender identity have become focal points of political contestation, ESG has emerged as a flashpoint in the 2024–25 election cycle. Yet beneath the political theater, one reality remains indisputable: while green investment returns may be uncertain, the long-term risks posed by climate change are tangible, material, and increasingly visible. This tension between political resistance and climate risks raises a critical question: can financial systems maintain credible, consistent pathways for climate action in the absence of stable policy support?
Green bonds, issued by firms or municipalities with proceeds explicitly earmarked for environmentally friendly projects (e.g., renewable energy or clean transportation), are also affected by climate-related risks.
Baker et al. (2018) examine a sample of over 2,000 U.S. municipal and corporate green bonds and find that green bonds are issued at a premium and trade at lower yields than comparable conventional bonds. Specifically, green bonds are issued at after-tax yields approximately five to seven basis points lower than ordinary bonds, with the strongest effects observed for bonds externally certified as green. The authors attribute these pricing differences to a subset of investors whose utility includes a non-pecuniary component, such as social responsibility derived from holding green bonds. Realizations of climate challenges may enhance the value of this non-pecuniary component, thereby introduce transition risks and affecting green bond valuations. 
Figure 4.25: After-tax bond yields at issuance (in basis points)

Source: Baker et al. (2018), https://www.nber.org/papers/w25194
Del Giudice et al. (2025) provide further evidence that climate change risk is indeed priced in the green bond market. They find that climate-related events, particularly severe natural disasters, lead to significant divergences in bond yields and liquidity. Following such events, brown bonds experience rising yields and deteriorating liquidity, whereas green bond yields remain stable or even decline. This pattern is consistent with investors shifting their preference toward green assets, reducing demand for brown bonds. The impact of disasters on green bond prices is temporary, typically fully reabsorbed by the fifth month after the event, and the effect diminishes as disasters become more frequent. These findings suggest a behavioral mechanism in which investors tend to overreact immediately after a disaster, but this overreaction fades as the event becomes less salient, rendering the price effect temporary.
Figure 4.26: Natural disasters and bond yields 

Source: Del Giudice et al. (2025), https://finanzasostenibile.it/wp-content/uploads/2024/08/Presentazione_Del-Giudice.pdf
Case: IFC (International Finance Corporation) and green bond market 
The International Finance Corporation (IFC) plays a crucial role in addressing climate change by facilitating private sector investments and innovative financing solutions. In the green bond market, IFC serves multiple functions, including that of an issuer, investor, and market builder. It works to strengthen the green bond market by both generating supply through its own green bond issuances and stimulating demand by investing in green bonds and green bond funds.
In 2010, IFC issued its first green bonds in response to growing investor demand for fixed-income products tied to climate-related investments. As of March 31, 2025, IFC had issued a total of $14.8 billion across 213 bonds in 21 different currencies.
