The proceeds from these green bonds are exclusively allocated to finance or refinance projects that significantly contribute to at least one of the following four environmental objectives: climate change mitigation, climate change adaptation, biodiversity protection, and ocean and water conservation.
Figure 4.27: Cumulative green bond issuance

Source: IFC, https://www.ifc.org/en/about/investor-relations/green-bonds
Climate risks and real estate markets 
Real estate markets are similarly sensitive to climate risks, particularly physical risks. Since property values are closely tied to geographic location, hazards such as floods, wildfires, and sea-level rise can have direct and substantial effects. If these risks are not reflected in property prices, it may encourage development in high-risk areas, threatening housing, infrastructure, and human safety. Moreover, when real estate is used as collateral in mortgage markets, accurate property valuations become critical. Overvaluation of collateral can lead to misallocation of resources, while sudden downward adjustments in property prices may threaten financial stability. Therefore, it is essential to investigate whether climate change risks are effectively priced into real estate markets.
Bernstein et al. (2019) provide empirical evidence that housing markets in the U.S. coastal regions do incorporate sea level rise (SLR) risk into property prices. Specifically, properties that are explicitly exposed to future inundation from SLR sell at an average discount of about 6.6% relative to nearby unexposed properties. The magnitude of the discount varies with the degree of exposure: homes projected to be inundated after 1 foot of global average SLR trade at a 14.7% discount, those inundated with 2–3 feet of SLR at a 13.8% discount, while properties at risk under 4–5 feet and 6 feet of SLR trade at discounts of 7.8% and 4.4%, respectively. Importantly, the SLR discount is concentrated in the non-owner-occupied segment of the housing market (e.g., second homes or investment properties), reflecting that market illiquidity and long investment horizons may amplify the salience of climate risk in pricing.
Furthermore, they examine whether the discount associated with SLR exposure is linked to regional beliefs about climate change, drawing on data from the Yale Climate Opinion Maps, which aggregate residents’ responses to the question “Are you worried about climate change?”. The results suggest that such belief-driven discounts are present only in the owner-occupied segment of the housing market, but not in non-owner-occupied properties. 
This indicates that the market for owner-occupied properties incorporates SLR risk only to the extent that local residents express worry about it. In contrast, non-owner-occupied property transactions appear to be driven more directly by the market’s expectations of future cash flows, rather than bilateral negotiations shaped by personal preferences and beliefs. Hence, investors’ ownership motives and perceptions of risk are crucial in determining whether climate risks are effectively priced. 

Figure 4.28: Feet of SLR until underwater and house price

Source: Bernstein et al. (2019), https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0304405X19300807
Figure 4.28: Transaction price and SLR exposed

Source: Bernstein et al. (2019)
Transmission to insurance and mortgage markets
Question: How do the effects of climate change on real estate propagate to the insurance and mortgage markets? 
As the frequency and intensity of climate-related disasters increase, the impact on real estate extends beyond the property value to the broader financial system, especially in the insurance and mortgage markets. Homeowners in high-risk areas are increasingly losing access to insurance or facing dramatically higher premiums. In some cases, insurance companies may refuse to provide coverage altogether, exacerbating the gap between the demand for property insurance and the availability of coverage.
For properties used as collateral in mortgages, this presents a dual risk: not only does the value of the property erode due to physical climate risks such as flooding or wildfires, but these properties may also be left “uninsured”, creating further exposure for lenders. This could lead to increased premium and defaults, higher credit losses, and greater financial instability for mortgage lenders and investors. 
Case: Climate risks and U.S. home values
In recent years, natural disasters such as the wildfires in California, hurricanes in Florida, and rising sea levels in Miami have displaced thousands of Americans, inflicting significant losses to both life and property. The increasing frequency of these climate events highlights the growing tension between climate change and real estate value. On one hand, the rising frequency of natural disasters has led to escalating payout costs for insurance companies, which in turn causes home insurance premiums to skyrocket. On the other hand, more people are starting to avoid purchasing properties in high-risk areas, further exacerbating the decline in property values. These two factors combined contribute to an overall decrease in real estate values. According to research by First Street, climate change could reduce the value of U.S. homes by $1.47 trillion by 2055.
First Street’s study predicts that average home insurance premiums will increase by 29.4% over the next 30 years. Furthermore, starting in 2025, around 19,000 home foreclosures in the U.S. will be directly or indirectly linked to climate risks, with expected credit losses of up to $1.2 billion. By 2035, this number will increase to nearly 84,000 foreclosures, totaling an estimated $5.4 billion in credit losses, accounting for nearly 30% of total mortgage foreclosure losses in the U.S. The most heavily impacted regions include Florida, Louisiana, and California—areas characterized by frequent natural disasters, low insurance coverage, and high real estate market activity.
Figure 4.30: Annual credit losses due to climate-related repossessions could reach $5.4 billion by 2035

Source: First Street, Financial Times, https://www.ft.com/content/c1d1ee7f-f34a-490d-bbd6-95b895ffd33e
Climate risks and derivatives markets
Options provide a form of insurance against tail and variance risks, with their pricing often influenced by general uncertainty. Ilhan et al. (2021) investigate whether climate policy uncertainty is priced in the options market. Their findings suggest that the carbon intensity of firms is primarily driven by industry characteristics. Additionally, the cost of option protection against downside tail risks is higher for firms with carbon-intensive business models.
The authors also highlight that heightened public attention to global warming increases the likelihood of pro-climate policies being adopted, which, in turn, leads to greater regulatory certainty. This, consequently, raises the cost of option protection against downside risks during periods when climate change garners more public attention. A key observation is that following the 2016 U.S. presidential election of Donald Trump, which temporarily reduced climate policy uncertainty, the cost of options decreased, especially for firms with high carbon intensity.
Figure 4.31: Effects of public attention to climate change

Source: Ilhan et al. (2021), https://www.zora.uzh.ch/id/eprint/234552/1/SSRN_id3204420.pdf
Weather derivatives, such as futures and options contracts linked to weather variables (e.g., temperature, precipitation, wind speed), can effectively hedge specific climate risks. The Chicago Mercantile Exchange (CME) pioneered the introduction of temperature-linked futures and options in 1999, marking the beginning of a market for weather derivatives.
The two main weather products are:
Cooling Degree Days (CDDs): Measure the need for cooling during hot temperatures, typically in summer.
Heating Degree Days (HDDs): Measure the need for heating during cold temperatures, typically in winter.
These contracts are especially useful for participants like insurance companies and firms aiming to offset weather risks. For example, an energy company might sell an HDD contract to protect against lower demand for heating oil due to a mild winter. Similarly, a citrus company might purchase an HDD contract to hedge the risk of a winter freeze damaging crops. These weather derivatives can be traded in advance of the actual weather event, providing a direct reflection of the market's expectations regarding climate conditions.
Figure 4.32: DALLAS CDD MONTHLY FUTURES - VOLUME & OPEN
INTEREST

Source: CME Group, https://www.cmegroup.com/markets/weather/cdd/dallas-cdd-monthly.calendar.html
The observation that financial markets, particularly futures prices, reflect the expectation of climate change—indicating a warming trend—suggests that the market as a whole is aligning with scientific consensus on climate change. Schlenker and Taylor (2021) investigate the accuracy of global climate models and how market participants incorporate these climate forecasts into their decision-making. Their research reveals that market expectations, as reflected in futures prices, are influenced by both short-term weather forecasts (for the next two weeks) and longer-term climate trends. Specifically, the study shows that futures prices respond more closely to climate model predictions than to actual weather station trends, indicating that market participants rely on scientific forecasts rather than historical observations when making trading decisions.
Notably, the futures market also incorporates emerging scientific findings, such as the impact of the polar vortex that can cause February cooling over the eastern U.S. This phenomenon, though not visible in climate model data or recent weather station observations, is reflected in futures prices, further supporting the idea that weather markets provide a forward-looking hedge against potential risks.
Figure 4.33: Cooling degree day (CDD) and heating degree day (HDD) contracts

Source: Schlenker and Taylor (2021), https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0304405X21001495
Question: Betting Against Warming Trend
The observation that futures markets align with climate model predictions, suggesting a broad consensus about the warming climate, does raise an intriguing question: Will anyone attempt to profit by betting against the observed warming trend? 
While the general trend toward warming appears firmly priced into futures markets, it is possible that some investors may attempt to profit from short-term fluctuations or anomalies in the climate trend. Think some examples of such scenarios.
Climate risks in banking sector
Banks, as the primary providers of credit in financial markets, play a central role in shaping the economic consequences of climate disasters. In this section, we focus on three interrelated aspects: (i) borrower performance following climate-related disasters, (ii) the banking sector’s role in facilitating post-disaster recovery, and (iii) how banks incorporate and price climate risk in their lending and portfolio decisions.
Gallagher and Hartley (2017) compare financial outcomes of residents living in areas with equal ex-ante flood risk. They find that delinquency rates increase significantly among residents in flooded areas: the probability of being 90 days past due is about 10% higher than for non-flooded residents during the year following Hurricane Katrina. Importantly, the delayed effect in delinquency rates is partly explained by the one-year grace period granted for most mortgages.
Flooded residents also experienced modest but persistent declines in their credit scores. Specifically, the credit scores of the most heavily flooded residents remained lower than those of non-flooded residents for about two years after Katrina. Although the effect was modest, this evidence highlights the broader financial strain imposed by natural disasters on households.






Figure 4.34: Change of delinquency rates for New Orleans residents 

Source: Gallagher and Hartley (2017), https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=2547662
Figure 4.35: Estimates for the Equifax Risk Score (TM) 

Source: Gallagher and Hartley (2017)
When natural disasters strike, both firms and households experience a surge in credit demand as they seek to rebuild destroyed or damaged capital. This demand is typically met through a combination of government transfers, insurance payouts, and, crucially, liquidity provision from banks. Since banks differ in their exposure to climate risks, those located outside disaster zones may possess stronger balance sheet capacity and thus play a critical role in post-disaster lending. Moreover, intra-bank capital markets enable the reallocation of funds across regions, helping to stabilize credit supply.
Koetter et al. (2020) analyze the case of the Elbe River flooding in Germany in May 2013 and study how German banks supported SMEs through recovery lending. They distinguish between banks directly exposed to the disaster—those holding credit portfolios with borrowers in flooded counties—and banks located in unaffected areas. The authors find that exposed banks outside the flooded areas substantially increased their lending after 2013, thereby supporting firms in affected counties through established lending relationships. In particular, SMEs connected to these out-of-county recovery lenders benefited significantly in terms of credit access.
The study further shows that local banks without access to geographically diversified intra-group markets faced heightened credit risk, as they lacked the ability to draw on resources from other regions. By contrast, membership in regionally diversified banking groups enhanced resilience, allowing local affiliates to mitigate shocks more effectively. These findings underscore that local lenders are central to recovery lending, especially for smaller firms, while geographically diversified banking groups provide an important buffer against regional climate shocks.




Figure 4.36: Classification of banks and firms across affected and unaffected counties

Source: Koetter et al. (2020), https://www.econstor.eu/bitstream/10419/191676/1/1047648482.pdf#page=25.09







Figure 4.37: The role of intra-group markets in mitigating local credit shocks

Source: Koetter et al. (2020)
Lenders are increasingly incorporating climate change as a critical risk factor in their pricing decisions. Using a firm’s headquarters location to measure its exposure to climate risk, Javadi and Masum (2021) find that firms located in high-risk areas—as proxied by drought conditions—face significantly higher loan spreads than firms in low-risk areas. Moreover, when a firm’s customers are more vulnerable to climate risk, the cost of borrowing also increases. Importantly, the adverse effect of climate risk is particularly pronounced for long-term loans issued to poorly rated firms. The spreads on such loans rose substantially over the 10-year window surrounding the Stern Review (2006), suggesting that the heightened awareness of climate risks following the report was reflected in credit market pricing. Overall, the evidence shows that banks incorporate climate risk into loan spreads and other contractual features of lending.

Figure 4.38: The difference in loan spreads following the Stern Review (2006)

Source: Javadi and Masum (2021), https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0929119921001401
Case: Climate as the “Sixth C” of credit
Traditionally, lenders evaluate borrower creditworthiness through the “Five Cs of Credit”—character, capacity, capital, collateral, and conditions. However, climate risk is rapidly emerging as a critical sixth dimension. Unlike conventional borrower-specific characteristics, climate risks are determined by a property’s geography. This introduces a location-based component of asset risk into credit-risk assessments. For example, two borrowers with identical credit scores, histories, and incomes could face substantially different odds of default if one lives within a 100-year floodplain while the other does not.

Figure 4.39: The 6th “C” of credit

Source: First Street, https://assets.riskfactor.com/media/The%2013th%20National%20Risk%20Assessment%20Climate,%20the%20Sixth%20C%20of%20Credit#page=2.09
Flood exposure erodes home values and increases uninsured repair costs, thereby amplifying financial strain even for otherwise low-risk borrowers. As demonstrated during Hurricane Sandy (2012), property-level climate exposure can translate into hidden credit losses that are not captured by standard underwriting. These risks will only intensify with the rising severity and frequency of climate-driven disasters.
Evidence from an ancillary analysis of 1.4 million loan transactions using a loan-level logistic regression model supports this view. When incorporating a property’s Flood Factor alongside traditional predictors, the results indicate that while loan-to-value (LTV) ratios remain the strongest predictor of foreclosures, Flood Factor is a close second. Specifically, each one-point increase in Flood Factor raises foreclosure odds by approximately 5%.
Taken together, these findings highlight the importance of treating climate exposure as a “Sixth C” in credit risk frameworks. By integrating high-resolution physical risk metrics such as Flood Factor into underwriting and stress testing, lenders can more accurately price risk, anticipate losses, and develop targeted mitigation strategies. This would not only safeguard financial stability but also enhance resilience in an era of mounting climate volatility.
Climate risks in insurance sector
Another important part of the financial system that is significantly exposed to climate change is the insurance sector. On the one hand, insurance serves as a mechanism for insureds to mitigate the impact of negative shocks caused by climate-related disasters. On the other hand, the sector itself can become a source of financial instability if large payouts threaten insurers’ solvency or trigger fire sales of assets. Moreover, insurance may generate moral hazard, delaying necessary adaptations to reduce long-term climate costs.
Insurance as a buffer. Empirical studies demonstrate that insurance can help mitigate the adverse financial consequences of climate disasters. Collier et al. (2025) examine the effects of Hurricane Harvey on local firms and find that delinquent credit balances doubled in severely flooded areas, while even nonflooded areas experienced significant impairments. Harvey also increased credit demand but revealed binding credit constraints for many firms. Importantly, firms that lacked insurance coverage were more likely to adopt recovery strategies with long-term negative consequences, such as neglecting debt payments or resorting to informal financing. Yet, insurance itself played a relatively small role in recovery: only 15% of surveyed firms used insurance to finance losses, with only 40% holding flood insurance and a fraction of insured businesses receiving claims payments. These findings highlight both the mitigation role of insurance and the barriers to effective insurance utilization.
The protection gap. Low coverage is not unique to Harvey. According to Swiss Re (2024), only 43% ($ 137 billion) of global economic losses from natural catastrophes were insured, leaving a protection gap of $ 181 billion. 
Recent data indicate that the overall damage from climate-related disasters continues to rise, while the insurance protection gap fell to a record low of 38% globally in the first half of 2025. This improvement was driven largely by strong insurance penetration in the U.S., where rising wildfire-related damages were increasingly offset by insured coverage.

Figure 4.40: Recovery funding sources
Source: Collier et al. (2025), http://faculty.bus.olemiss.edu/rvanness/Speakers/Presentations%202022-2023/HarveyRecovery_2022.pdf#page=30.74
Figure 4.41: Global natural catastrophe insured and uninsured losses, share of insured losses ($ bn, 2024 prices)

Source: Swiss Re, https://www.swissre.com/institute/research/sigma-research/sigma-2025-01-natural-catastrophes-trend.html
These trends reflect a growing maturity in the U.S. insurance market but also underscore persistent disparities across regions. Many countries in Asia, Africa, and Latin America continue to face heightened climate risks, where insurance penetration and risk modeling capabilities are limited. Thus, while progress has been made in narrowing the protection gap in advanced economies, there is a pressing need to scale insurance penetration and develop loss-mitigation strategies in underserved regions. Importantly, the role of the insurance industry must evolve from simply transferring risk to actively managing and mitigating climate risks. Such a shift is critical not only for reducing the economic shocks associated with future disasters, but also for strengthening long-term resilience and supporting sustainable development outcomes.
Figure 4.42: Wildfire-related damages are rising – but so are insured damages

Source: Swiss Re, World Economic Forum, https://www.weforum.org/stories/2025/08/global-insurance-industry-gap/
Questions: What are the underlying reasons for the persistently low levels of insurance coverage for climate-related risks? Through what mechanisms can insurance coverage be expanded to provide greater protection and enhance risk-sharing across regions and sectors? As insurance penetration increases, will insurance firms ultimately benefit from a broader risk pool, or will they face heightened exposure and financial vulnerability?
Insurance firms’ vulnerability to climate risks. While insurance serves as a risk-transfer mechanism for households and firms, insurers themselves are increasingly vulnerable to climate-related losses. When catastrophic events occur, insurers may be forced to engage in asset liquidations that could threaten both their solvency and broader financial stability.
Massa and Zhang (2021) examine insurers’ portfolio adjustments following Hurricane Katrina. They find that property insurance and reinsurance companies with the greatest exposure reduced their corporate bond holdings by 14%, compared with a 1% reduction among other property insurers, while life insurers increased their bond holdings. This pattern suggests that redemption claims surged immediately after Katrina, forcing highly exposed insurers to engage in fire sales of assets to meet liabilities. Importantly, the balance sheets of these firms showed limited cash buffers, indicating a reliance on investment assets (primarily bonds) and incoming policy premiums to fund unexpected payouts.
Figure 4.43: Change in high exposed property (Re) insurance bond holdings (2005Q2-2005Q3)

Source: Massa and Zhang (2021), https://www.cambridge.org/core/journals/journal-of-financial-and-quantitative-analysis/article/abs/spillover-effects-of-hurricane-katrina-on-corporate-bonds-and-the-choice-between-bank-and-bond-financing/66A7E1872A41AAD85B4BBBFD0D247501
Case: U.S. homeowners’ insurance
Evidence shows insurers are rapidly repricing and reassessing climate risk. Homeowners’ insurance costs in the U.S. have risen sharply and unevenly across regions. According to J.P. Morgan, while U.S. CPI inflation increased by about 23% between 2019 and 2023, homeowners’ insurance inflation surged by nearly 49%. 
Figure 4.44: While overall U.S. CPI inflation has eased, elevated home insurance inflation persists: Home insurance inflation and CPI (2013–2023)

Source: J.P.Morgan, https://privatebank.jpmorgan.com/nam/en/insights/markets-and-investing/ideas-and-insights/how-climate-risk-and-losses-are-creating-high-prices-for-home-insurance#fn2
Persistent underwriting losses from wildfires, hurricanes, and severe storms have already rendered some insurers unprofitable, particularly in the western, midwestern, and eastern regions of the United States. Looking ahead, insurers—leveraging the extensive data they collect on climate risks and property characteristics—are increasingly opting not to renew policies in areas where climate risks make it no longer economically viable to operate. These non-renewal decisions effectively reduce the supply of insurance coverage, which in turn drives up homeowners’ insurance costs as a function of supply and demand.
Figure 4.45: Counties with higher climate risk exhibit significantly greater insurance non-renewal rates

Source: J.P.Morgan, https://privatebank.jpmorgan.com/nam/en/insights/markets-and-investing/ideas-and-insights/how-climate-risk-and-losses-are-creating-high-prices-for-home-insurance#fn2
Moral hazard. Existing empirical evidence suggests that public insurance programs can induce moral hazard, whereas private insurance shows limited evidence of doing so. Annan and Schlenker (2015) investigate the U.S. federal crop insurance program and its impact on farmers’ incentives to adapt to extreme heat. Using county-level data on corn and soybean yields between 1989 and 2013 and the fraction of farmland insured under the program, they find that insured crops experience significantly larger yield declines under extreme heat than uninsured crops. This indicates that federal crop insurance may discourage farmers from adopting adaptation strategies to mitigate climate risks. Overall, this study highlights that poorly designed insurance schemes can inadvertently amplify the challenges of climate change by impeding necessary adaptation efforts.
Question: how should insurance programs be designed to minimize moral hazard while still providing adequate protection? 
Figure 4.46: Panel regression for corn yields 1989-2013

Source: Annan and Schlenker (2015), https://www.aeaweb.org/articles?id=10.1257/aer.p20151031
Climate regulations, tools, and future challenges
As climate risks intensify, the global financial system is under increasing pressure to mobilize capital toward a sustainable transition. Regulatory frameworks, financial instruments, and international cooperation have become the cornerstones of climate finance. This section outlines key developments in global climate regulations, emerging financial tools, and the challenges that will shape the future of this field.
Global frameworks
The United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (UNFCCC) established in 1992—shortly after the release of the first IPCC assessment report in 1990—laid the foundation for multilateral collaboration in addressing climate-related risks. With 198 Parties today, the Convention has become the primary global platform for negotiations. Each year, Parties meet at the Conference of the Parties (COP) to assess progress and negotiate coordinated responses. 
Figure 4.47: The 30th COP will be held in Belém, Brazil, in November 2025

Source: UN, http://un.org/en/climatechange/cop30
Building on this foundation, landmark agreements have emerged. The Kyoto Protocol (1997) introduced binding emission-reduction targets, while the Paris Agreement (2015) marked a breakthrough in collective ambition. Adopted by 195 Parties and in force since November 2016, the Paris Agreement aims to hold the increase in global average temperature “well below 2°C above pre-industrial levels” and to pursue efforts to limit it to 1.5°C. It operates on a five-year cycle of increasingly ambitious nationally determined contributions (NDCs). Importantly, the Agreement reaffirms the principle that developed countries should take the lead in providing financial assistance to vulnerable economies, underscoring the central role of climate finance in supporting mitigation and adaptation.
Figure 4.48: A framework from Paris Agreement

Source: UNFCCC, https://unfccc.int/process-and-meetings/the-paris-agreement
At COP15 in Copenhagen (2009), developed countries collectively pledged to mobilize $100 billion annually by 2020 to support developing countries. This goal, later reaffirmed in Cancun (2010) and Paris (2015), was only achieved in 2022, with developed countries providing $115.9 billion in climate finance. Yet the distribution remains uneven: only a fraction has reached low-income countries, and roughly one-quarter has gone to Africa. According to OECD estimates, excluding China, emerging markets and developing economies will require nearly $2.4 trillion annually by 2030 to meet climate targets—four times current investment levels.
Institutions such as the World Bank have taken an increasingly active role in filling this gap. The Bank has committed to aligning its financing flows with the Paris Agreement, directing 45% of its financing toward projects with climate benefits. For example, following the devastating 2022 floods in Pakistan, the World Bank supported the construction of weather-resilient housing in Sindh Province, benefiting 1,050,000 families and creating an estimated 1.1 million jobs across construction and supply chains. Such cases illustrate how climate finance can directly support both resilience and economic development.
Figure 4.49: Public climate finance (bilateral and multilateral attributable to developed countries) accounted for close to 80% of the total in 2022 and increased from USD 38 billion in 2013 to USD 91.6 billion in 2022

Source: OECD, https://www.oecd.org/en/topics/sub-issues/climate-finance-and-the-usd-100-billion-goal.html
Figure 4.50: Sindh flood emergency housing reconstruction project

Source: World Bank, https://projects.worldbank.org/en/projects-operations/project-detail/P180008

National policies
At the national level, countries have adopted diverse regulatory approaches to climate governance, reflecting differences in political systems, economic priorities, and technological capabilities. 
European Union (EU)
As a global leader in emissions reduction and climate action, the EU pursues an ambitious dual strategy of “internal emissions reduction + external carbon border tariffs”. The EU Emissions Trading System (EU ETS), its flagship carbon market, operates on a cap-and-trade mechanism for emission allowances covering energy-intensive industries and the power generation sector. The Fit for 55 packages further strengthened the ETS by introducing new reforms, such as extending coverage to the maritime sector and establishing a new carbon market (ETS II) for building and road transport fuels, set to launch in 2027/2028. Unlike the existing ETS, ETS II issues all allowances through auctioning, with no free allocations.
Figure 4.51: Fit for 55: reform of the EU emissions trading system

Source: EU, https://www.consilium.europa.eu/en/infographics/fit-for-55-eu-emissions-trading-system/
Complementing the ETS, the Carbon Border Adjustment Mechanism (CBAM) ensures that imported carbon-intensive goods bear a carbon price equivalent to that within the EU. Since its transitional phase began in October 2023, CBAM will become fully operational in 2026, requiring EU importers to purchase CBAM certificates aligned with EU carbon market prices. This mechanism helps prevent carbon leakage, supports the EU’s industrial green transition, and incentivizes cleaner production globally.
Figure 4.52: Carbon border adjustment mechanism

Source: EU, https://taxation-customs.ec.europa.eu/carbon-border-adjustment-mechanism_en
United States (US)
In contrast, U.S. climate policy has been shaped by sharp partisan divisions and significant regional variation. Federal commitments to international agreements have shifted with political leadership: the U.S. withdrew from the Paris Agreement in 2020, rejoined in 2021 under President Biden, and is set to withdraw again in January 2026 under the Trump administration.
Despite federal policy volatility, state-level initiatives remain robust. California’s Cap-and-Trade Program, a cornerstone of its climate strategy, distributes allowances through direct allocation and auctions, providing long-term regulatory certainty. Meanwhile, market-driven forces—such as the diffusion of green technologies and the rising competitiveness of clean energy industries—have reshaped U.S. industrial development and job creation. As a result, even amid federal reversals, states and corporations continue to pursue green transition strategies, giving U.S. climate policy a complex, multi-layered character influenced by politics, local interests, and technological change.
Figure 4.53: CARB allocates allowances for a given year by October 24 of the preceding year

Source: California Air Resource Board, https://ww2.arb.ca.gov/our-work/programs/cap-and-trade-program/allowance-allocation
China
China began carbon market pilots in 2013 and formally launched a unified national carbon market in 2017. The system follows a “quota allocation–market trading–compliance” cycle, currently covering the power sector but set to expand to steel, cement, chemicals, and other high-emission industries, thereby supporting the country’s dual-carbon goals.
China has also relaunched the Certified Emission Reduction (CCER) market. On January 22, 2024, the national voluntary carbon market resumed operations at the Beijing Green Exchange after a seven-year suspension. While no new CCERs have been issued yet, trading volumes of pre-2017 credits exceeded 19.6 million tons in 2024, demonstrating strong market demand and operational stability.
Figure 4.54: Transactions and prices during the first and second compliance cycles ofthe National ETS

Source: Ministry of Ecology and Environment ofthe People’s Republic of China, https://www.mee.gov.cn/ywdt/xwfb/202407/W020240722528850763859.pdf
Global financial supervision
Beyond national frameworks, international initiatives are increasingly shaping climate-related financial governance. The Network for Greening the Financial System (NGFS), founded in 2017 by eight central banks and supervisors, has grown to 147 members across more than 90 countries as of July 2025. NGFS focuses on the impact of climate change on both macro-financial stability and micro-prudential supervision. Its climate scenarios—covering both long-term transitions and short-term shocks—provide central banks and regulators with common reference points to assess climate-related risks and guide financial institutions toward sustainable investment and risk management practices. 
Figure 4.55: The NGFS long-term climate scenario

Source: NGFS, https://www.fsb-tcfd.org/
Disclosure standards
Frameworks such as the Task Force on Climate-related Financial Disclosures (TCFD), the new International Sustainability Standards Board (ISSB), and the EU’s Corporate Sustainability Reporting Directive (CSRD) have accelerated the shift toward standardized and transparent climate reporting. These frameworks reduce information asymmetry, enhance market efficiency, and help integrate climate considerations into corporate governance and strategy.
Many jurisdictions—including the EU, Japan, the United Kingdom, and New Zealand—have already incorporated climate disclosure into mandatory legal requirements, typically adopting internationally recognized standards such as TCFD and ISSB. These frameworks emphasize four pillars: governance, strategy, risk management, and metrics and targets. In April 2024, the Hong Kong Stock Exchange announced that climate disclosures would be elevated to mandatory requirements effective January 1, 2025, underscoring the growing global convergence around uniform reporting standards.
Figure 4.56: The TCFD’s framework 
Source: TCFD, https://www.fsb-tcfd.org/
Case: En-ROADS simulation: designing climate policy packages
EnROADS is a global climate simulator developed by the MIT Sloan Sustainability Initiative. It enables users to explore how dozens of policies and technologies—such as electrifying transport, pricing carbon, or improving agricultural practices—affect hundreds of interrelated factors, including energy prices, air quality, global temperature, and sea-level rise. By testing different combinations of interventions, the model helps decision-makers better understand the trade-offs involved in climate action and equips them with the knowledge to pursue the Paris Agreement’s goal of limiting global warming to well below 2°C.
For example, policy instruments such as carbon taxes, emissions trading systems, and renewable energy subsidies are critical levers for reducing greenhouse gas emissions and accelerating low-carbon transitions. With En-ROADS, users can simulate varying levels of policy intensity and assess their projected impacts on future emissions trajectories. Similarly, technological shifts—such as the widespread adoption of electric vehicles, the deployment of carbon capture and storage (CCS), or breakthroughs in clean energies—can be incorporated into the model to reveal how innovations reshape energy use, emissions pathways, and temperature outcomes. In this way, the tool allows for a systematic evaluation of both single policies and integrated policy packages, highlighting synergies and trade-offs while offering insights into potential costs and benefits.
Figure 4.57: En-ROADS climate solutions simulator
 
Source: EnROADS, https://www.climateinteractive.org/en-roads/
However, while the simulator provides valuable insights into physical and economic dynamics, the real-world feasibility of these policies and technologies remains a crucial consideration. When it comes to future technologies, should policymakers prioritize investments in speculative options like CCS and hydrogen, or rely on proven, scalable solutions such as solar and wind power? Which policy instruments are more politically and economically viable—ambitious carbon pricing schemes or targeted subsidies? And should developing countries shoulder the same carbon reduction costs as developed economies, given their different stages of industrialization and social needs?
These questions illustrate that effective climate policy requires not only technical modeling but also political judgment, economic pragmatism, and an appreciation of equity between nations. The ultimate challenge is to strike a balance between ambition and feasibility—designing pathways that achieve meaningful emissions reductions while remaining adaptable to diverse national contexts and development priorities.
Climate finance tools
Financial markets are simultaneously expanding their toolkit to address climate-related risks and opportunities. As discussed earlier, instruments such as green bonds, sustainability-linked bonds, and green loans channel capital directly into low-carbon projects. Meanwhile, carbon derivatives—including futures and options—are gaining traction as hedging instruments across both compliance and voluntary markets. 
Risk management practices are also evolving. Climate stress testing, scenario analysis, and ESG rating systems are increasingly adopted by financial institutions to capture both transition and physical risks. Insurance solutions, including climate risk insurance and catastrophe bonds, provide additional buffers against climate shocks, particularly in vulnerable regions.
Recent technological advances further enhance these practices. Artificial intelligence and big data analytics enable more precise modeling of climate risk, while blockchain technologies improve transparency and traceability in climate disclosures and carbon markets. These innovations will be discussed in greater detail in the next chapter, with a focus on how AI can help mitigate climate risks and promote sustainable development.
Future challenges and directions
Despite these advances, significant challenges remain. Regulatory fragmentation and inconsistent emission targets across countries undermine the effectiveness of global action. The shifting political dynamics in Europe and the United States further complicate the continuity of climate policies, which require long-term commitment and stability.
Financing divides between developed and developing economies also remain unresolved. At COP29, developed nations pledged to provide $300 billion annually by 2035 to support climate action in developing countries—a figure far short of the $1.3 trillion demanded by developing economies, leading to strong criticism and renewed tensions. Questions of climate finance and technology transfer thus remain politically sensitive.
Within financial markets, obstacles such as greenwashing weaken investor confidence, while the lack of standardized ESG metrics reduces comparability across jurisdictions. Liquidity constraints and risk premia in green assets may further limit large-scale capital allocation.
Looking ahead, several emerging trends are shaping the next stage of climate finance. Global carbon pricing is becoming a central focus, with mechanisms such as the EU’s CBAM pushing other countries to establish domestic carbon pricing schemes and explore cross-border coordination. The digitalization of carbon markets and the use of AI-driven forecasting models are likely to improve market efficiency and risk assessment. At the same time, achieving a low-carbon transition will require a just transition—ensuring the creation of decent jobs and high-quality employment while balancing decarbonization with social equity.
In sum, climate regulations and financial instruments are becoming indispensable in managing the multidimensional risks of climate change. Integrating climate risks into finance not only mobilizes resources for mitigation and adaptation but also strengthens the resilience of the global financial system. Future progress will depend on policy coordination, financial innovation, and the capacity to align global capital flows with the long-term objective of building a sustainable and climate-resilient economy.

Chapter 5
AI and Sustainability 
AI and climate change
AI (Artificial Intelligence) has emerged as a transformative force in the global economy, spurring investment, fostering innovation, and enhancing productivity across sectors(Cockburn et al., 2018; Furman & Seamans, 2019) (Agrawal et al., 2019). At its core, AI relies on algorithms and data to perceive, learn, and make decisions. The recent development of multimodal models has further expanded these capabilities by enabling the joint processing of text, images, audio, and video. These models can identify subtle emotional cues in speech or detect fine-grained visual features, allowing for more nuanced and context-aware analysis. By uncovering patterns and correlations within vast, complex datasets, AI systems produce predictions that increasingly guide strategic and operational choices.
Given these strengths, AI is often positioned as a key enabler of climate solutions: it can accelerate decarbonization, optimize energy transitions, and support broader sustainable development goals. Yet AI’s rise has also introduced new layers of complexity. Training and operating large-scale AI systems demand enormous computational resources, driving up energy consumption and associated emissions. This creates an inherent tension: can a tool that significantly increases environmental burdens still credibly contribute to global climate objectives? And if AI is to be integrated into sustainability frameworks, how must we reassess its risks, potentials, and long-term developmental pathways?
Despite these concerns, AI’s analytical power remains undeniable. In climate fields, AI enhances the quantification and forecasting of climate impacts, including extreme weather events, thereby improving the accuracy and usefulness of climate models. More reliable models strengthen prevention strategies, inform adaptation policies, and improve emergency preparedness—ultimately contributing to more robust climate risk management.
One notable example is ClimateAi’s FICE (Foundational Intelligence for Climate and Economics) model. FICE integrates aggregated consumer transaction data with macroeconomic indicators and embeds them into weather risk models within an AI framework. Crucially, it simulates how weather conditions shape consumer behavior, income fluctuations, and broader economic dynamics. Unlike many “black-box” AI systems, FICE provides transparent attribution, quantifying how much of an observed outcome can be traced to climate- or weather-related drivers.
Figure 5.1: ClimateAi help understand seasonal hurricane risks to supply chains

Source: ClimateAI, https://climate.ai/blog/keeping-the-lights-on-during-hurricane-ian-reflections-from-a-climate-tech-founder/
For instance, in its retrospective analysis of Hurricane Ian—the third-costliest hurricane in U.S. history—FICE revealed how companies such as Ace Hardware outperformed competitors like Home Depot and Lowe’s. Ace’s climate-resilient strategies, including decentralized decision-making, empowering local franchisees, and pre-positioning inventory in high-risk regions, were shown to be major contributors to its resilience. An eight-year analysis of climate performance further indicated that Ace consistently ranked at the top in operational robustness. These types of insights, once relegated to anecdotal interpretation or opaque internal assessments, can now be quantified and operationalized through AI-enabled climate analytics.
The implications extend well beyond a single case. As models like FICE mature, businesses gain the ability to plan more strategically for climate-related disruptions, potentially turning environmental volatility into a source of competitive advantage. Investors can evaluate portfolio resilience with greater precision, insurers can refine risk pricing, and governments can allocate disaster relief more effectively. In this sense, AI does not merely forecast climate pressures—it enables more adaptive, evidence-based responses to them. 
Rethinking AI’s roles in sustainability
Form a broader perspective, we will explore how AI promotes sustainable development across multiple dimensions. In the environmental domain, AI also offers powerful solutions for emissions monitoring, energy optimization and supply chains management. In traditional carbon monitoring systems, data primarily relies on self-reported emissions from enterprises, government verification, satellite remote sensing, and environmental station measurements. Such approaches often suffer from inaccuracy, time lags, and data fragmentation. AI can overcome these limitations by integrating satellite imagery, sensor data, and information from Internet of Things (IoT) devices to monitor carbon emissions and pollution sources in real time, thereby enhancing both the precision and timeliness of emissions tracking. 
AI can be deployed to reduce overall greenhouse gas emissions by optimizing energy consumption patterns. In 2016, Google, in collaboration with DeepMind, developed an AI-driven cooling control system for data centers to enhance energy efficiency and reduce CO₂ emissions. Every five minutes, the AI system collects operational data from thousands of cooling system sensors and uses deep neural networks to predict the impact of various operational combinations on future energy consumption. It then identifies the optimal set of actions that minimize energy use while meeting safety constraints, which are subsequently verified and executed by the local control system. Over nine months, the AI control system improved its performance from a 12% to approximately a 30% reduction in cooling energy use, ultimately achieving a 40% reduction in cooling energy consumption and a 15% improvement in Power Usage Effectiveness (PUE). This translates into annual savings of hundreds of millions of kilowatt-hours of electricity.
Figure 5.2: AI performance and training data

 Source: Google, https://deepmind.google/discover/blog/safety-first-ai-for-autonomous-data-centre-cooling-and-industrial-control/
Beyond cooling optimization, AI-driven energy scheduling systems can enhance the utilization efficiency of renewable energy. By integrating forecasts of renewable energy generation, grid load, electricity prices, and other relevant factors, the AI system optimizes electricity usage plans—ramping up non-critical loads when solar or wind generation is abundant, and scaling them down during periods of low renewable supply. This approach not only improves renewable energy utilization but also reduces operational costs.
Moreover, AI can be employed in supply chain tracking and transportation route optimization, enabling end-to-end visibility and operational efficiency. Through scenario-based simulations, AI can evaluate the potential impacts of various strategies—such as different sourcing or logistics configurations—on both carbon emissions and costs. Based on these insights, the system can recommend optimized, data-driven solutions that reduce emissions, minimize waste, and improve overall resource efficiency.
On the social front, Artificial Intelligence (AI) is emerging as a powerful enabler of educational inclusion, human rights protection, financial inclusion, and social equity. AI-powered personalized learning platforms lower the barriers to accessing knowledge, enabling students in underdeveloped regions to benefit from high-quality educational resources. For example, Khan Academy’s Khanmigo, developed with GPT-4, can engage in interactive dialogues with students, pose guiding questions, and track individual learning progress in real time. This significantly reduces the cost of delivering personalized education and extends its reach to a broader population.
Figure 5.3: Khan Academy’s Khanmigo

source: Khan Academy, https://blog.khanacademy.org/navigating-cheating-in-the-age-of-ai/
In labor rights protection, companies can deploy AI to analyze employee feedback, social media content, and news reports to monitor workforce sentiment and satisfaction levels, as well as to detect potential risks such as labor disputes or gender discrimination. For instance, SAP SuccessFactors’ Experience Management System integrates natural language processing (NLP) to identify emotional patterns hidden within employees’ textual feedback, enabling early detection of dissatisfaction or anxiety. This facilitates proactive interventions to improve retention and well-being. Furthermore, incorporating large language models into recruitment processes can automate candidate screening and assessment, thereby reducing subjective bias related to gender, educational background, or age, and advancing diversity and inclusion in hiring.
In the realm of financial inclusion, AI-powered credit risk models help financial institutions assess the creditworthiness of individuals with limited or no formal credit history. Digital lending platforms such as Tala apply machine learning algorithms to analyze alternative data sources from borrowers’ mobile devices—such as transaction patterns and communication behaviors—to generate credit assessments. This approach expands access to credit for individuals traditionally excluded from formal financial services, thereby strengthening inclusive finance, particularly in developing economies.
In corporate governance, AI’s ability to process vast and complex datasets not only enhances the efficiency of information acquisition and analysis but also improves transparency and risk management. By enabling early detection of potential threats, AI supports proactive decision-making and strengthens modern governance practices. Leveraging big data and AI technologies, companies can identify early signs of financial fraud, reputational risks, and ESG-related exposures. This foresight allows organizations to conduct timely risk assessments and strategic planning, ensuring decisions are forward-looking, adaptable, and sustainable.
In the area of information disclosure, AI can significantly improve efficiency while reducing the cost of ESG compliance. Through natural language processing and machine learning, AI can quickly extract critical insights from diverse and heterogeneous data sources—such as corporate disclosures, news media, environmental monitoring data, and employee welfare records. This capability streamlines data collection and supports model development, enabling the rapid drafting of ESG reports. Furthermore, AI can assist in monitoring policy changes, aligning disclosures with varying regulatory requirements across jurisdictions, and producing automated, multilingual, and multi-version ESG reports. Such functionalities allow multinational corporations to conduct cross-market benchmarking and substantially lower their ESG compliance costs. For instance, Datamaran leverages AI technologies to collect and analyze ESG data, enabling real-time monitoring of ESG risks and opportunities. It provides standardized reports and decision-making support aligned with different international frameworks.
Figure 5.4: Datamaran AI – powered Smart ESG platform

Source: Datamaran, https://www.datamaran.com/esg-software
AI is transforming sustainable investment approaches as well. Asset managers increasingly use AI to monitor firms’ ESG performance, extract relevant investment factors, and identify new opportunities for sustainable growth. In quantitative investing, large language models (LLMs) analyze analysts’ earnings calls, corporate announcements, and news coverage to identify potential ESG risk factors. These insights, combined with traditional financial metrics, are used to optimize investment portfolios and ESG strategies.
For instance, Huang et al. (2023) developed FinBERT, a finance-specific large language model based on BERT. FinBERT is trained on financial texts and fine-tuned for tasks such as sentiment analysis and ESG classification. The study finds that FinBERT significantly outperforms the Loughran and McDonald dictionary and other machine learning algorithms in correctly identifying positive or negative sentiments in sentences that other models often misclassify as neutral. This improved performance is attributed to FinBERT’s ability to capture contextual information in financial texts. Moreover, FinBERT excels at detecting discussions related to environmental, social, and governance issues. Their results demonstrate strong potential for enhancing market information processing and optimizing investment strategies. 
AI in sustainable finance
AI in corporate finance
Artificial Intelligence (AI) has become a transformative force in the global business landscape, exerting significant influence on corporate Environmental, Social, and Governance (ESG) performance. A. Wang et al. (2024) employ text analysis techniques to construct an AI index from key terms in corporate annual reports. Furthermore, they assess firms’ environmental performance across four dimensions: information disclosure, work rewards and punishments, nonprofit organizations, and pollution prevention. Their findings indicate that AI significantly improves corporate environmental performance, with stronger effects observed in state-owned enterprises and firms operating in heavily polluting industries. Building on this foundation, the following sections will delve into both theoretical frameworks and practical applications, examining how AI technologies contribute to environmental governance, social responsibility, and corporate governance—thereby advancing corporate sustainability.
Energy usage
AI can assist companies in optimizing energy usage and reducing overall carbon emissions.  Luqman et al. (2024) outline both direct and indirect applications of AI in supporting firms to reduce carbon emissions and achieve carbon neutrality. Key strategies include monitoring energy consumption patterns, detecting unjustified or excessive usage, and optimizing machine configurations and production processes to minimize energy waste and associated emissions.
Figure 5.5: Direct and indirect emission control measures

Source: Luqman et al. (2024), https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1002/bse.3689
In practice, firms can apply AI to optimize energy use by deploying smart sensors that continuously track consumption patterns. Data from these sensors are processed by AI algorithms to forecast energy demand, enabling dynamic adjustments to operational schedules and usage plans that reduce waste, improve efficiency, and lower emissions. A prominent example is Google’s AI-powered data center cooling system, which has substantially enhanced energy efficiency while cutting carbon dioxide emissions. Furthermore, AI can predict the supply and demand of renewable energy, mitigating its inherent intermittency and enabling optimal integration into energy systems. Such capabilities facilitate coordinated energy scheduling and management, thereby reducing overall costs and carbon footprints.
Siemens’ Smart ECX platform exemplifies this approach. By integrating AI algorithms with a comprehensive energy management system, intelligent power distribution simulations, maintenance services, and smart control products, Smart ECX accurately forecasts energy demand and supply, maximizing overall energy efficiency through source–load interaction. The platform also enables enterprises to monitor energy and carbon data in real time, conduct accurate carbon inventories, and develop science-based emission reduction strategies—creating a closed-loop carbon management process.
Smart ECX has already delivered measurable emission reductions in multiple projects. For instance, Siemens Numerical Control (Nanjing) Co., Ltd. (SNC)—Siemens’ first fully digitalized factory—utilizes Smart ECX for comprehensive, precise energy management and carbon footprint tracking. The system enhances the stability and reliability of solar and energy storage supplies, ensuring 100% green electricity consumption. This implementation is projected to save over 5 million kWh of electricity, 6,000 cubic meters of water, and cut approximately 3,300 tons of carbon emissions annually—equivalent to about 400 round-trip flights between Munich and New York City.
Figure 5.6: Streetlights powered by solar and wind energy

Source: Siemens, https://www.siemens.com/global/en/company/about/businesses/real-estate/info-center/digital-native-factory-nanjing.html

Supply chain management
Artificial Intelligence (AI) plays a vital role in promoting sustainable supply chain management. Nahr et al. (2021) proposed a green supply chain framework based on Artificial Intelligence of Things (AIoT), which facilitates real-time monitoring of goods’ flow and quality while helping managers ensure compliance with environmental regulations and emission limits. By integrating AIoT sensors for supply chain management and asset tracking, firms can obtain accurate insights into resource consumption, including electricity and water usage. This enables optimization of logistics routes and operational strategies, thereby improving energy efficiency and reducing overall energy consumption. Such frameworks offer valuable guidance for the adoption of AI technologies in advancing green supply chain management.
Figure 5.7: A framework for a green supply chain based on AIoT

Source: Nahr et al. (2021), https://ijimes.ir/index.php/ijimes/article/view/18
An applied example is found in over 7,500 Starbucks stores across China, where core equipment is connected to Envision EnOS through intelligent IoT management terminals. This integration allows comprehensive monitoring and management of energy, water, and carbon emissions. Looking forward, Envision is collaborating with Starbucks China to develop a supply chain carbon management platform covering 100% of directly sourced suppliers and major indirect procurement suppliers. This platform will enable precise calculation of the carbon footprint for thousands of items across the entire supply chain, pioneering a collaborative decarbonization model throughout the value chain.
Beyond environmental monitoring, AI also enhances social responsibility within supply chains. Firms leverage AI to analyze global news, third-party audit reports, and publicly available social media data to perform real-time, multidimensional risk assessments of suppliers, focusing on labor conditions, health and safety, and wage compliance. This proactive approach shifts social responsibility management from reactive crisis responses to preventive measures, thereby strengthening supply chain sustainability and fostering responsible sourcing. However, social and environmental performance data from suppliers are often confidential, posing a significant challenge: How can companies access accurate and reliable data to effectively integrate into supply chain risk management frameworks?
Climate resilience
AI serves as a powerful tool for enhancing corporate resilience to climate change. By integrating satellite remote sensing data with machine learning algorithms, firms can predict the impacts of climate-related extreme events such as floods and droughts. These forecasts enable the development of targeted disaster prevention and mitigation strategies, reducing associated risks. The Foundational Integrated Climate-Economy (FICE) model, developed by ClimateAi, further supports firms in evaluating their performance during climate events and designing effective response plans. Han et al. (2025) find that companies investing in AI demonstrate stronger valuation resilience amid natural disasters. Notably, firms where AI-related roles constitute 2.4% of total job demand recover production and operations faster, effectively offsetting valuation losses within a short disaster window.
Human resource management
Artificial Intelligence (AI) is profoundly transforming business development and operational practices, playing a crucial role in human resource management. The use of AI in recruitment has rapidly increased, reshaping how candidates apply and how hiring decisions are made. Importantly, compared to traditional recruitment processes often affected by human biases related to gender, education, or race, AI has the potential to improve algorithms and models to mitigate or even eliminate such biases, thus promoting diversity and fairness.
Avery et al. (2024) find that women’s expectations of bias from human evaluators lower their likelihood to apply for jobs and complete interviews. However, when recruiters experienced in the technology sector use AI-generated applicant scores, these scores mitigate gender bias typically present in traditional evaluations. Further analysis shows that only when AI tools exhibit severe bias against women—around 25%—does female representation decline, primarily in highly selective roles.
Figure 5.8: The density of evaluations in the Human-Demand and AI-Demand treatments by applicant gender

Source: Avery et al. (2024), https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=4764343
Since 2016, Unilever has implemented AI algorithms globally to optimize its recruitment process, employing tools such as Pymetrics and HireVue for candidate assessment and interviewing. Applicants initially complete gamified tests designed to evaluate logical reasoning and cognitive skills. In the subsequent AI-powered video interview phase, machine learning algorithms analyze vocal tone, body language, and other behavioral indicators to generate detailed evaluation reports that support interviewers in initial screening. This AI-driven approach has reduced evaluation time by 75%, provided feedback to all candidates to enhance transparency and fairness, and significantly improved diversity and inclusion by mitigating unconscious biases during early recruitment stages.
Figure 5.9: The simulated fraction of female applicants (y-axis) across different score quantiles (x-axis)

Source: Avery et al. (2024)

Beyond recruitment, AI supports employee development through personalized learning experiences delivered via online courses, digital avatars, and dynamic assessments. These technologies lower barriers to knowledge acquisition, improve skills and competencies, and help employees better prepare for future challenges. Brynjolfsson et al. (2025) conducted a study on the implementation of a GPT-3-based assistant among over 5,000 customer service agents at a Fortune 500 company. The results showed an average productivity improvement of 15%, with less experienced workers experiencing gains up to 30%, while highly skilled agents benefited to a lesser extent. The study further demonstrated that AI assistance enhances employee learning and English language proficiency, with especially pronounced effects for international customer service representatives.

Consulting firm Accenture acquired the online education platform Udacity and subsequently launched Accenture LearnVantage, an AI-native learning platform. Later, Accenture partnered with ETS to integrate ETS’s talent assessment solution, FutureNav, into LearnVantage, creating a comprehensive AI-driven talent management system. This platform personalizes learning pathways based on employees’ existing skills, progress, interests, and career objectives. It supports employees in aligning with relevant job roles, enhancing technical competencies, and cultivating a more adaptable workforce, thereby promoting sustainable employee development.

AI also plays a significant role in enhancing employee job satisfaction. Companies can leverage AI to analyze data on employee sentiment, health, compensation and benefits, work conditions, and career development, thereby identifying potential issues and employee needs to improve overall satisfaction and well-being. Chiong & Xie (2024) analyzed employee reviews from crowd-sourced job platforms such as Glassdoor and found that generative AI has a positive overall effect on job satisfaction, with the greatest gains in occupations with moderate AI exposure. Moreover, they found that these effects stem mainly from negative job security sentiment in high-exposure roles and positive compensation sentiment in moderate-exposure roles, while work-life balance and career opportunities remain largely unchanged.
Microsoft’s employee experience platform, Microsoft Viva, integrates work, learning, and management to enhance employee well-being and productivity. Viva Insights provides personalized recommendations for individuals, managers, and organizational leaders, helping to balance work efficiency with employee happiness, foster better work habits, and improve business outcomes—ultimately supporting the growth of both employees and organizations. 


Figure 5.13: Effect of Generative AI on Determinants of Job Satisfaction

Using AI to analyze data from Outlook and Teams—such as task flows, meetings, and emails—Viva Insights offers employees suggestions on protecting time for regular breaks and scheduling focused work and learning periods. This helps employees better understand their work patterns, improve well-being, and boost productivity. Importantly, these personal insights are visible only to the individual employee, preserving privacy through data aggregation and de-identification.
At the managerial level, Viva Insights offers team- and organization-wide trend analyses and actionable advice to balance productivity with employee welfare. The platform identifies potentially harmful work habits—like after-hours work, excessive meetings, and limited focus time—and equips managers to foster a healthier, more balanced team culture that enhances both efficiency and mental well-being.
Viva Learning facilitates seamless professional skill development by integrating learning opportunities directly into employees’ workflows. It consolidates premier educational resources, including LinkedIn Learning, Microsoft Learn, and Skillsoft, to promote active and ongoing engagement in continuous learning. Additionally, Viva Learning enables the creation of personalized learning academies that deliver targeted content customized by role, team, or topic. This capability allows organizations to curate, assign, and monitor learning activities aligned with both business objectives and individual development goals, thereby enhancing workforce skills and organizational capacity.

ESG management
The rise of AI is fundamentally transforming how corporations collect, analyze, and transmit information. AI-driven data analytics are increasingly adopted to gather and process complex, alternative data for ESG disclosures. Machine learning and AI excel at integrating multisource data, identifying patterns, and making predictions—capabilities essential for improving the efficiency and transparency of ESG reporting. In ESG disclosure, AI leverages natural language processing (NLP) and machine learning to automatically consolidate information from diverse sources such as news reports, financial statements, and ESG monitoring data. This enables rapid extraction of key information from unstructured text, data cleaning, and standardization, while providing trend forecasts on market and corporate ESG performance and efficiently generating ESG report drafts. Moreover, AI can continuously track updates to relevant ESG regulations in real time—adjusting report language and structure according to specific industry verticals, organizational maturity levels, and legislative frameworks—to ensure compliance with regulatory requirements.
Figure 5.16: How generative AI support each phase of the ESG workflow within firms

Source: AWS, https://aws.amazon.com/cn/blogs/machine-learning/the-executives-guide-to-generative-ai-for-sustainability/
Amazon Web Services (AWS) offers a Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) service that uses generative AI to query sustainability documents and generate ESG reports. This service can browse and summarize a variety of ESG information, including corporate reports, regulatory filings, and industry standards, providing key insights to meet companies’ ESG reporting needs.
AI can identify potential ESG risk events based on real-time public sentiment and corporate indicators, conduct scenario analyses, and provide more comprehensive and timely information to support corporate decision-making. This capability helps shorten strategic response times and enhances strategic agility and competitiveness. Campello et al. (2023) propose AlphaManager, a model that utilizes deep learning combined with offline reinforcement learning to analyze managerial decisions and firm performance. By integrating firm fundamentals, market returns, and macroeconomic variables, the model generates high-dimensional, dynamic, and counterfactual insights. Their findings highlight AI’s capability to enhance strategic decision-making, offering more effective guidance for corporate policy and demonstrating AI’s potential to improve firm outcomes. 
Figure 5.17: The long-term performance of AlphaManager trained with short-term market capitalization growth (left) and enterprise value growth (right) as objective functions

Source: Campello et al. (2023), https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=4590323
Looking ahead, the growing integration of AI in decision support raises critical questions: To what extent will AI shape the future of corporate strategy? Will it challenge the traditional role of human managers, or primarily serve to enhance and augment managerial capabilities? Under a human-AI collaborative framework, what role should human managers play? How can the rationale behind AI-driven decisions be effectively explained, and in cases of erroneous outcomes, who bears ultimate responsibility?
AI-driven ESG data analysis
With the rapid advancement of artificial intelligence, AI has permeated various sectors of the financial industry—from customer service and risk management to product innovation and investment decision-making. It is fundamentally reshaping the financial ecosystem. In today’s digital economy, financial markets are influenced not only by traditional metrics such as earnings and interest rates but also by vast amounts of structured and unstructured data. AI empowers investors and institutions to analyze diverse data sources—ranging from real-time social media feeds and satellite imagery to annual reports and ESG disclosures—transforming raw data into actionable, predictive insights.
In sustainable finance, ESG data collection and analysis face significant challenges due to fragmented sources, unstructured formats, and delayed updates. Primary data stems from corporate self-disclosures—covering carbon emissions, energy consumption, and employee welfare—found in company websites, annual and sustainability reports; third-party ESG providers such as NGOs and government agencies; and real-time ESG signals from news, social media, and company reviews. Many ESG data vendors manually gather, organize, and analyze these diverse inputs, resulting in inherent data latency. Additionally, voluntary disclosures often lead to incomplete datasets. The lack of unified classification standards further complicates the landscape, as ESG reporting requirements vary across industries and regions, while regulatory frameworks continually evolve.
Traditional ESG data processing requires considerable human labor and time, with a non-negligible risk of errors introduced by manual execution. AI technologies, leveraging natural language processing (NLP), computer vision (CV), and intelligent document processing (IDP), have revolutionized this space by automating the extraction of structured information from heterogeneous sources such as annual reports, news articles, and social media. Machine learning models can then identify patterns and similarities within the data to generate more accurate estimates and fill data gaps. As a result, such AI-enhanced ESG datasets are now widely adopted by rating agencies, institutional investors, banks, and other financial institutions.
Table 5.1: AI applications in data collection and analysis
Data Type
Specific Content
AI Application Areas
ESG Disclosure Data
Corporate annual reports, CSR reports, ESG databases (e.g., MSCI, Sustainalytics)
ESG rating, responsible investment, green asset screening
Unstructured Text Data
News, social media, NGO reports, policy announcements
Greenwashing detection, sentiment monitoring, social event response
Carbon Emissions and Climate Data
Government platforms (e.g., EU ETS), CDP questionnaires, satellite remote sensing
Carbon risk modeling, green credit approval, climate scenario analysis
Corporate Operational Data
Energy consumption, water usage, transportation data, supply chain footprints
Supply chain management, environmental performance evaluation
Individual Behavioral Data
Bank transactions, loan records, insurance claims, mobile device and consumption data
Financial inclusion, green finance product design
Regulatory Data
Policy texts, compliance disclosures, historical penalties
Compliance monitoring, risk prediction, regulatory sandbox modeling
Socioeconomic Data
Population, education, income, digital penetration rates
Financial inclusion assessment, digital divide identification

In inclusive finance, AI transcends the traditional reliance on credit reports and collateral by leveraging multi-source, non-traditional data—such as transaction behavior, social networks, and regional macroeconomic indicators—to more accurately assess the creditworthiness and needs of individuals and enterprises. This approach enhances financial inclusion and enables more personalized financial services.
These capabilities empower financial institutions to gather and analyze large-scale, multi-source, and heterogeneous data, enabling a comprehensive understanding of individual behaviors and market dynamics. AI plays a pivotal role in improving ESG scoring, promoting sustainable investments, expanding credit access for underserved populations, designing green and personalized financial products, and ensuring compliance with environmental and social regulations. As such, AI has become a crucial catalyst for advancing green, inclusive, and sustainable finance.
Visalli et al.(2023) propose an ESG data collection method based on Altilia Intelligent Automation (AIA), an AI-driven Intelligent Document Processing (IDP) platform. The AIA platform integrates hybrid and adaptive AI paradigms, utilizing deep learning techniques in Computer Vision (CV) and Natural Language Processing (NLP) to aggregate diverse documents from disparate sources. These sources include annual reports, sustainability reports, notes to financial statements, news articles, NGO reports, and company websites accessed via web scraping. This approach transforms unstructured documents into structured data, enabling detailed company profiles that serve banks, wealth management firms, rating agencies, investors, and business analysts. The AIA platform implements an end-to-end ESG data collection workflow encompassing stages of data gathering, ingestion, recognition and extraction, validation, and utilization.
Saxena et al. (2023) examine the integration of Industry 4.0 technologies in ESG data collection and evaluation. IoT sensor networks enable real-time, precise monitoring of environmental and social indicators—such as air quality, carbon emissions, and health metrics. These raw data undergo filtering and standardization before being transmitted to centralized databases via Web APIs. Subsequently, suitable machine learning models are selected and trained on the datasets using various algorithms to identify the optimal model. Once trained, the model analyzes the data to uncover patterns and forecast future trends. This AI-driven framework enhances ESG metric assessment, enabling financial institutions to gain deeper insights into corporate sustainability performance.

AI empowering ESG ratings
In ESG ratings, AI integrates technologies such as the Internet of Things (IoT), blockchain, and natural language processing (NLP) to acquire corporate ESG data from diverse channels, thereby enhancing data coverage, timeliness, and reliability. It then cleans, standardizes, and extracts key features to assess corporate performance across environmental, social, and governance dimensions. Leveraging established rating frameworks and analytical models, AI generates comprehensive ESG scores. Furthermore, AI enables real-time monitoring of ESG performance through tools like satellite imagery and sentiment analysis, detecting emerging events and hidden risks. This supports dynamic, cross-regional ESG scoring and provides a robust technological foundation for continuous rating updates. 
Morningstar Sustainalytics has incorporated AI technologies since 2014, building the first document retrieval and classification models, subsequently integrating deep learning capabilities and leveraging state-of-the-art large language models (LLMs) to enable natural language generation (NLG). Throughout its information value chain, Morningstar Sustainalytics employs NLP, machine learning (ML), and symbolic AI techniques to automate document management, data extraction, and support advanced analytical and predictive reasoning systems.
Figure 5.20: Artificial Intelligence in Morningstar Sustainalytics’ information value chain

Source: Morningstar Sustainalytics, https://connect.sustainalytics.com/hubfs/INV%20-%20AI%20&%20ESG%20White%20Paper.pdf?utm_id=n&utm_source=linkedin&utm_medium=cpc&utm_campaign=sus_gl_n_n_en_2505_tf_n_susbrand_aiforesgassessments&utm_content=image_1200x630
By applying AI, Morningstar Sustainalytics evaluates ESG risks and impacts more efficiently and comprehensively. First, AI integrates vast datasets from multiple sources, providing real-time, holistic ESG insights that help identify potential risks and overlooked issues. Second, AI-driven automation expands coverage and enhances depth—e.g., incorporating geolocation data to assess physical climate risks or shifting from sub-industry risk evaluations to portfolio-level business activity assessments.
