Finally, Sustainalytics explores using LLMs to convert ESG research findings into concise analytical summaries, better supporting investor decision-making.
Figure 5.20: Leveraging AI solutions in ESG methodologies

Source: Morningstar Sustainalytics, https://connect.sustainalytics.com/hubfs/INV%20-%20AI%20&%20ESG%20White%20Paper.pdf?utm_id=n&utm_source=linkedin&utm_medium=cpc&utm_campaign=sus_gl_n_n_en_2505_tf_n_susbrand_aiforesgassessments&utm_content=image_1200x630
The Morningstar Sustainalytics ESG Risk Smart Score combines machine learning models with ESG and financial variables to deliver broad ESG risk coverage. This approach expands ESG insights to a diverse range of companies across various market capitalizations and geographies, with a focus on smaller firms and those in emerging markets that often have limited or no ESG disclosures. By identifying comparable peer groups and calculating prediction intervals, machine learning further refines risk estimates and effectively addresses data gaps through the detection of ESG risk outliers. Covering over 34,000 companies—primarily small, micro-, and nano-cap firms—with more than 70% located in the Asia Pacific, Africa, and Middle East regions, the score facilitates ESG integration for asset managers and banks.
Figure 5.21: Machine learning in ESG risk smart score

Source: Morningstar Sustainalytics, https://connect.sustainalytics.com/hubfs/INV%20-%20AI%20&%20ESG%20White%20Paper.pdf?utm_id=n&utm_source=linkedin&utm_medium=cpc&utm_campaign=sus_gl_n_n_en_2505_tf_n_susbrand_aiforesgassessments&utm_content=image_1200x630
AI in sustainable investing
Since the 2010s, the integration of AI technologies with sustainable investing has attracted increasing attention, as investors seek innovative methods to incorporate ESG factors into their investment strategies. Both fintech startups and traditional financial institutions are increasingly leveraging AI to analyze ESG datasets, identify investment opportunities, and assess sustainability risks. A growing number of institutional investors—including pension funds, asset managers, and sovereign wealth funds—have adopted AI-driven ESG analytics to enhance their investment decision-making processes. AI fundamentally transforms ESG portfolio management by enabling data-driven analysis, integrating complex ESG frameworks, and employing dynamic optimization algorithms. This empowers investors to construct balanced portfolios that align with sustainable development goals while maximizing financial returns.
ESG score prediction
Traditional sentiment analysis methods typically classify sentiments as simply positive or negative and have been widely used to predict market performance. However, these approaches often fail to capture nuanced sentiment on ESG metrics, especially at the product-specific level. Saxena et al.(2024) addressed this limitation by creating a dataset with products manually labeled as eco-friendly or non-eco-friendly based on their environmental impact. They proposed a nested sentiment analysis framework that integrates the FinBERT model with explainable AI (XAI) techniques to classify consumer sentiments and predict ESG scores. Sentiments were assigned at the product level using FinBERT’s keyword-based approach, where positive or negative keywords indicated sentiment polarity. This finer granularity enhances ESG scoring models by more accurately reflecting consumer perceptions related to sustainability.
Figure 5.22: Flow of sentiment classification and ESG impact for eco-friendly and non-eco-friendly products

Source: Saxena et al.(2024), https://www.mdpi.com/2227-7390/12/21/3332
The results indicate that the model achieved an accuracy of 91.76% in predicting ESG scores and 99% accuracy in sentiment classification. The study further revealed that negative sentiment positively influences ESG scores for both eco-friendly and non-eco-friendly products, whereas positive sentiment is associated with lower average ESG and environmental scores. These findings underscore the link between product sentiment, eco-friendliness, and ESG performance, offering valuable guidance for ESG investment strategies and helping investors more effectively assess corporate sustainability.
Figure 5.23: Pipeline for sentiment analysis and ESG impact assessment using FinBERT and explainable AI

Source: Saxena et al.(2024)
Figure 5.24: Comparison of ESG and environmental scores based on sentiment and eco-friendliness

Source: Saxena et al.(2024)
ESG screening
There is a strong linkage between ESG performance and the sustainability of corporate business models, prompting investors to increasingly integrate ESG criteria into portfolio selection. Franco et al. (2020) developed a machine learning (ML) algorithm designed to identify patterns between companies’ ESG profiles and their financial performance. The algorithm uses a dynamically updated rule set to map regions in a high-dimensional space of 469 ESG features—including raw and derived indicators, sector and country classifications, company size, and controversy metrics—into scores of {−1, 0, +1}, which serve as a screening tool to select stocks and predict excess returns.
Figure 5.25: ML screenings and simulated performance

Source: Franco et al. (2020), https://arxiv.org/pdf/2002.07477
Their findings demonstrate that the Positive ML Screening strategy consistently outperforms the Negative ML Screening, with the MSCI World Index benchmark positioned between the two. Notably, in strong bull markets characterized by high returns and low volatility, the performance gap narrows; however, during bear markets or periods of market uncertainty, the Positive ML Screening strategy delivers significantly superior returns compared to its negative counterpart.
This ML algorithm captures nonlinear relationships between ESG features and financial performance, offering an effective stock screening tool that surpasses traditional strategies relying solely on ESG ratings. The study demonstrates that measurable alpha is embedded within a company’s ESG profile; however, extracting this alpha requires advanced, nonlinear methods like machine learning to fully realize its potential.
ESG integration
AI technologies significantly enhance investment decision-making by analyzing vast textual data sources—such as company reports, news articles, and social media—to identify ESG performance, governance issues, and reputational risks. These insights are then integrated with traditional financial metrics to provide a comprehensive evaluation framework.
Jain et al. (2023) developed a GPT-3.5-based ESG analyzer that detects historical ESG-related events and aligns them with corporate ESG profiles. The analyzer processes unstructured data through an advanced classification module, incorporating diverse streams including news, social media posts, and corporate disclosures to generate nuanced insights into firm-level ESG performance. The model was trained on a large-scale dataset of ESG-related headlines classified by sentiment, enabling it to assign ESG scores based on the sentiment of relevant news and to regularly update company evaluations.
Figure 5.26: Logic flow of OpenAI prompts to get an ESG analyzer

Source: Jain et al. (2023), https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=4495647
The system integrates multiple APIs—such as NewsAPI for news aggregation, OpenAI API for natural language understanding, and Alpaca API for financial data access—combining ESG indicators (e.g., greenhouse gas emissions, labor practices, executive compensation) with market information (e.g., stock prices, trends). Further regression analysis validates the model, revealing a notable 20% correlation between stock returns and ESG-related news coverage. This demonstrates that AI-powered ESG tools effectively complement traditional quantitative investment strategies, such as return forecasting, thereby enhancing the precision and responsiveness of ESG-driven portfolio management.
Figure 5.27: Stage-wise deployment of components used for the model

Source: Jain et al. (2023), https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=4495647
In 2023, HSBC partnered with Arabesque AI, a fintech company specializing in ESG, to launch the HSBC ESG Risk Improvers Index, powered by ESG Book—Arabesque AI’s ESG data and analytics platform. This initiative marked HSBC’s first global index leveraging AI to measure improvements in corporate ESG performance. The index tracks over 1,000 liquid global stocks, focusing on companies anticipated to achieve financial gains from enhanced ESG risk profiles, as quantified by ESG Book’s AI-driven ESG scores.
ESG Book calculates ESG scores for each index constituent using AI techniques—primarily natural language processing—to continuously extract and analyze relevant public information, including ESG-related news and NGO reports, on a daily basis. Arabesque AI then generates a six-month ESG momentum score to evaluate whether each company has improved its ESG credentials, dynamically adjusting the index weights in response. Unlike traditional ESG investment strategies—such as best-in-class or ESG integration approaches, which focus solely on high ESG ratings—this index emphasizes recent improvements or deteriorations in ESG performance. By doing so, it provides investors with an innovative ESG investment tool that channels capital toward companies demonstrating sustainable progress, fostering greater market attention on long-term value creation.
Figure 5.28: The ESG Performance Score offers an ISSB-aligned, rules-based evaluation of a company's sustainability progress

Source: ESG Book, https://www.esgbook.com/solutions/data-analytics
Dynamic portfolio management
In dynamic portfolio management, AI algorithms facilitate multi-objective portfolio optimization by balancing financial returns, risk mitigation, and ESG considerations to achieve investment goals. By analyzing publicly available policy documents, news reports, and social media discussions, AI can identify emerging sustainability trends and market risks. Integrating these insights with portfolio performance metrics and investor risk preferences, AI-driven systems dynamically adjust allocations to ensure alignment with sustainable development objectives. Machine learning models further enable scenario analysis—such as the impacts of climate change or regulatory shifts—allowing investors to evaluate potential effects on portfolio returns and risk profiles, and to make proactive adjustments that mitigate sustainability-related investment risks.
Robo-advisors 
Robo-advisors leverage big data and algorithms to reduce monetary, cognitive, behavioral biases, and psychological transaction costs associated with human decision-making. This democratizes access to high-quality financial management for less-wealthy investors. Moreover, robo-advice can finely profile users’ preferences and financial situations, providing personalized investment recommendations and asset allocation plans, with the potential to improve financial literacy and narrow wealth gaps.
D’Hondt et al. (2019) introduce the concept of AI Alter Egos—shadow robo-investors designed to simulate actual investor behavior. Leveraging a comprehensive dataset of 22,972 individual investors, which includes detailed attributes such as income, education, gender, risk aversion, and trading habits, the authors compare the performance of human investors with that of robo-investors focused on stocks and ETFs. Their results show that machine learning–based AI Alter Egos can markedly enhance portfolio performance for certain investor segments, particularly those with high risk aversion and lower income, who are prone to under-diversification and poor risk management. 
Figure 5.29: Summary statistics are computed for separate samples with low/high education, low/high risk aversion and low/high income classification for investors 

Source: D’Hondt et al. (2019), https://arxiv.org/pdf/1907.03370
During financial crises, AI-driven strategies notably outperform human investors. Specifically, the median AI Alter Ego using machine learning with rolling variance achieves an overall return of 60%. Robo-investors tend to withdraw cash from the market more readily during downturns, avoiding behavioral biases like the disposition effect that harm human investors.
Stash is a digital investment and banking platform that aims to make investment management accessible and convenient for inexperienced users. About 86% of Stash’s customers are first-time or beginner investors, many of whom have never had personalized financial advice before. Based on understanding users’ financial goals and risk preferences, Stash offers three subscription plans and comprehensive advice, covering banking, investments, retirement plans, trusts, and financial education. With 1.3 million paying users and $4.3 billion in assets under management, Stash is redefining inclusive finance by providing personalized, AI-driven financial advice at scale to everyday Americans.
In 2024, Stash introduced Money Coach AI, a tool designed to help customers make efficient and smart financial decisions within its ecosystem. Money Coach’s chat interface ranges from open-ended questions like “What is my next best action?” and “How am I doing?” to specific inquiries. Nearly 25% of users take an actionable step—such as diversifying their portfolio or making deposits—within 15 minutes after interacting with Money Coach, with an average weekly deposit of $45 per user.
Figure 5.30: Cumulative return performance of AI Alter Ego and human investors 

Source: D’Hondt et al. (2019)
Figure 5.31: Recognized as the “Best No B.S. Investing App” by Popsugar in 2024, Stash highlights that diversified portfolios historically deliver annual returns between 5-10%

Source: Stash, https://www.stash.com/

Ai in risk pricing
Climate risk analysis
Artificial intelligence also strengthens financial systems’ capacity to assess climate risks. Investors, banks, and regulators leverage AI to simulate climate scenarios by integrating meteorological, geographic, and financial data, forecasting the potential impacts of extreme weather events on asset prices and broader macro-financial stability. These predictive insights allow regulators and market participants to intervene proactively, reducing the likelihood of financial crises and minimizing associated social and environmental consequences. Regarding how climate change translates into significant economic risks, Klusak et al.(2023) employed machine learning models to simulate climate change impacts on sovereign credit ratings for 109 countries. 
Figure 5.32: Four-step process integrating climate economics into sovereign credit ratings and cost of debt calculations
 
Source: Klusak et al.(2023), https://ueaeprints.uea.ac.uk/id/eprint/79581/1/Klusak_et_al_AAM_230120.pdf
Their findings suggest that significant impacts on sovereign credit ratings could materialize as early as 2030, with downgrades becoming more pronounced as global temperatures rise and climate variability intensifies. Under stringent climate policies consistent with the Paris Agreement—limiting warming to below 2°C, in line with Representative Concentration Pathway (RCP) 2.6—the effects of climate change on sovereign credit ratings remain minimal. In contrast, under a high-emissions scenario (RCP 8.5), by 2030, 59 sovereign nations are projected to experience average rating downgrades of 0.68 notches, increasing to 81 countries with average downgrades of 2.18 notches by 2100.
The study further quantifies the impact of climate-induced sovereign downgrades on borrowing costs for both governments and corporations. Under RCP 2.6, sovereign debt interest expenses could rise by $45–67 billion annually, increasing to $133–203 billion under RCP 8.5. For corporate debt, additional costs are estimated at $10–17 billion under RCP 2.6 and $35–61 billion under RCP 8.5, highlighting the broader financial implications of climate change on credit markets.
Figure 5.33: Climate-adjusted predicted sovereign ratings under RCP 8.5 (Panel A) and RCP 2.6 (Panel B) for 2030, 2050, 2070, and 2100
 
Source: Klusak et al.(2023), https://ueaeprints.uea.ac.uk/id/eprint/79581/1/Klusak_et_al_AAM_230120.pdf
In practice, Jupiter Intelligence offers comprehensive solutions for climate risk-based investment planning, risk identification and mitigation, pricing, resilience planning, and regulatory compliance. Its services span dozens of industries—including insurance, banking, asset management, real estate, energy, manufacturing, and agriculture—as well as public-sector organizations.
Jupiter’s ClimateScore Global leverages AI and machine learning algorithms to quantify physical climate risks worldwide using high-dimensional data. By forecasting changes in sea levels, wave heights, storm intensities, land and sea temperatures, and precipitation patterns—and accounting for their complex interactions—the platform constructs climate risk loss models and prices climate change impacts. ClimateScore Global enables financial institutions to assess and manage physical climate risks across global portfolios by identifying exposures, vulnerable assets and regions, evaluating risk evolution, and uncovering opportunities to enhance resilience. This supports compliance with disclosure requirements and informs strategic decision-making.
Currently, Jupiter Intelligence collaborates with three of the five largest U.S. banks, 25% of the world’s largest financial institutions, four leading global asset managers, and 20% of the Global 100, demonstrating its broad adoption among major financial players.
Figure 5.34: ClimateScore Global delivers decision-grade risk and adaptation analysis quickly, flexibly, intuitively

Source: Jupiter Intelligence, https://www.jupiterintel.com/
Credit scoring model optimization
AI is revolutionizing traditional credit scoring systems by overcoming their reliance on conventional credit histories. It enables the incorporation of unstructured and multi-source heterogeneous data—such as social media activity, online payment behaviors, e-commerce sales, and inventory turnover—allowing the construction of credit scoring models for the “credit invisible” population. This expands financing opportunities to individuals, microenterprises, and startups lacking traditional credit records.
Traditional credit scoring often relies on logistic regression, but AI leverages advanced machine learning (ML) and deep learning techniques on diverse data sources to build more accurate and optimized credit rating models. AI methods excel at capturing complex, nonlinear relationships between macroeconomic variables and borrower attributes, resulting in enhanced risk assessment and more precise loan pricing.
For example, Sadhwani et al. (2021) developed a deep learning model analyzing over 120 million U.S. mortgages from 1995 to 2014 to examine borrower behavior across economic cycles. Unlike traditional linear models, their approach captures highly nonlinear effects of loan-specific and macroeconomic variables, which can be refined down to the ZIP-code level. Notably, strong interactions exist among variables, such as the joint impact of unemployment rates and credit scores on prepayment probabilities. Among all outcomes, prepayment exhibited the most pronounced nonlinear effects. Their results demonstrate superior predictive accuracy of deep learning models over linear counterparts for both loan-level and pool-level risk forecasts. 
Credit inclusion improvements derive both from the use of alternative data (e.g., education, employment, digital footprint) and from the adoption of sophisticated ML techniques. In the U.S. context, Di Maggio & Yao (2020) analyze anonymized administrative data from fintech firm Upstart to assess whether alternative data usage expands credit access. Comparing actual outcomes from Upstart’s AI-driven credit model to counterfactuals based on traditional models used for regulatory reporting, they find that Upstart approves a significantly higher proportion of applicants with low credit scores—over 20% of such applicants approved by Upstart would have been rejected under traditional models. 
Figure 5.35: Upstart provides personal loans to borrowers across the United States, with over 90% of loans being fully automated

Source: Upstart, https://ir.upstart.com/static-files/5eee2acb-30ff-4e2e-afa8-0f954db98bd9
Figure 5.36: The outcome distributions under Upstart’s model

Source: Di Maggio & Yao (2020), https://www.nber.org/system/files/working_papers/w29840/w29840.pdf
Their analysis further shows that borrowers approved by Upstart’s model tend to have higher education levels, are more likely consolidating debt, and are less frequently hourly workers. Key drivers of the approved outcomes include nontraditional factors such as education, employment type, loan purpose, and device type used during application. Their findings reveal that alternative data not only broadens credit availability by funding loans otherwise rejected, but also lowers borrowing costs for individuals previously limited to expensive credit options.
Figure 5.37: Differences in alternative borrower characteristics by model approval outcomes

Source: Di Maggio & Yao (2020)
Worth AI provides an AI-driven platform for comprehensive credit scoring of businesses. By analyzing over 1,100 data points from diverse unstructured sources, the platform generates the standardized Worth Score™, offering lenders deeper insights into the financial health and risk profile of small and medium enterprises (SMEs). This facilitates opportunity identification, risk mitigation, and fraud prevention, ultimately reducing financing frictions and enhancing access to capital for SMEs.
Figure 5.38: Worth Score™ is the first business credit score based on more than 1,100 data points aggregated from various unstructured sources

Source: Worth AI, https://worthai.com/worth-score/
AI in regulatory governance
Regulators, central banks, and financial institutions benefit significantly from advancements in AI tools that enable rapid identification and extraction of ESG- and sustainability-related information from diverse corporate contracts, disclosures, and ESG reports. These tools help navigate differing reporting standards, definitions, and disclosure frameworks across jurisdictions, detect non-compliant clauses and greenwashing, reduce compliance monitoring costs, and ensure adherence to policy standards in green investments and inclusive lending, thereby mitigating social and environmental risks.
Traditionally, researchers have relied on bag-of-words or similar approaches to analyze corporate disclosures, which are error-prone and insufficient for complex topics like climate change.  And the development of large pretrained language models (LM) brought new opportunities to language processing and understand context. Webersinke et al.(2021) proposed CLIMATEBERT, a transformer-based language model that is pretrained on over 2 million paragraphs of climate-related texts, crawled from various sources such as common news, research articles, and climate reporting of companies. They find that CLIMATEBERT leads to a 48% improvement on a masked language model objective which, in turn, leads to lowering error rates by 3.57% to 35.71% for various climate-related downstream tasks like text classification, sentiment analysis, and fact-checking.
Figure. Sequence of training phases. 

Source: Webersinke et al.(2021), https://arxiv.org/pdf/2110.12010
Bingler et al. (2022) furtehr developed ClimateBert, the first context-aware algorithm to evaluate improvements in climate-related disclosures following the introduction of the Task Force on Climate-related Financial Disclosures (TCFD) recommendations.
Their findings show only a modest (nearly 2.2 percentage points) but statistically significant increase in TCFD-aligned disclosure from 2017 to 2020, mainly in governance and risk management, while strategy and metrics disclosures remain limited. Notably, France—with mandatory climate-risk reporting under Article 173 of the French Energy Transition Law—exhibits much higher disclosure levels.
Figure 5.39: Corporate climate risk disclosure by TCFD categories

Source: Bingler et al. (2022), https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1544612322000897
The study highlights “cherry-picking” behaviors, where firms selectively disclose less materially relevant TCFD categories, and material information, i.e., information related to strategy and metrics and targets, might not be disclosed to the extent, potentially undermining climate risk management by external stakeholders. This underscores the utility of AI to audit the quality and completeness of corporate environmental disclosures in practice.
Figure 5.40: Human F1 scores serve as reference points to the corresponding model F1 scores and represent the performance of annotators’ assessments in comparison to subject matter experts’ assessments

Source: TCFD, https://assets.bbhub.io/company/sites/60/2023/09/2023-Status-Report.pdf#page=133.01
Actually, the Task Force on Climate-related Financial Disclosures (TCFD) has employed artificial intelligence to review the alignment of information in publicly available financial filings, annual reports, integrated reports, sustainability reports, and other relevant documents. The AI model was based on a deep learning natural language processing approach, specifically leveraging RoBERTa. This language model was trained using a proprietary dataset containing climate-related texts and passages that correspond to the Task Force’s 11 recommended disclosures—referred to as labeled data. The AI’s performance was evaluated by comparing model predictions with human labeled data. A passage was marked as correctly classified if the model’s assessment of whether it sufficiently captured a specific recommended disclosure matched the label. The F1 score, which is the harmonic mean of precision and recall, was used as the key performance metric. The model’s F1 scores were close to those of human reviewers across all 11 recommended disclosures, demonstrating the reliability of AI-assisted ESG disclosure evaluation.
Companies may misrepresent their ESG efforts via “greenwashing” or “social washing”. Baker et al. (2024) studied US-listed firms’ public commitments to diversity, equity, and inclusion (DEI) versus actual workforce diversity using novel data from Revelio Labs. They created a 989-term DEI dictionary to quantify DEI discourse frequency in financial filings. Their analysis reveals only a weak correlation between DEI discussion frequency and actual diversity levels (measured by female and non-white employee proportions), with diversity explaining merely 0.1% to 0.7% of variance in DEI word frequency. Although the median number of DEI-related words slightly increases with higher diversity ratings, the distribution of DEI discussions within each diversity rating group remains similar across the diversity score range. The substantial unexplained variation suggests that many companies may be opportunistically engaging in selective voluntary DEI disclosures to perform “diversity washing” meaning they distort or exaggerate their true commitment to diversity.
Figure 5.41: The distribution of DEI-related words in corpus of SEC documents, broken down by the decile of underlying diversity

Source: Baker et al. (2024), https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/1475-679X.12542
A quintile-based matrix combining DEI discussion frequency and diversity measures further shows that about 40% of firms discuss DEI more than warranted by their actual diversity. Larger, more profitable, and less volatile companies are more prone to diversity washing but paradoxically receive higher ESG ratings and attract more ESG fund investments despite poor DEI performance. This exposes risks of false ESG claims and calls for regulators to deploy large language models (LLMs) to enhance oversight of corporate ESG disclosures.
Figure 5.42: The number of firm-year observations in each quintile

Source: Baker et al. (2024)
Regulators can leverage NLP and AI techniques to parse corporate ESG reports, extract ESG data, and cross-validate with regulatory databases to flag inconsistencies and green washing and social washing risks, providing reliable information to stakeholders. For example, under the EU Carbon Border Adjustment Mechanism (CBAM), importers submit product carbon footprint data via an electronic registry system. Automated AI-driven checks validate data completeness and logical consistency to ensure conformity with EU technical standards, significantly improving verification accuracy and efficiency.
Going forward, AI-based solutions can be more effectively deployed to detect green washing and social washing practices across a wide range of entities, including corporations, investment funds, and banks. While these tools can significantly enhance the efficiency of screening ESG disclosures, ensuring accuracy remains critical. Human oversight should play a central role in validating AI-generated findings, refining detection criteria, and providing contextual judgment that algorithms may overlook. Ultimately, enhanced AI capabilities, combined with expert supervision, can improve the quality and transparency of ESG reporting, uphold the integrity of sustainable finance, and safeguard both investors and society from misleading claims.
Risks and governance 
Challenges to responsible AI
Global ESG regulation is undergoing a notable shift—from voluntary ESG reporting toward legally binding requirements—while ESG considerations are moving from a peripheral reference to a central parameter in investment decision-making. Artificial intelligence (AI), with its powerful and versatile data-processing capabilities, is increasingly pivotal in reducing ESG management costs, enhancing value creation, and supporting corporate and financial institutions’ strategic decision-making. Its application also brings new challenges. These include concerns over data security, technological transparency, green AI development, and the strengthening of regulatory and ethical frameworks. Only when AI operates as a responsible tool can it truly advance sustainable development.
Data privacy and consent
AI’s data-driven nature places a premium on both the quantity and quality of information. In ESG practice, relevant datasets often involve personally identifiable information and sensitive corporate data—such as individual trading records, employee welfare information, and supply chain compliance data. Collecting and using such data can raise significant privacy concerns and information security risks, including unauthorized data usage, privacy violations, and regulatory breaches.
For example, in 2024, the Dutch Data Protection Authority (DPA) imposed a €30.5 million fine on Clearview AI, a company providing facial recognition services to intelligence and law enforcement agencies. The penalty was issued for building an “illegal” database containing billions of facial images without the knowledge or consent of Dutch citizens, in clear violation of the General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR).
In response to stricter data protection requirements under regulations such as the GDPR, China’s Personal Information Protection Law (PIPL), and the California Consumer Privacy Act (CCPA), organizations are increasingly implementing measures such as data anonymization, pseudonymization, secure data processing protocols, and explicit consent agreements for AI training on customer data. Distributed modeling frameworks and federated learning algorithms enable data integration while safeguarding privacy. Regular privacy audits have also become standard practice, ensuring compliance with legal requirements and preventing unauthorized data leaks while still meeting the needs of AI model training.
Bias and fairness
AI models often inherit societal biases embedded in their training data. If the underlying datasets contain bias, AI systems may produce discriminatory outcomes in domains such as lending and credit scoring. For example, Bartlett et al. (2022) used mortgage data from government-sponsored enterprises (GSEs) and the Federal Housing Administration (FHA) to examine discrimination in loan pricing. Their findings suggest that while FinTech algorithms can reduce some forms of face-to-face lending bias, algorithmic pricing strategies—designed to maximize profits based on large datasets—can inadvertently perpetuate discrimination. As a result, minority borrowers, particularly Latino and African American applicants, continued to face higher rejection rates and interest rates when purchasing or refinancing mortgages. This pattern may reflect the historical legacy of higher pricing and discrimination in transactions involving minority borrowers. 
In response, financial institutions are adopting fairness-enhancement techniques to reduce discriminatory outcomes at the source. Key approaches include developing more diverse and representative datasets, implementing bias-detection algorithms, and applying counterfactual testing to evaluate decision robustness. These measures aim to strike a balance between efficiency and fairness, ultimately enhancing inclusion.
Transparency and explainability
A major limitation of AI—particularly in machine learning—is its black-box nature. Combining or stacking multiple models can obscure the influence of individual input variables on final predictions, resulting in poor interpretability. This stands in direct conflict with regulatory and client demands for transparency. For instance, robo-advisors can provide automated and personalized investment advice, lowering the barrier to professional wealth management. However, their opaque decision logic can leave clients in the dark about why certain recommendations are made. More critically, when managers and clients rely on such non-transparent models, accountability becomes challenging—especially if these decisions lead to losses or ethical breaches.
Financial institutions are increasingly integrating explainability tools such as feature permutation importance, partial dependence plots, SHAP value analysis, and LIME algorithms to interpret AI-driven decisions. In sensitive contexts like loan approvals or investment advice, these methods enable both clients and analysts to understand the logic behind outputs, thereby supporting accountability and building trust.
Misinformation and Hallucination 
AI’s powerful generative capabilities and low barriers to use also make it a potent tool for creating and amplifying false or misleading information. AI hallucinations—fabricated but plausible-sounding outputs—can mislead consumers and investors, increase market friction, and undermine the integrity of the information environment.
For example, the U.S. Food and Drug Administration reportedly faced internal controversy over an AI tool, “Elsa,” used to assist in new drug approvals. The system allegedly generated false research reports and distorted scientific data, raising widespread concerns about government technology governance and public health safety. The root technical issue lies in the nature of large language models: they are probability-based text generators, not fact-checkers. Without rigorous constraints, validation, and domain-specific adaptation, such systems can produce highly convincing but factually incorrect outputs. Deploying immature, general-purpose AI directly in high-risk decision-making—such as drug evaluation—can lead to catastrophic outcomes and severely erode public trust.
Addressing these risks requires improving data quality, incorporating knowledge provenance mechanisms into AI models, and ensuring robust human oversight in high-stakes contexts. Clear application boundaries and accountability frameworks must be established to define responsibilities in the event of AI-generated misinformation. This includes regulatory guidance on acceptable use cases, mandatory verification protocols for critical outputs, and sanctions for negligent deployment of generative AI in sensitive domains.
Accountability and Human-in-the-Loop (HITL)
As AI systems become more capable in supporting predictions and decision-making, critical questions arise: Who is responsible when AI outputs lead to errors or ethical violations? The OECD Principles on Artificial Intelligence state that AI actors should be accountable for the proper functioning of AI systems and for ensuring compliance with relevant principles, based on their role, the context, and the state of the art in technology (OECD AI Principles).
A growing consensus among scholars and practitioners favors hybrid human–AI systems rather than fully automated decision-making. The Human-in-the-Loop (HITL) framework incorporates human expertise at critical decision points to enhance efficiency, accuracy, and accountability, while mitigating automation bias. Human review is particularly vital in high-stakes domains such as loan approvals, investment strategy, and corporate decision-making. Increasingly, researchers are developing semi-automated or fully automated deep learning training tools that integrate human knowledge at various stages of the model development cycle to optimize performance and maintain ethical safeguards (Wu et al., 2022). (Eduardo et al.,2022)
Figure 5.43: Human-in-the-loop system in machine learning algorithm optimizing
Human-in-the-loop machine learning (HITL-ML-relations) mind map


Source: https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10462-022-10246-w#Fig10
Employment disruption
AI-driven automation is reshaping labor markets, displacing certain traditional roles and accelerating structural changes in employment. A McKinsey report analyzing labor markets in nine major EU economies, the United Kingdom, and the United States estimates that up to 30% of hours worked could be automated by 2030—an effect amplified by generative AI. This transformation could require millions of occupational transitions. Labor demand is projected to grow in STEM, healthcare, and other high-skill sectors, while continuing to decline in roles such as food service, production, customer service, sales, and office support. These latter roles often involve repetitive tasks, data collection, and basic data processing, all of which can be efficiently executed by automated systems. Future demand will increasingly favor not only technical expertise but also social and emotional skills, requiring adaptive education and training systems to prepare workers for the evolving demands of the AI-enabled economy.
Figure 5.44: Demand for technological and social and emotional skills increase in Europe

Source: McKinsey, https://www.mckinsey.com/mgi/our-research/a-new-future-of-work-the-race-to-deploy-ai-and-raise-skills-in-europe-and-beyond?utm_content=blog
While public discourse often highlights the risk of “AI-induced mass unemployment,” it is important to recognize that, thus far, there has been no sharp increase in unemployment directly attributable to AI. Rather, the displacement effects appear to be gradual—except in hypothetical scenarios involving extremely advanced AI capabilities in the future. Hampole et al. (2025) offer a nuanced explanation for why the observed impact of AI on employment has been limited. They develop a task-based model and empirical framework to measure how AI adoption influences labor demand across occupations, emphasizing the distinction between mean task exposure (the average degree to which tasks can be substituted by AI) and dispersion in task exposure (the variation in which specific tasks are affected). Their findings show that, on average, AI adoption reduces labor demand due to task substitution. However, holding the average effect constant, greater dispersion in exposure can actually raise labor demand. This occurs because workers shift their efforts toward tasks that AI cannot easily replace. Consequently, the overall impact of AI on employment is weakened by this offsetting mechanism. Moreover, the productivity gains from AI adoption tend to expand overall employment opportunities across occupations.
Historically, technological change has displaced some types of work while simultaneously creating new ones, with overall employment generally keeping pace with population growth. For instance, the spread of earlier generations of automation and robotics did not lead to mass unemployment; instead, the significant gains in productivity generated new demand and additional jobs. A key question today is whether artificial intelligence will follow a similar path—ultimately creating more jobs than it replaces. The New York Times highlights that in the AI era, human capabilities in trust, integration, and taste will be more valuable than ever. These areas are expected to give rise to new professions such as AI auditors, fact-checkers and compliance officers, AI integrators, AI trainers, and curators of taste. The World Economic Forum projects that between 2025 and 2030, emerging trends in AI and information processing technologies will displace 9 million jobs while creating 11 million new ones, resulting in a net increase of 2 million positions.
Rather than focusing solely on whether our current jobs might be replaced, it may be more productive to consider how we can cultivate distinctive insights and skills in domains where machines cannot easily substitute for human judgment. The more pressing challenge is to recognize and manage the transition, as certain sectors, regions, or demographic groups may encounter concentrated job losses, temporary unemployment, or skill mismatches. To mitigate these risks, governments, enterprises, and individuals alike must make substantial investments in workforce reskilling, vocational training, and lifelong learning programs.
Carbon emissions
As artificial intelligence models become increasingly prevalent, their training and operation demand substantial computational resources, resulting in significant energy consumption and associated carbon emissions—posing potential conflicts with sustainability objectives. For instance, answering a single abstract algebra question using DeepSeek-R1 70B produces approximately 4.8 g of CO₂—equivalent to consuming 0.01 kWh of electricity, enough to power a 5 W light bulb for two hours (Dauner & Socher, 2025).
Microsoft’s sustainability reports illustrate the scale of this challenge. Microsoft’s 2025 environmental data fact sheet shows that despite its public commitments to carbon neutrality, total emissions rose by 23.4% between fiscal years 2020 and 2024, driven largely by exponential increases in data center electricity demand from cloud and AI services. This surge has also compelled the company to engage in large-scale carbon credit purchases to offset its footprint.
Figure 5.45: Tracking progress toward carbon negative by 2030

Source: Microsoft, https://cdn-dynmedia-1.microsoft.com/is/content/microsoftcorp/microsoft/msc/documents/presentations/CSR/2025-Microsoft-Environmental-Sustainability-Report.pdf#page=11.27
AI researchers should prioritize the development of computationally efficient models, including energy-saving AI chips, sparse neural network architectures, and algorithms that reduce the training burden of deep learning models. These improvements may involve more efficient parallelization, optimization methods that reduce the number of required iterations, and model compression techniques. At the infrastructure, integrating advanced building materials, renewable energy solutions, and innovative energy management technologies—such as dynamic power optimization and waste heat recovery—can lower the operational footprint of large-scale computing facilities. Policymakers can play a pivotal role by enacting targeted regulations and fostering collaboration between the energy sector and AI industry to align technological innovation with climate goals.
Emerging governance frameworks 
AI’s dual role—driving progress toward global sustainability goals while introducing new governance challenges—has prompted regulatory responses worldwide. This section examines key developments in the European Union, United States, China, Singapore, and South Korea, focusing on regulatory efforts to guide AI’s sustainable development.
European Union: comprehensive, risk-based regulation
The EU has adopted a risk-oriented legislative approach to AI, aiming to strike a balance between fostering innovation and safeguarding safety, ethics, and social responsibility. In 2024, the European Commission introduced the Artificial Intelligence Act (AI Act)—the world’s first comprehensive legal framework for AI. The Act classifies AI systems into four categories: unacceptable risk, high risk, limited risk, and minimal risk, with regulatory obligations increasing with the level of risk.
Applications in credit scoring and insurance pricing are designated as “high risk” and must meet requirements for transparency, explainability, human oversight, and robust data governance. For example, credit scoring models must explain their decision logic and avoid discrimination based on sensitive attributes such as race or gender. However, AI systems designed for detecting fraud in financial services or for prudential purposes—such as calculating capital requirements for credit institutions and insurance companies—are not classified as high-risk. With regard to generative AI, the Act requires providers to disclose whether copyrighted material was used for training and to offer users clear explanations of the generated outputs.
In addition, the AI Act has extraterritorial effect. It applies not only to companies operating AI systems within the EU, but also to entities outside the EU whose AI outputs are used within the Union. For example, providers of AI systems or general-purpose AI models located outside the EU must ensure compliance if their models are deployed by downstream users in the EU. This includes observing their own compliance obligations, as well as aligning with the contractual terms and regulatory requirements governing the deployers.
Figure 5.46: A summary of the most important actors in the creation and enforcement of the AI Act, and their relationships

Source: EU, https://artificialintelligenceact.eu/context/
Complementing the AI Act, the EU’s Digital Markets Act (DMA) and Digital Services Act (DSA) ensure fair competition, data use transparency, and platform accountability, while the General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR)—in effect since 2018—provides stringent protections for data privacy and security. Together, these laws form an integrated governance framework that addresses AI’s technical risks, market impacts, and rights implications, offering a model of comprehensive regulation for global AI governance.
United States: multi-Level, function-oriented oversight
The United States has not enacted a federal-level, comprehensive privacy law but has established a multi-tiered, flexible regulatory environment. At the federal level, agencies such as the National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) have issued non-binding guidelines, including the Blueprint for an AI Bill of Rights and the AI Risk Management Framework, which outline principles for safety, fairness, and transparency.
At the state level, more than 30 states have introduced AI-related regulatory initiatives, often focused on consumer protection, privacy, and transparency in AI-generated content. California Consumer Privacy Act (CCPA), enacted in 2018, remains a landmark in state-level data protection. In 2024, Colorado Artificial Intelligence Act (CAIA) established a state AI oversight system with explicit requirements for transparency, accountability, and fairness, obligating developers and deployers of “high-risk AI systems” in sectors such as finance, insurance, and healthcare to mitigate algorithmic discrimination and protect consumer rights.
Unlike the EU’s precautionary and comprehensive approach, the U.S. framework is shaped by its strategic imperative to maintain global AI leadership. Under the Trump administration (2025–), regulatory pressure has been reduced to encourage domestic innovation. On July 23, 2025, the administration released Winning the Race: America’s AI Action Plan, emphasizing the role of AI in economic competitiveness, national security, and human advancement, with explicit focus on strengthening AI infrastructure, accelerating innovation, and leveraging AI as a strategic asset in geopolitical competition, particularly with China.
China: dual focus on technology and ecosystem governance
China regulates artificial intelligence through a multi-tiered legal framework, anchored by the Cybersecurity Law, Data Security Law, and Personal Information Protection Law. These laws provide governance from the perspectives of network security, data security, and personal privacy. Building on this foundation, China has issued AI-specific regulations. In 2023, the Interim Measures for the Management of Generative Artificial Intelligence Services became the country’s first dedicated regulation on generative AI. It stipulates clear requirements for content identifiability, lawful sourcing of training data for foundational models, and safety responsibilities during content generation. This forms a “three-tier regulatory pyramid” targeting foundational algorithms, generation, and dissemination—advancing ecosystem-wide compliance.
Singapore: non-binding governance
Similar to the U.S., Singapore has not enacted mandatory AI-specific legislation. Instead, it leverages a flexible, non-binding governance approach grounded in existing legal frameworks. Through policy documents and open-source testing toolkits, Singapore seeks to balance technological innovation with ethical, privacy, and security concerns.
Since the release of its Model AI Governance Framework and National AI Strategy, the government has promoted responsible AI adoption, with a particular focus on critical sectors such as finance. Soft compliance tools—such as the FEAT principles, the Veritas framework, and supporting software packages—establish governance guidelines on transparency, explainability, safety, robustness, fairness, inclusive growth, and societal and environmental well-being. The overarching goal is to maximize AI’s value while safeguarding citizens’ rights and privacy.
South Korea: balancing technological innovation and legal norms
South Korea combines flexible experimentation with a formal legal framework. Its “regulatory sandbox” offers a controlled environment for testing emerging technologies, accelerating innovation while ensuring social safeguards. In December 2024, South Korea passed the Basic AI Act, becoming the second country after the EU to adopt a comprehensive AI legal framework.
The Act enshrines three core AI ethics principles—safety and reliability, accessibility, and human-centricity—aligned with South Korea’s 2020 AI development guidelines. It defines the obligations of AI practitioners, establishes mechanisms to ensure reliability and safety, and aims to balance industrial growth with robust safeguards.




Figure 5.47: AI governance frameworks

Regulatory challenges and international collaboration
AI innovation and deployment are evolving far faster than regulatory responses, leading to inevitable lag. Regulatory fragmentation remains a challenge due to divergent legal frameworks, cultural contexts, and interpretations of AI ethics. This poses potential conflicts in values, ethics, and compliance for AI vendors, multinational corporations, and financial institutions.
To address these gaps, international collaboration is accelerating:
In 2023, the G7 launched the Hiroshima AI Process, calling for cooperation with the Global Partnership on AI (GPAI), the OECD, and developing economies to advance inclusive, human-centered AI governance.
In March 2024, the UN adopted its first-ever AI resolution, highlighting AI’s potential to advance all 17 Sustainable Development Goals while urging action on its societal and ethical risks.
In May 2024, the OECD updated its 2019 AI Principles to address generative AI’s challenges, emphasizing misinformation control and information integrity. Forty-seven countries have adopted these principles.
Figure 5.48: OECD principles for trustworthy AI

Source：OECD, https://oecd.ai/en/ai-principles
As AI technology advances, new business models emerge, and applications expand, global AI competition is intensifying. National policies in support of AI development are likely to strengthen, making AI legislation increasingly necessary and mature. No globally proven “gold standard” for AI regulation yet exists. However, fostering responsible AI and building an inclusive, equitable, and diverse global governance framework remain shared priorities for governments, regulators, and industry leaders.
Artificial intelligence is driving a comprehensive transformation across industries—not merely a technological shift but a co-evolution of technological, business, and ethical logics. At the same time, AI presents new challenges for environmental, social, and governance (ESG) concerns. The true value of AI must be grounded in technological controllability, ethical traceability, and regulatory compliance. The future is no longer about whether to adopt AI, but how to adopt it responsibly. Organizations that successfully integrate disruptive technology with robust governance will secure a strategic advantage in this new era, advancing themselves, their industries, and society toward a more efficient, equitable, and sustainable future.

Chapter 6
Challenges and Opportunities 
In the previous chapters, we explored how corporations and investors approach ESG strategies, how different forms of climate risk influence financial systems, and how emerging technologies—especially artificial intelligence—are transforming the way we implement and assess ESG practices. Together, these discussions reveal that ESG is not a fixed concept, but a dynamic and evolving process shaped by social expectations, market forces, and technological change. What began as a set of voluntary initiatives has gradually evolved into a global movement toward standardized disclosure and accountability. Yet, significant differences remain across regions and industries, making consistency and comparability difficult to achieve.
In recent years, the rapid pace of regulatory reform, the surge of technological innovation, and a series of unexpected global shocks—such as the COVID-19 pandemic—have brought new layers of uncertainty. These shifts urge us to reconsider how economic growth, environmental responsibility, and social equity can be balanced in an era defined by both opportunity and disruption. ESG offers a promising framework for navigating this tension, but the path forward is far from clear. There is no universal best practice; rather, effective ESG strategies depend deeply on local context, institutional capacity, and societal priorities.
The following section turns to several of the most pressing challenges and emerging opportunities in this field. By reflecting on these issues, we hope to provide a clearer understanding of how to address the complex realities of sustainable development in a rapidly changing world.
Key challenges
ESG rating and governance
As discussed in Chapter 2, sustainability standards such as TNFD, TCFD, ISSB, and GRI have greatly improved the transparency of ESG disclosures. Yet, despite these advances, significant challenges persist. Regulatory progress varies substantially across jurisdictions, leading to fragmented disclosure practices that hinder comparability and inflate compliance costs—particularly for multinational firms navigating multiple reporting regimes.
A central issue lies in the inconsistency of ESG ratings. Rating agencies often adopt distinct methodologies, diverging in indicator selection, measurement approaches, and weighting schemes. Empirical work by Berg et al. (2022) reveals that measurement divergence remains the dominant source of inconsistency, weakening cross-market reliability. This methodological fragmentation not only complicates benchmarking but also provides scope for strategic manipulation—as firms selectively emphasize favorable metrics or engage in greenwashing and keyword engineering to enhance ratings.
Data scarcity further exacerbates these problems, especially for SMEs and emerging-market firms with limited disclosure capacity. For these entities, ESG reporting is often viewed as a cost burden rather than a strategic investment, resulting in data gaps that undermine global information integrity.
Empirical evidence also shows that ESG performance interacts with financing conditions in nuanced ways. While high-rated firms typically enjoy lower equity financing costs, the opposite may hold in certain sectors or regions, suggesting that contextual factors—such as industry dynamics, cultural norms, and policy frameworks—shape ESG–financial linkages. At the same time, trends like green lending, sustainable procurement, and eco-conscious consumption create both opportunities for innovation and pressures for organizational transformation.
Finally, aligning governance structures with sustainability objectives remains a persistent challenge. Ownership patterns, leadership commitment, and incentive design all influence ESG outcomes. The key lies in building governance mechanisms that integrate sustainability into decision-making and align managerial incentives with long-term social and environmental goals.
Investment and market frictions
Chapter 3 examined how ESG considerations reshape investment behavior and asset pricing. The evidence underscores that investor motivations are diverse: while individual investors often pursue ethical alignment, institutional investors emphasize risk management, diversification, and long-term value creation. This divergence raises a critical question—whether sustainable finance primarily reflects moral values or a rational response to evolving financial risks.
Empirical studies reveal a dual mechanism through which ESG factors affect stock performance: firm fundamentals drive long-term returns, whereas short-term movements are shaped by market sentiment and investor attention. This tension between value-driven and sentiment-driven pricing highlights the behavioral complexity of sustainability investing.
Recent research also indicates retail investors typically do not alter their portfolios in response to ESG-related press releases during routine trading. This apparent indifference cannot be attributed to a lack of economic significance, poor visibility of the information, or difficulties in incorporating ESG factors into investment decisions. Such findings imply that retail investors may interpret and utilize ESG information in ways that differ from institutional investors. Therefore, further research is warranted to explore how various investor segments respond to ESG disclosures, both in the short term and over longer horizons. Gaining deeper insights into these behavioral differences could inform more effective corporate communication strategies, promote the wider adoption of sustainable investment practices, and guide regulatory policies within capital markets.
Climate financing gaps and policy uncertainty
Chapter 4 explored how accelerating climate change is reshaping financial systems. Despite the proliferation of climate-related frameworks and national policies, progress remains uneven. Most policy attention still concentrates on mitigation, whereas adaptation finance—crucial for resilience against physical risks—remains underfunded and under-researched.
Economic disparities and policy fragmentation have widened the divide between developed and developing economies, intensifying tensions between climate ambition and developmental needs. The persistent global climate finance gap, particularly in emerging markets, underscores the need for enhanced international cooperation and innovative funding models that mobilize private capital. 
Methodologically, quantifying and pricing climate risks remains challenging. Existing models rely on incomplete datasets and inconsistent assumptions. The integration of AI, IoT, and natural language processing offers promising pathways to enhance precision, yet empirical findings on climate risk pricing are mixed. 
Financial institutions face the dual task of managing transition and physical risks. Scenario analysis and climate stress testing have become essential for banks, insurers, and regulators, yet their effectiveness depends on transparent assumptions and comparable metrics. Meanwhile, policy uncertainty— like shifts of U.S. climate priorities—continues to generate volatility and regulatory ambiguity.
Similarly, despite growing global consensus on the need for carbon pricing, there remains no unified evaluation framework, and national carbon markets are developing at different speeds. Broadly, carbon pricing mechanisms fall into two categories: carbon taxes (administratively set prices) and emissions trading systems (ETS), which rely on market forces. Both depend on accurate carbon accounting—the precise measurement of direct (Scope 1), indirect (Scope 2), and value-chain (Scope 3) emissions.
Under a carbon tax, authorities calculate a firm’s payable tax based on verified emissions multiplied by the official tax rate. Accuracy in carbon accounting is thus essential to maintain fairness and prevent evasion. By contrast, in a carbon market, regulators first establish a cap on total emissions consistent with reduction targets. This cap is divided into emission allowances allocated to firms. Companies emitting above their quota must purchase additional allowances, while those below can sell their surplus—creating a market-driven carbon price determined by supply and demand.
However, the design and maturity of these systems vary significantly across countries, leading to carbon leakage—where firms relocate high-emission activities to jurisdictions with weaker climate policies—and to competitive imbalances. The European Union Emissions Trading System (EU ETS), with over two decades of operation, has become the world’s most mature carbon market. It has gradually shifted from free allocation toward paid auctions, and covers roughly 40% of EU greenhouse gas emissions. The EU ETS remains the central instrument for achieving the bloc’s goal of cutting emissions by 55% by 2030.
In contrast, the United States has no nationwide carbon pricing scheme. Instead, regional programs such as California’s Cap-and-Trade system and Quebec’s linked market cover around 85% of their respective emissions. Emerging economies, however, face greater challenges—limited infrastructure, technological capacity, and economic flexibility often constrain implementation. The EU’s Carbon Border Adjustment Mechanism (CBAM) further intensifies the pressure, as it imposes carbon costs on imports from regions without comparable pricing, raising concerns over equity and cross-border trade friction.
China launched its national carbon market in 2017, initially covering the power sector and planning expansion to heavy industries like steel and cement by 2025. Its system adopts a benchmarking approach, setting quotas based on industry-specific emission intensity and actual output. This method helps reduce compliance costs but weakens the market’s carbon price signal. In 2024, the average price of China Carbon Emission Allowances (CEA) stood at 91.8 RMB per ton, with total trading volume reaching 18 billion RMB. Meanwhile, EU Allowances (EUA) averaged €65.3 per ton, driving the EU market’s total value to €781 billion, accounting for roughly 85% of global carbon trading volume.
Figure 6.1: The total transaction value of major global carbon markets between 2020 and 2024

Source: LSEG, https://www.carbonmark.com/post/the-carbon-market-in-2024-a-year-in-review-and-emerging-trends
AI risks and governance 
Chapter 5 highlighted how artificial intelligence (AI) is transforming sustainability practices—from optimizing energy use to enhancing ESG analytics and regulatory oversight. Yet, the rapid diffusion of AI introduces new risks and paradoxes.
On one hand, AI enables real-time environmental monitoring, predictive analytics, and automated reporting, substantially improving corporate efficiency and transparency. It also contributes to social progress by expanding access to credit, education, and inclusive employment. On the other hand, these technologies raise ethical, operational, and environmental concerns. Data privacy, algorithmic bias, and explainability remain unresolved issues. When deployed in lending, recruitment, or investment decisions, biased data can inadvertently reinforce inequality, eroding trust and accountability.
Moreover, AI itself is energy intensive. Data centers supporting large-scale model training consume roughly 1.5% of global electricity and are expected to triple their demand by 2035. This growing carbon footprint challenges the notion of AI as a purely sustainable enabler. Promoting “green AI” through energy-efficient algorithms, renewable-powered data centers, and low-carbon computing infrastructure is thus imperative.
The employment dimension adds further complexity. Contrary to fears of mass displacement, evidence from PwC (2025) indicates that AI is driving productivity and wage growth across most occupations, though it also accelerates skill transformation. Workers now face faster-changing competency demands, emphasizing the need for continuous reskilling and lifelong learning.
Finally, the governance landscape for AI remains fragmented. National approaches diverge between innovation-driven flexibility and precautionary regulation. Without coherent international coordination, firms face rising compliance burdens and uncertainty. Meanwhile, geopolitical competition over AI capabilities risks diverting attention from sustainability cooperation.

Emerging opportunities
Standardization and global convergence
As discussed in Chapter 2, sustainability reporting has evolved through a variety of frameworks and standards, each differing in scope, depth, and materiality orientation. While this diversity once reflected regional priorities and stakeholder preferences, it also produced inconsistencies and comparability challenges. Now, as ESG disclosure transitions from voluntary good practice to legally binding obligation, a pivotal opportunity has emerged—to advance global standardization and convergence.
Figure 6.2: ESG standards, frameworks and everything in between

Source: GRI (2022), https://www.globalreporting.org/media/jxkgrggd/gri-perspective-esg-standards-frameworks.pdf
In recent years, major international initiatives such as the TCFD, CDP, CDSB, ISSB, and TNFD have begun to move toward alignment through cooperation and consolidation. Together, they are shaping the foundation of a unified global system for sustainability disclosure. Such harmonization enhances data comparability and reliability, lowers compliance costs for multinational corporations, and strengthens investor trust and regulatory oversight.
A particularly promising shift lies in the integration of climate risk into mainstream financial reporting. Frameworks like the TCFD and ISSB now position climate disclosure as a core reporting dimension, and jurisdictions including the European Union, Japan, the United Kingdom, New Zealand, and Hong Kong have adopted or are transitioning to mandatory disclosure. These developments mark a growing consensus on the financial materiality of climate risks, opening pathways toward greater market transparency and cross-border comparability.
At the same time, technological innovation is helping bridge methodological divides among ESG rating agencies. The application of AI, natural language processing, and blockchain in ESG data collection, verification, and reporting can automate validation processes, detect inconsistencies, and track sustainability metrics in real time—enhancing both the transparency and credibility of evaluations.
Viewed in this light, the move toward global ESG convergence represents more than regulatory progress; it signals a structural transformation in how capital markets function. By aligning standards and harnessing technology, investors and corporations can allocate resources more effectively toward truly sustainable outcomes, paving the way for a more transparent and accountable global economy.
Sustainable market opportunities
The accelerating global transition toward sustainability is reshaping competitive dynamics and opening new market frontiers across industries. As consumers, governments, and investors increasingly value environmental and social responsibility, companies that embed sustainability into their strategies are gaining both financial and reputational advantages. These opportunities are particularly evident in two domains: the rise of sustainability-oriented consumption and the expansion of green public procurement.
Rising consumer demand
Consumers around the world are shifting their preferences toward products that are environmentally friendly, health-conscious, and socially responsible. Firms that recognize and adapt to these expectations can unlock new revenue streams, attract impact-driven investors, and strengthen long-term competitiveness.
According to Euromonitor International, the number of online stock-keeping units (SKUs) carrying sustainability claims across 11 FMCG industries in 25 countries rose from 4 million in mid-2022 to 5 million by mid-2024. Retail sales of these products have climbed steadily since 2020, with brands offering clear sustainability propositions achieving, on average, 1.5% higher growth than their conventional peers. What once served as a niche marketing appeal has now become a mainstream determinant of consumer choice.
Figure 6.3: Global retail sales of sustainable products by industry

Source: Euromonitor International, https://go.euromonitor.com/rs/805-KOK-719/images/Euromonitor-2025ConsumerTrends.pdf?mkt_tok=ODA1LUtPSy03MTkAAAGdZhohAfudKVnZHLq_e49AvDRpT18VxFeBRVF6ETXh3bodrG9lZa6GAjb_c0w8I6fMCGol-xxC_9rluoKk9TXaPM47HTIhq7EcByG2Si35b-UPde6oSg
Beauty and personal care lead the market in global sustainable product sales, generating more than USD 120 billion in 2023, while pet care recorded the strongest compound annual growth rate between 2020 and 2023. Across categories, attributes such as plastic-free packaging, “MADE SAFE” certification, and upcycled ingredients are rapidly gaining traction. These shifts reveal that sustainability claims increasingly reinforce perceptions of product quality, health, and durability. By integrating sustainability into product design, packaging, and verification, firms can meet evolving consumer expectations while securing distinct brand positioning in a crowded marketplace.
Figure 6.4: Fastest-growing sustainability claims (% CAGR of global retail sales from 2020-2023 per selected industry)

Source: Euromonitor International
Expanding green public procurement
The public sector is also becoming a powerful engine for sustainable market growth. Through green procurement and low-carbon transition policies, governments are shaping demand for environmentally responsible products and services. Companies offering innovative green technologies or sustainability solutions are increasingly prioritized in public tenders, gaining competitive advantages and greater visibility across markets.
Data from Dataintelo(2024)  show that the global green procurement platform market reached USD 3.2 billion in 2024 and is projected to expand at a 14.6% CAGR, hitting USD 10.2 billion by 2033. This growth reflects both the digitalization of procurement systems and mounting regulatory pressure for supply-chain transparency. The rise of digital platforms also promotes collaboration across industries, accelerating the diffusion of green innovations.
Nevertheless, small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) often face barriers to participation—such as large contract thresholds, complex eligibility requirements, and limited transparency. To address this, the European Commission introduced the Voluntary Sustainability Reporting Standards for SMEs(VSME)  in December 2024. Aligned with the EU’s Corporate Sustainability Reporting Directive (CSRD), the VSME provides two reporting levels—basic and comprehensive—simplifying disclosures while ensuring consistency with broader sustainability frameworks.
Incorporating these standards into public tenders could significantly broaden SME participation and foster a more inclusive green economy. By coupling simplified reporting with transparent evaluation criteria, the initiative supports innovation and fairness, ensuring that smaller firms can also contribute to national and regional transition strategies.
In short, sustainable market opportunities are expanding as private consumption and public procurement increasingly reflect environmental and social values. Companies that proactively integrate sustainability into their innovation, production, and disclosure practices will be best positioned to capture these emerging opportunities, build stakeholder trust, and play a vital role in steering the global economy toward a more low-carbon and inclusive future.
Sustainable investing trend
As discussed earlier, individual and institutional investors often approach sustainable investing with different motivations—some prioritizing social impact, others focusing on long-term financial performance. Yet across both groups, enthusiasm for sustainability continues to accelerate, reshaping global asset allocation and portfolio strategies.
A 2024 Morgan Stanley survey found that 78% of asset managers and 80% of asset owners expect the share of sustainable assets under management to rise within two years, driven by new mandates and a growing track record of competitive returns. More than three-quarters of institutional investors report that sustainability offerings directly influence their mandate decisions, while 90% cite rising client demand as the main catalyst.
Figure 6.5: Why are institutional investors practicing sustainable investing?

Source: Morgan Stanley (2024), https://www.morganstanley.com/content/dam/msdotcom/en/assets/pdfs/CRC_3923864-2024_Sustainable_Signals_Institutional_Investors-PDF_Report-FINAL.pdf
Globally, investment themes are evolving in response to social priorities and regional contexts. Healthcare (41%) and financial inclusion (40%) rank among the top global focuses. Yet clear regional distinctions remain: European investors emphasize nature and biodiversity, consistent with the EU’s green agenda; those in Asia-Pacific lean toward climate action and healthcare, mirroring local development challenges; and North American investors increasingly explore climate adaptation—a once-overlooked but now essential theme for long-term resilience.
This growing diversity of investment priorities reflects a shift from compliance-driven approaches toward opportunity-oriented strategies. Investors no longer view sustainability as an ethical add-on but as an integral dimension of financial performance—where environmental and social goals coexist with risk-adjusted returns.
Figure 6.6: Is your organization currently invested in, or does it offer, products addressing the following themes?

Source: Morgan Stanley (2024)
Evolving financial market
The rise of sustainability and climate-related risks is transforming how financial systems allocate capital and manage uncertainty. In response, a growing suite of financing instruments and system designs has emerged to support low-carbon transitions, climate resilience, and inclusive growth.
Innovative financial systems
To align capital markets with sustainability goals, a diverse range of financial products has been developed to reduce financing costs, provide risk protection, and channel funds toward green and social projects. Among them, green bonds, green loans, and sustainability-linked loans stand out for embedding measurable sustainability targets directly into pricing mechanisms—strengthening the link between corporate performance and financing conditions. These instruments not only provide lower-cost funding but also incentivize firms to invest in carbon reduction and clean technologies, acting as powerful drivers of corporate transformation.
In addition, catastrophe bonds and resilience insurance enable the transfer and diversification of climate-related risks among market participants, reflecting a deeper integration of adaptation strategies into financial design. Similarly, the growing complexity of climate risk has spurred the development of weather derivatives that help firms hedge against extreme weather events. By tying financial returns to environmental variables, these instruments extend sustainable finance beyond capital allocation to encompass risk transfer and market adaptation.
At the same time, carbon pricing have been rapidly advancing worldwide. According to the World Bank Group’s State and Trends of Carbon Pricing 2025 report, as of April 2025, there are 80 carbon pricing instruments in operation globally, including 37 emissions trading systems (ETS) and 43 carbon taxes, five more than last year. Emerging economies such as China, Brazil, and India are playing a crucial role in this progress. China’s national carbon market continues to expand, Brazil has formally approved legislation for a greenhouse gas emissions trading system, and India has launched its Carbon Credit Trading Scheme (CCTS). These developments demonstrate that emerging economies are actively engaging in carbon pricing practices. 
The introduction of the EU Carbon Border Adjustment Mechanism (CBAM) is further accelerating the interconnection of national carbon markets and the construction of integrated systems combining carbon taxes and emissions trading, highlighting the growing importance of cross-border coordination. For many firms, carbon pricing has transformed from a compliance obligation into a strategic opportunity—driving emission reduction, enhancing competitiveness, corporate reputation, and innovation capacity.

Integration of climate risk
Financial economists and practitioners are increasingly developing hybrid models that merge traditional financial indicators with climate-related data to form a more comprehensive view of risk exposure and systemic vulnerability. Advanced tools such as natural language processing (NLP), Internet of Things (IoT) sensors, and machine learning algorithms now enable real-time tracking and interpretation of climate signals—transforming how institutions perceive and manage emerging environmental risks.
In the banking sector, credit risk models are being recalibrated to incorporate borrowers’ exposure to climate risks, directly influencing loan pricing. Likewise, the insurance industry has begun embedding climate data into premium design, reinsurance structures, and loss projections, helping to reduce unexpected operational losses and achieve a more balanced risk–return profile.
To systematically evaluate potential impacts, scenario analysis and stress testing have become central tools in climate risk management. The Task Force on Climate-related Financial Disclosures (TCFD) offers a structured framework for assessing outcomes across different climate pathways—from orderly transitions to severe physical disruptions. Through these exercises, financial institutions can identify both vulnerabilities and opportunities under varying temperature or policy trajectories.
