Equally important, documenting the full analytical process—including assumptions, data sources, methodologies, and management responses—enhances transparency, comparability, and regulatory credibility. Ultimately, the integration of climate risk management not only strengthens institutional resilience but also supports more forward-looking, sustainable capital planning across the financial system.
Figure 6.7: Process for applying scenario analysis to climate-related risks and opportunities

Source: TCFD, https://www.tcfdhub.org/scenario-analysis/
Blue finance and blended framework
As an emerging branch of climate finance, blue finance provides crucial capital support for the sustainable development of ocean systems and is gaining growing attention from global investors and financial institutions. The two main instruments—blue bonds and blue loans—channel funding into projects such as water and wastewater management, marine pollution reduction, ecosystem restoration, sustainable shipping, eco-tourism, and offshore renewable energy.
However, financing the blue economy often faces structural barriers. Banks and investors are hesitant to engage in marine projects due to their high risks and uncertain profit models. Without initial catalytic capital to pilot and de-risk these projects, commercial funds remain reluctant, leaving a significant financing gap, especially in developing economies. 
To address this imbalance, blended finance has emerged as an effective solution that combines public, philanthropic, and private capital. By balancing financial incentives with long-term social and environmental outcomes, it not only closes funding gaps but also catalyzes innovation at the frontier of sustainable development. The creative “debt-for-nature swap” structures adopted by ocean nations such as Seychelles, Belize, and Barbados—where sovereign debt is exchanged for conservation commitments—illustrate how blended mechanisms can unlock new models of blue financing.
Beyond conventional finance, blended models hold transformative potential in early-stage or policy-uncertain markets—where commercial viability is still nascent, infrastructure remains underdeveloped, and regulations are evolving. In China, public–private partnerships (PPP) in areas such as climate adaptation, biodiversity protection, and marine conservation can be seen as a form of blended finance, where government guidance funds help attract private participation and scale up market solutions.
Figure 6.8: Blend finance framework

Source: convergence, https://www.convergence.finance/blended-finance
A notable example is the “Blue Circle” project, jointly launched in 2019 by the Zhejiang provincial government and Zhejiang Blueview Technology. The initiative established a collaborative mechanism of “government guidance, corporate leadership, industrial synergy, and public participation,” transforming local marine waste management into a market-driven system. In this model, the government shifted from a regulator to a service provider—creating policies, establishing mechanisms, and supervising waste disposal—while motivating marine waste collection through economic incentives.
The Blue Circle project links environmental governance with social inclusion. Local fishermen are contracted to collect marine plastic waste, which is purchased at above-market prices. For many, this provides an additional monthly income of over 1,000 yuan, while simultaneously reducing marine pollution. As participation grows, public awareness of ocean protection has risen, generating both ecological and social dividends. 
Collected marine plastics are sorted, compressed, and transported to recycling plants, where they are processed into GRS-certified recycled materials used in automobile parts, textiles, cosmetics packaging, and stationery. This “marine cleanup–income growth–industrial upgrading” cycle turns waste into value, with downstream products supporting upstream livelihoods. 
Figure 6.9: The “Blue Circle” initiative mobilizes local fishermen to collect and sort marine plastic waste

Source: UNEP, https://www.unep.org/championsofearth/zh-hans/laureates/2023/lansexunhuan
Moreover, digital technologies have enhanced transparency throughout the recycling chain. Blockchain enables full traceability from ocean collection to product manufacturing, ensuring data integrity and eliminating “greenwashing.” By scanning a QR code on packaging, consumers can view details such as the waste’s origin, collection date, and recycling enterprise—making the plastic’s “ocean identity” fully visible.
Figure 6.10: Blue Circle uses blockchain technology to certify the goods it makes from plastic collected from the ocean

 Source: UNEP, https://www.unep.org/championsofearth/laureates/2023/blue-circle 
To ensure long-term sustainability, a coalition of environmental organizations, certification authorities, and industry partners established the Blue Alliance and its Blue Common Wealth Fund, allocating around 20% of profits from recycled plastic utilization to support waste collectors and local communities. By creating a value redistribution system that empowers vulnerable groups, promotes green industries, and turns marine waste governance into shared prosperity, the Blue Circle model has achieved remarkable success.
The project’s innovative approach earned it the United Nations Champions of the Earth Award in 2023, selected from over 2,500 global initiatives. Today, it is expanding beyond Zhejiang to coastal provinces such as Shanghai, Jiangsu, Shandong, Fujian, and Hainan—offering valuable lessons for global marine conservation. 
In essence, the success of China’s Blue Circle project illustrates how well-designed financial mechanisms that combine public and private efforts can transform ecological challenges into engines of inclusive growth. The continued extension of blend finance offers a powerful blueprint for channeling capital toward environmental resilience and shared prosperity, marking a pivotal step in the evolution of sustainable finance.
Table 6.1 Stages of Blue Circle

Green and just AI
Artificial intelligence (AI) has become one of the most transformative forces of our time—reshaping industries, redefining productivity, and offering new pathways toward sustainable development. Yet, alongside its promise, AI raises pressing concerns about environmental impact and labor disruption. This section explores how to steer AI toward greener and fairer outcomes, turning potential risks into opportunities for sustainable growth. 
The environmental footprint of AI
Despite its role in advancing sustainability, AI itself demands vast amounts of energy. Most AI training and deployment occur in data centers, which accounted for roughly 1.5% of global electricity consumption in 2024 (IEA, 2024). The rapid expansion of AI-related services has driven large-scale investment in hyperscale data centers—each consuming electricity equivalent to that of 100,000 households.
Figure 6.11: Data center electricity consumption in household electricity consumption equivalents

Source: IEA, https://www.iea.org/data-and-statistics/charts/data-centre-electricity-consumption-in-household-electricity-consumption-equivalents-2024
IEA projections show electricity demand from data centers rising from 460 TWh in 2024 to over 1,000 TWh by 2030, with emissions peaking around 320 million tons of CO₂ before gradually declining as renewable energy adoption accelerates. Encouragingly, renewables are projected to supply nearly half of the additional electricity needed by 2030, growing at an annual rate of 22%.
Figure 6.12: Data center electricity consumption in household electricity consumption equivalents

Source: IEA
Figure 6.13: Global data center CO2 emissions, base case, 2020-2035

Source: IEA
While data centers will still contribute less than 3% of global electricity use and 1% of total emissions by 2030, the scale of AI’s growth demands careful governance. Building a Green AI ecosystem requires coordinated efforts:
Energy-efficient algorithms and model compression can curb training costs.
Deployment prioritization in high-value sectors—such as education, healthcare, and climate science—maximizes social return.
Integration with smart grids and renewables optimizes energy flows.
And most importantly, policy frameworks setting emissions standards for AI infrastructure can ensure digital progress aligns with environmental goals.
In essence, making AI green is not a secondary concern but a cornerstone of the broader ESG transformation.
AI and the future of work
Beyond its environmental implications, AI is reshaping global labor markets in complex ways. Contrary to fears of widespread job loss, recent evidence suggests that AI often complements rather than replaces human labor. According to PwC’s 2025 global report, both employment and wages have grown across AI-exposed occupations. Between 2019 and 2024, jobs less affected by AI rose by 65%, while AI-exposed roles still expanded by 38%.
A useful distinction emerges between automatable jobs—where AI performs routine tasks—and augmentable jobs, where AI enhances human capability. The latter category is growing faster, signaling that AI’s true value lies in amplification, not substitution. In industries most exposed to AI, wages have risen twice as fast as in others, and demand for AI-related skills continues to surge, with postings requiring such expertise increasing by 7.5% even amid an overall job slowdown.


Figure 6.14: Change in demand by AI exposure for all jobs, 2019-2024, globally

Source: PwC, https://www.pwc.com/gx/en/issues/artificial-intelligence/job-barometer/2025/report.pdf
Figure 6.15: Job numbers are growing for both automatable and augmentable jobs in all industries
 
Source: PwC
Figure 6.16: Wages are rising 2x faster in industries most vs least exposed to AI

Source: PwC
Figure 6.17: Total number and share of job postings requiring AI related skills, Hong Kong, SAR, 2021-2024

Source: PwC
In Hong Kong SAR, for instance, job postings demanding AI skills grew from 1.6% in 2023 to 1.9% in 2024, led by the information and communication sector. Similar “augmented” roles are expanding in public administration, energy, and utilities—indicating a structural shift toward human–machine collaboration.
Figure 6.18: Share of AI job postings by sector, Hong Kong, SAR, 2021-2024

Source: PwC
Figure 6.19: Growth rate in postings by sector for augmented and automated jobs, Hong Kong, SAR, 2021-2024

Source: PwC
However, this transformation also accelerates skill transformation. Employers now change required competencies 66% faster in AI-related occupations than before, emphasizing digital literacy, critical thinking, and adaptability over traditional credentials. The gender dimension adds further complexity: a larger share of women than men are in AI-exposed jobs, which could either amplify inequality or empower new inclusion—depending on access to reskilling and digital education.
AI’s diffusion also introduces important gender implications. Across most countries, a higher proportion of women are employed in AI-exposed occupations than men. This duality presents both new opportunities and new risks. If women can successfully navigate the rapid shift in skill requirements, they stand to gain disproportionately from the AI revolution; if not, they risk being further marginalized in an evolving digital economy. This underscores the urgency of ensuring inclusive access to reskilling programs and digital education.
Ultimately, AI is redrawing the boundary between automation and creativity. Rather than erasing human work, it elevates qualities machines cannot replicate—judgment, empathy, ethics, and aesthetic sense. Many firms are beginning to use AI not to cut jobs, but to enhance human potential, creating what some call an “exponential workforce multiplier.”
To realize this vision, governments, enterprises, and individuals must shift from a logic of job protection to capability empowerment. Investing in reskilling, lifelong learning, and inclusive education will determine whether AI becomes a catalyst for inequality—or a cornerstone of sustainable and just growth.
Stakeholder's Views on Sustainability Issues
Sustainability is a shared but differently interpreted agenda. Corporates, investors, and technology providers approach it through distinct logics of value, responsibility, and innovation. Understanding their evolving perspectives helps reveal how sustainability is embedded within the broader economic and technological transformation.
Corporates: value vs. risk
As ESG disclosure shifts from voluntary to mandatory, corporate sustainability has evolved from a compliance task to a strategic lever. According to Morgan Stanley’s 2025 global survey of over 300 companies, 88% now view sustainability as a long-term driver of value creation—three percentage points higher than in 2024. North American and European firms reported the largest increase in this perception, while companies in the Asia-Pacific region placed stronger emphasis on sustainability as a tool for managing risk.
Industry patterns further illustrate this divergence. Firms in utilities, consumer staples, real estate, and finance tend to link sustainability to value creation, whereas those in technology, industrials, and energy sectors see it as both an opportunity and a safeguard. Yet challenges remain. Many companies cite investment costs, policy uncertainty and the difficulty of quantifying sustainability performance as persistent barriers. Over half reported direct physical impacts from climate events during the past year—most commonly extreme heat—causing higher costs, workforce disruptions, and revenue losses. Encouragingly, 80% plan to strengthen resilience measures, underscoring that sustainability has become an operational as well as strategic priority.
Figure 6.20: How does sustainability and/or ESG impact your long-term corporate strategy?

Source: Morgan Stanley(2025), https://www.morganstanley.com/insights/articles/corporate-sustainability-signals-report-2025

Investors: return vs. impact
For investors, sustainability represents both a financial opportunity and an ethical commitment. According to Morgan Stanley (April 2025), nearly 90% of individual investors worldwide express interest in strategies that aim for market-rate returns while generating positive social or environmental outcomes. Confidence in the performance of sustainable assets remains the strongest motivation—especially among younger generations, with 99% of Gen Z and 97% of Millennials showing strong enthusiasm for sustainable investments.
Figure 6.21: How interested are you in sustainable investing?

 Source: Morgan Stanley(2025), https://www.morganstanley.com/insights/articles/sustainable-investing-interest-2025
However, enthusiasm is not immune to economic headwinds. A 2024 Stanford Business School survey found that support for ESG investing among young American retail investors dropped sharply amid rising inflation and economic uncertainty. Millennials and Gen Z participants were far less inclined to back fund managers advocating stronger ESG practices if such advocacy meant lower financial returns. Only 10% said they would be willing to forgo more than 10% of their retirement savings for environmental goals such as cutting carbon emissions or expanding renewable energy—down from 33% in 2022. Similar declines were observed for social causes like closing the gender wage gap or expanding employee benefits.
Figure 6.22: percentage of investors who are unwilling to lose any retirement savings for companies they’re invested in to set net-zero emissions targets

Source: 2024 Survey of Investors, Retirement Savings, and ESG, https://www.gsb.stanford.edu/insights/young-investors-support-esg-dropped-dramatically-2024
Researchers attribute this shift to a changing macroeconomic environment. When the initial survey was conducted in 2022, interest rates were low, the labor market was strong, and stimulus funds were still boosting household confidence. By contrast, subsequent years brought persistent inflation, higher rates, and a cooling job market—conditions that made investors more cautious and less willing to trade returns for impact. As sentiment converged across age groups, ESG investing came to be viewed less as a moral imperative and more as a cyclical luxury—pursued in prosperous times but deprioritized in lean ones.
Taken together, these surveys highlight the delicate balance investors strike between returns and impact. Most individuals are willing to engage in sustainable investing, but not at the cost of financial security. The durability of ESG investing, therefore, may hinge on both macroeconomic stability and the sector’s ability to demonstrate tangible, long-term value creation.
As climate risks grow increasingly visible, environmental considerations will likely remain central to both retail and institutional investment decisions. Nonetheless, recent retreats from ESG commitments by major asset managers such as BlackRock and Vanguard underscore a more complex, policy-driven reality. The path forward for sustainable finance is likely to be shaped by shifting economies, evolving regulations, and the ongoing negotiation between impact and return.
AI providers: technology vs. responsibility
Artificial intelligence has become both a catalyst and a proving ground for sustainable innovation. While regulatory frameworks differ across countries, leading AI developers are increasingly aware of their dual mission: to push technological boundaries while ensuring that progress contributes to social and environmental well-being.
Lenovo Morningstar, a subsidiary specializing in AR and computer vision, exemplifies this balance. By positioning ESG as a driver of long-term value rather than a cost, Lenovo embraces the principle of “AI for Good.” As Xiangbo Lv, head of Lenovo’s Wuhan R&D Center, explains, “We aim to use our AI capabilities to meet real-world needs and take on the responsibility of protecting our planet and maintaining biodiversity,” emphasizing the company’s dedication to leveraging AI and technology for environmental protection.
Since 2021, Lenovo has actively supported the conservation of the Yangtze finless porpoise—an endangered species affectionately known as the “Smiling Angel,” whose health reflects that of the entire river ecosystem. Partnering with the Tian’ezhou Reserve, Lenovo donated China’s first AI-powered ecological protection system for the species. The system applies visual recognition to identify and track individual porpoises, analyze their behaviors, and generate data-driven insights for habitat management—solving persistent data challenges that once hindered effective protection.
Building on this initiative, Lenovo adopted a young female porpoise named “No. 1575,” whose rewilding journey symbolizes both scientific innovation and environmental commitment. Training at the Hubei Baiji National Nature Reserve, “No. 1575” learned to navigate natural waters, find food, and survive independently. Lenovo supported this process not only through funding but also by supplying AI-enabled tools—such as intelligent PCs, including an original AI assistant with advanced information processing capabilities—to assist researchers in monitoring and rehabilitation. As the first company to adopt a porpoise with a dedicated rewilding plan, Lenovo set a new precedent in both ex-situ conservation and wild population recovery.   
Figure 6.23: A Yangtze finless porpoise

Source: Lenovo, https://news.lenovo.com/technology-community-unite-yangtze-finless-porpoise/
Figure 6.24: No. 1575 undergoing a health check

Source: Lenovo, https://news.lenovo.com/technology-community-unite-yangtze-finless-porpoise/

In collaboration with the National Geographic Channel, Lenovo later launched the world’s first Yangtze finless porpoise tracking map and viewing guide, extending its conservation efforts from science to public education. The story of Lenovo is more than the protection of the Yangtze finless porpoise — It reflects a shift in how technology firms can weave innovation into stewardship, turning compassion into capability.
As artificial intelligence moves beyond the race for algorithms and computing power to become a guardian of nature, life, and culture, technological innovation and ESG leadership no longer stand apart. They merge into an integrated and mutually reinforcing force. Only when AI internalizes the spirit of ESG can technology evolve from mere efficiency to enduring impact—creating value that is both sustainable and humane.
In sum, the evolution of ESG in finance is dynamic, shaped by policy shifts, market adaptation, and technological progress. To navigate this complexity, stakeholders must integrate sustainability into core decision-making while fostering collaboration across sectors. AI, when developed responsibly, can serve as a powerful ally—enhancing our ability to assess risks, design solutions, and pursue inclusive growth. The path forward demands not only innovation but also shared commitment, ensuring that sustainability becomes both an ethical compass and a source of lasting competitiveness.
